T he Red Page
Meredith’s Letters
Despite the reverential worship of the
critic-crowd, the truth is that there is much
that is tedious in any collection of aay man’s
letters. At the best, such a collection is
but a selection of letters; there is thus but
a partial glimpse of the man. And in the
letters selected there is much that requires
explanation, much judiciously omitted. One
has to wade through two volumes of Letters
of George Meredith, collected and edited by
his son, W. M. Meredith (Constable, per
Dymock’s Library, Sydney), to discover
amid their redundancies and surplusage
what the editor has left of Meredith.
But out of the commonplace of correspond-
ence one occasionally has a glimpse of the
man behind the discursive pen, the man at
his ease with his friends, speaking out, care-
lessly and uncouthly, his inner convictions.
But this wheat has to be diligently sought
for among the two volumes of chaff.
Meredith, it has been hinted, had a grave
disagreement with the son of his first mar-
riage, Arthur. The letters that might have
explained the causes of that rupture are not
given. All we have are his letters to his
son while the latter was a school-boy, then
a long silence, and a letter offering recon-
ciliation. So, too, the tragedy ©f his first
marriage is merely hinted at. Not one of
his letters to his first wife is included,
though it is possible, of course, that such
letters had been destroyed. If a volume
such as this is necessary it should he as
complete, as frank, as is compatible with the
fear of libel. The editor anticipates hhis
blame in his preface,' by stating that they
have been printed first and foremost for his
friends, which fact must explain whatso-
ever appears illogical, superfluous and ob-
scure. But the book is not privately printed.
It must be judged from a public standpoint.
So much having been said, it may be
admitted that the letters were worth the
printing, and that from them one may
glimpse, however imperfectly, certain facets
of a remarkable mind not implicit in his
novels or poems. One of these aspects of
Meredith the letter-writer is his love of fun,
his “chaff” for his friends, his riming jests,
his delight in nicknames. Of his love for
Nature, his understanding of Earth, his de-
light in walking, these letters are but con-
firmatory of his works. But his judgment
off contemporaries, delightfully frank, his in-
terest in the world, his love for his friends,
his bitter contempt for his critics —all these
give a new interest to Meredith the man.
And especially is this book valuable in
affording materials for his biography, in
showing us the inner side of his long struggle
to win recognition, and, in his later period,
in indicating the hope he cherished for
humanity and the genial philosophy of his
resignation. It is the picture of a lovable,
straight, fearless man. And the student will
find here Meredith’s private opinion of his
works, and the elucidation of many a point
of obscurity.
From Meredith's Letters
Of His Second Wife.
It is time that your friend should show
you a clean breast. He loves a woman as
he never yet loved, and she, for the first
time, has let her heart escape her... .1 can-
not play at life. I loved her when we were
in Norwich... .1 believe that Ido her good.
I know that she feels it. Me she fills with
such deep and reverent emotion that I can
hardly think it the action of a human crea-
ture merely. I seem to trace a fable thusfar
developed by blessed angels in the skies.
She has been reserved for me, ray friend. It
was seen that I could love a woman, and one
has been given to me to love. Her love for
me is certain. I hold her strongly in my
hand....
My friend, I have written of love, and
never felt it till now. I have much to pass
through in raking up my history with the’
first woman that held me. But I would pass
through fire for my darling, and all that I
have to endure seems little for the immense
gain I hope to get. When her hand rests in
mine, the world seems to hold its breath, and
the sun is moveless. I take hold of Eter-
nity. She is intensely emotional, but with-
out expression for it, save in music. I call
her my dumb poet.
Of Tennyson.
Byron—he’s abused, so I take to him;
and I’m a little sick of Tennysonian green
tea. I don’t think Byron wholesome —ex-
actly, but a drop or two so—eh? And he
doesn’t give limp, lackadaisical fishermen,
and pander to the depraved sentimentalism
of our drawing-rooms. I tell you that “Enoch
Arden” is ill done, and that in 20 years’
time it will be denounced as villainous
weak, in spite of the fine (but too conscious)
verse, and the rich insertions of tropical
scenery....
The “Holy Grail” is wonderful, isn’t it?
The lines are satin lengths, the figures
Sevres china. I have not the courage to
offer to review it, I should say such things.
T© think! —it’s in these days that the fore-
most poet of the country goes on fluting of
creatures that have not a breath of vital
humanity in them, and doles us out his regu-
lar five-feet with the old trick of the vowel
endings—the Euphuist’s tongue, the Exqui-
site’s leg, the Curate’s moral sentiments, the
British matron and her daughter’s purity
of tone: so he talks, so he walks, so he
snuffles, s« he anpears divine Why, this
stuff is not the Muse, it’s Musery. The man
has got hold of the Muses’ clothes-line and
hang it with jewellery.
But the “Lucretius” is grand. I can’t
say how much I admire it and hate the Sir
landarus public which has corrupted this
fine (natural) singer. In his degraded state
I really believe he is useful, for he reflects
as much as our society chooses to show of
itself. .. .Do you care to find the Holy Grail,
Fred? Twenty years ago it would have
excited me. This your foremost poet is 20
years bfchind his time Isn’t there a scent
of damned hypocrisy in all this lisping and
vowelled purity of the Idylls? Well, just as
you like. It’s fashionable; it pleases the
rose-pink ladies, it sells. Enough.
Of His Poems.
As to his poems. I don’t think the age
prosaic for not buying them. A man who
hopes to be popular must think from the
mass, and as the heart of the mass. If he
follows out vagaries of his own brain, he
cannot hope for general esteem. “Modern
Love,” as_ a dissection of the sentimental
passion of these days, could only be appre-
hended by the few who would read it many
times. _ I have not looked for it to succeed.
Why did I write it? Who can account for
pressure?....
As for my poems, I have lost the ardor for
publishing them.... The conditions of sale,
which frown on collections of verse not
offering themselves as appropriate gifts for
the innumerable curate and his bride, are, I
fear, adverse. Poetry in England is required
to have a function of a practical kind, and
t© exercise it. . ..
As to publishing books of verse, I have
paid heavily for that audacity twice in
pounds sterling. I had for audience the bull,
the donkey and the barking cur. ...
Truly, the pass-ion to produce verse in our
region is accursed. I ask myself why I
should labor, and, for the third time, pay to
publish the result, with a certainty of being
yelled at, and haply spat upon, for my pains.
And still I do it. At heart, it is plain, I
must still have a remainder of esteem for
our public; or I have now the habit of com-
position, which precipitates to publishing.
I scorned myself for my folly. Where he can
get no audience a spouting Homer would
merit the cap and be 115....
I read in a critical review of some versed
of mine that I was “a harlequin and a per-
former of antics.” lam accustomed to that
kind of writing, as our hustings orator is to
the dead cat and the brickbat flung in his
face—at which he smiles politely; and I
too; but after many years of it my mind
looks elsewhere.
Of Prose.
Few Englishmen can write a resonant
prose dialogue that is not blatant; and when
avoiding those alarms, they drop to flabbi-
ness. It is merely to say that Style is
rarely achieved here. The prose in Shake-
speare and in Congreve is perfect. They
have always the right accent on their termi-
nations. Apart from drama, Swift is a
great exemplar ; and in his mild,
tea-table way, Addison, follow. Johnson
and Macauley wielded bludgeons; they had
not the strength that can be supple. Gibbon
could take a long stride with the leg of a
dancing master; he could not take a short
one. Matthew Arnold was born from the
pulpit and occupied it, and might have ser-
monised for all time, but that he conceived
the head of the clerk below to be the sconce
of the British public, and that he must drum
on it with an iterated phrase perpetually to
awaken understanding.
From “Letters of George Meredith ”
(2 vols. Constable, per Dymsck’s
Library, Sydney).
—-+
The first number of the British Review
(Williams and Norgate), incorporating the
Oxford and Cambridge Review, gives good
value for its shilling. Compared with the
courageous modernity of the English Re-
view, this rival has a conservative air—the
taint of the universities is still faintly ap*
parent. Its contributors include Tolstoy,
Belloc and the inevitable Chesterton.
Lieutenant-Governor J. H. P. Murray
furnishes in his comprehensive work, Papua
(Unwin, per Lockley Library, Sydney) full
particulars of our great Dependency. His
view-point is that of a tolerant and sane
administrator, equipped with something more
human than the usual official understanding
of the native. The conclusion of the reader
is that in such efficient hands Papua hasth ‘
best chance of development. Also, th. i..
Australia is quite able to breed her
own Their Excellencies. The task of
civilising the hotch-potch of races that in-
habits this section of New Guinea, however,
is a hard one, as may be seen from this
statement of the problem : “When a man has
to hold himself in readiness to fight for his
life at a moment’s notice, and when in addi-
tion he has to get a livings out of the earth
with the aid of tools made of sticks and
stones and shells, supplemented by any fish
or game that he can procure by weapons
made of the same material, and when his
chief amusement consists of head-hunting
and raids on other villages, he is likely to
grow into a rather strenuous person of active
habits; and then, if he is suddenly told that
his life is perfectly safe, and is presented
with implements which will enable him to do
as much in a day as he could do before in a
week, but is told that he must never go on
any more raids and never collect any more
heads, he is likely to fee] a void in his ex-
istence, for his chief occupations will be
gone, and unless something is given to him
with which to fill the void he and his de-
scendants will suffer. This is the case of the
Papuan; we have taken away his old ideals
of war and bloodshed, and it is our duty to
put a new ideal in their place—to substitute
the activity of labor, for that of fighting, and
to transform the tribe of disappointed war-
riors into a race of more or less industrious
workmen.
Gissing in His Books
One who has enjdyed the art of Gissing,
and found fascination in the glimpses it
givea of his personality (surely .better than
full revelations), may be permitted to doubt
whether Morley Roberts was justified in
stripping the corpse in quite .the way de-
scribed on the “Red Page” of January 30.
Since the Roberts book has done this, how-
ever, it is perhaps worth while to indicate
a few of the directions in which one has
often guessed at the relation of the novels to
the character and experience of the man.
(I haven’t seen Roberts’s Private Life of
Henry Maitland, but I have read and re-
read nearly all that Gissing wrote, whether
collected or scattered.)
The heroine of The Unclassed, Ida Starr,
is apparently an idealisation of Gissing’s
first wife, the street girl whom he thought
to reform. Yet so do his portraits mingle
and merge that the awful Harriet Smales of
the novel may be the same woman in cold
reality, older and degenerate. A ‘repetition
of Harriet appears in the short story called
“A Lodger in Maze Pond,” and, like Gissing,
the man who is freed from her by death
rushes soon after into another foolish mar-
riage, this time with a girl who is “respect-
able,” but who is also utterly unsuited to
him. Why ?
“Listen, and I will tell you about it. I
am a fool about women. I don’t know what
it is—certainly not a sensual or passionate
nature; mine is nothing of the sort. It’s
sheer sentimentality, I suppose. I can’t be
friendly with a woman without drifting into
mawkish tenderness —there’s the simple
truth. If I had married happily, I don’t
think I should have been tempted to go
about philandering. The society of a wife I
loved and respected would be sufficient. But
there’s that need in me—the incessant hun-
ger for a woman’s sympathy and affection.
Such a hideous mistake as mine when I
married would have made a cynic of most
men; upon me the lesson has been utterly
thrown away. I mean that, though I can
talk of women rationally enough with a
friend, I am at their mercy when alone with
them—at the mercy of the silliest, vulgarest
creature. After all, isn’t it very much the
same with men in general?”
&
In The Unclassed, Gissing seems to use
Various sides of himself in Osmond Way-
mark, and in the love for classic lands and
lore of that hero’s friend, Julian Casti.
Waymark has that way of making a mess
of his love affairs of the usual self-reflecting
Gissing hero. He has been an atheist and
socialist of the early ’Bo’s (like the respec-
tive heroes of Born in Exile and Demos, and
also like their author), and rails in tne
usual style of Gissiug’s garret heroes and
their parent at the plight of intellect with-
out money, and without the solace of female
beauty and worth :
“Money means virtue; the lack of it is
vice. The devil loves no lurking place like
an empty purse. Give me a thousand pounds
to-morrow, and I become the most virtuous
man in England.... The moneyed man is
the sole king; the herds of the penniless are
but as slaves before his footstool. .. .Even
death he faces with the comforting con-
sciousness that his defeat will only coincide
with that of human science. He buys cul-
ture, he buys peace of mind, he buys love. —
You think not? I don’t use the word cyni-
cally, but in very virtuous earnest. Make
me a millionaire, and I will purchase the
passionate devotion of any free-hearted
woman the world contains!”
A common- note this in Gissing’s novels,
and to appreciate it one must have lived
lonely and poor in cities of wealth, or have
otherwise walked in Hell. Perhaps you have
never been there? What wonder if the in-
tellectual man who has should have his
thoughts turned towards one way of escape,
even pretence? I noted years ago the curi-
ous way in which a number of his plots
turn on the pretences of a sympathy-starved
or money-starved man of intellect, the sell-
ing of a soul for some advantage in life or
love. The hero of Our Friend the Charla-
tan exemplifies this in party politics. And
Godwin Peak, of Born in Exile, takes steps
towards becoming a clergyman, though
really an unbeliever, for the sake of winning
a woman of beauty and refinement; only to
find when too late that her beliefs have
gradually been turning from orthodoxy.
(This, remember, was when Darwin was'
still thought shocking.) Richard Mutimer, of
Demos, uses socialism for his own advan-
tage. Jasper Milvain, of New Grub Street,
turns literary journalist really with a view
to scoring by “what the public wants,”
though with a pretence at giving the real
thing. But, after all, Milvain is the only
one who benefits to the end, so, if 'you like,
you can consider that the working out of
the pretence plots is moral.
Every reader of Gissing must have noticed
how often he brings in food; others before
Roberts have mentioned it, but the food re-
frain is not surprising from a man who had
known what it was to be glad to dine in
London on twopenn’orth of pease-pudding
(vide Ryecroft, The Nether World, etc.),
or in “Troy, U.5.A.,” on peanuts m
Whelpdale, of New Gruh Street)
Well, I’m afraid this hasn’t told l
about Gissing’s books to those who alt?
know them. There are so many more than
those named, and all have so many sides i
them. Even his two or three potboilers
worth half a circulating library of averse
novels, and at least nine of his books a
masterpieces. Read, for a start, Will Wn
burton, The Odd Women, Born in pJv"
New Grub Street, In Jhe Year of JuUle
Henry Ryecroft, Human Odds and Ends—ff
the bookseller can find them for you. “TIT
Day of Silence,” a story in the book last
named, is one of the greatest things in
tragedy ever written. Forced into the slums
he found such pearls in the gutter. ’
NORMAN LILLEY.
The Wowser as hero has arrived in fi c .
tion. John Scarlett, Ganger (Hodder and
Stoughton, per George Robertson, Sydney)
by Donald Maclean, the author of that well-
wrought Australian yarn. The Man from
Cur die's River, is a Y.M.C.A. youth, who
quotes Bunyan, and looks a weed, but he can
swim well enough to rescue the heroine—
for himself, and, according to the author:—
His strength was as the strength of ten
Because his heart was pure. '
However, he is not as bad as that. By the
mistake in a name this innocent clerk is
engaged by the Y.M.C.A. to take charge of
the souls of the navvies at the Seventeen-
Mile Camp while the railway to Powlett
River State coal mine was being hurriedly
built. Though scared by the task, Scarlett
sticks to it, and by a combination of good
luck and doggedness contrives to make good
and, of course, collar the girl. The life at
tlie camp is admirably done, ahd good fic-
tional use has been made of the Australian
environment. But the book is obviously built
on the formula of Ralph Conor, for whose
novels both hero and heroine naively express
a deep admiration. The humor, in so moral
a book, is inevitably strained and false; but
the author’s sincerity and his belief
in Australia strike the right note. The
plot, with its obviously dragged-in res-
cue scene, is trite, and the writing
decorously dull. For Scarlett’s sermon gets
an unabridged report, and Alexander’s
alleged hymns are reverently quoted. Even
Judson appears. But there is a public for
these things ; and the book has its other in-
terests. A fair work-a-day Australian novel.
An admirable Australian novel, built, it is
true, upon the conventional formula, but
accurately “placed” in its Australian en-
vironment, is Mrs. Daskein’s A Desert Rose
(Heinemann, per Dymock’s Library, Syd-
ney). This first novel displays a clear
ability to write the popular novel —the
female of its species. ‘ln this sort of novel
the heroine, who loves one man—usually of
the big, strong, lean, masculine brand—con-
sents under financial pressure to marry the
other man. But the reader is comforted by
the knowledge that the last chapter will do
the fair thing for hero and heroine. In this
novel, the “desert rose,” the adorable Betty,
sacrifices herself, for the necessary number
of chapters, to save her father, a squatter,
from ruin. But, apart from the bare effron-
tery of such a shop-worn plot, A Desert Rose
has claims as a real Australian novel. The
feeling is Australian, the life in the back-
blocks is sincerely pictured, without the
usual exaggeration of pessimism or the
equally ' usual hilarious optimism, the
characters are real human people, limned
with many a deft touch of observation, and
seen through shrewd but genial eyes, and the
sentiment, though occasionally slopping over
from the characters into the author, is not
frantically feminine. Here is a picture of
the bush that is really rather like the real
bush. Mrs. Daskein may go on: she has
started admirably equipped.
Dugald Ferguson, of M.L. (author of
Castle Gay and Other Poems, recently re-
viewed on this page), writes: —
Sir, —I perceive the moral ethics of the “Red Page"
of The Bulletin have undergone no change : for the
better, since—-not sufficed with the vilification of my
colonial novel, Bush Life— and few more popular
works of the kind bear the imprint of eolonial author-
ship—its editor had the cool audacity to cast an
aspersion on my own personality. i.
In the present instance, the sneering reference of
“commonplaces” to the contents of a volume among
which are such poems as “The Lambs,” “The Flag of
Scotland,” “The Battle of Lutzen,” proves you to be
far gone in a bad form of cynicism. And that the
high moral picture of the leading poem must, there-
fore, be alien to your taste I can as readily imagine as
must be abhorrent to your Philistine soul the senti-
ments in the eighth verse of the fourth chapter of St.
Paul’s epistle to the Philippians.
Your knowledge, by your reference to “The Two
Sisters,” that from a* condition of manual toil I have
produced the other works mentioned in mv title
page, and which knowledge seems to give additional
zest to your flippancy, demonstrates that there is not
a particle of true manhood in your composition.
It was only on a friend’s assurance (for I seldom
see your paper) that the former blackguard comments
upon me in your “Red Page” had been succeeded
by a more respectful mention of my name therein
that I again courted the risk of unenviable notoriety
from your coarse lampoons.
But, henceforth, I will simply regard the Sydney
Bulletin as but an unmanly Bully.
Unfortunately the poems mentioned by the
author are too long for reprinting in
and selections therefrom would not be quite
fair to the author.

Shotgun
Rival”
Cartridges
There is economy in making cartridges out of paper, but
paper cartridges are liable to be affected by so many condi-
tions that great care should be taken in their selection. You
are perfectly safe in buying Winchester “New Rival” shot-
gun cartridges, as these cartridges are made according to
correct principles. The paper is manufactured specially and
made into cartridges by automatic machinery, which insures
correct size and compression. They are then waterproofed
by a process which absolutely excludes moisture of whatever
nature. The loading is all done by automatic machinery,
infinitely more certain than human hands, and only standard
brands of powder, shot and wadding are used. The primer
is sure and quick, and that corrugation around the head —a
patented feature allows for expansion and prevents the head
from pulling off. “New Rival” shotgun cartridges are
made in various gauges suitable for all kinds of shotguns
and are blue in color. They are made strong to permit reloading.

ELECTRIC LIGHT PLANT
(Lister-Bruston System—Patented).
This plant generates electricity automatically, and does away with large and
expensive storage battery. Can be started from drawing-room or bedroom.
With the exception of keeping the plant clean, lubricating and filling the
tanks, no attention is required.
Upon switching on the lights the plant works, and when the lights are turned
off the plant stops automatically.
The set is self-contained, and is delivered ready to work. No erection or
foundations necessary; all one has to do is to connect to the wires to the house,
shop, hall, hotel, or town lights, and fill up the tanks with oil. It is exceptionally
cheap as regards lighting costs, the user being able to enjoy all the many advantages
of electric light, at a rate per candle power cheaper than most systems of petrol
gas, acetylene, and other artificial lights, and have current always ready for lights,
fans, cooking, pumps, saws, separators, milking and chaffcutting, sewing and other
machines.
Is absolutely safe and reliable; no smell or fumes, dirt or smoke.

The Inky Way.
Gerald Longson and “Van Eck” Parks have drawn
out of the proposed Adelaide theatrical monthly.
However, Lipman, who is financing the venture, wll
go through with it. The title is to be The Call-
Boy.
*** * .
A new Socialist weekly is coming out in London,
with Bernard Shaw and Sidney Webb as its leading
pen-pushers. The name proposed is the Statesman,
long the style and title of a leading Indian journal.
Its price is to be 6d., and it will specialise in litera-
ture as well as Fabian politics.

Adam Lindsay Gordon’s poems are everybody’s pro-
perty now, their copyright having expired. One
London publisher already announces an edition in
336 pages, with “12 stirring illustrations in color by
Capt. G. D. Giles” —ss. in buckram ; 7s. 6d. in
Persian velvet.

The new Governor of the State of New York Wi
liam Sulzer, dispensed with all the customary ’nnm
at his inauguration. He quietly walked to the Wi?
lative Chambers, took the oath without any show f
millinery, and then addressed his old newsnano 01
reporter colleagues this way: “There has been wl
much circumlocution as regards the relationshm
the State’s Chief Executive to the Press and* th
public. I want you boys to regard my room as on®
to you at all times, because you are really repress
tatives of the people. It will be unnecessary to reaoh
others before reaching me ; the door will be onen at
all times for the reporter. I will answer all th
questions that I can. There may be some that I m
find it inadvisable to answer; if so, I will sav «
and will not be evasive about it.’’ Then he went ™
into personal matters, and concluded with the remnrL
that his name was still Bill.

they hinted pretty broadly that they would not
handle any newspapers on Christmas Day for th*

* * * *
“Looking Backward”: Among some early Maori
land records 1 find a copy of a Sydney Government
order which seems to have interfered with a pro
mising industry. It was issued from the Colonial
Secretary s Office on April 16, 1831, and states that
steps are to be taken to prevent the “inhuman and
disgusting traffic in human heads at New Zealand”
The Excellency of the time (the gentle Ralph Dar-
ling) expressed a hope that “those possessing human
heads recently brought from New Zealand by the
schooner Prince of Denmark will immediately deliver
them up, to be restored to the relatives of the
deceased parties to whom the heads belonged.” But
t °° 1 k a lot niore than this proclamation to kill the
head-hunting profession. When Samuel Leigh went
on his Methody mission to Maoriland one of his
earliest experiences was a visit to a head-shop in a
Maori village. The intelligent and obsequious shop-
walker informed him how much per capita his
wares were, and offered him a reduction if he took
a quantity. Leigh did no business, so the goods
were sold later to a ship captain. It does not
appear that any white men were punished for their
part in the trade. The heads were embalmed in a
style described as perfection, all tattoo marks being
preserved. It is alleged that prisoners were treated
to special courses of tattooing, and kept until the
marks were healed. Their heads brought high prices,
this kind being regularly recognised on the market
as A 1 grade. You can still see some of them in
respectable museums in Europe and ’Murka
WHEN MIGGLES ALSO SPOKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Miggles, M.L.A., also spoke.
—Newspaper report.
He rose up in his place, to talk—he got up, and he
spoke....
For all we know, when he began applausive thunders
woke
Loud echoes, with a burst of cheers —we cannot
vouch for that.
Perhaps his hearers yelled at him: “Yer torkin’
through yer hat!”
It may be that he belted Truth home with a master-
stroke ?
We do not know —we simply learn that Miggles also
spoke.
Perhaps he gained in speed and force, and bounded
as he went
Clean up among the rosy clouds of platform-senti-
ment ;
It may be that, with rhetoric disguising simple guff,
He bore his audience along on floods of bounce and
bluff.
He was impressive, hey ? Or p’r'aps a gaudy plat-
form-joke ?
We know not. Yet we know enough—that Miggles
also spoke.
Perhaps his hearers cheered; perhaps the speaker
merely flung
Bright pearls of flashing language unresponsive pork
among;
Perhaps in clear, compelling tones he got it off his
chest,
Or, pallid, mumbled out his piece, by platform-fears
possessed ;
Mayhap he raised a mighty voice, or merely rose to
croak ?
Can’t say! Dunno! We simply learn that Miggles
also spoke.
He also spoke—and it is not for kindly souls to gaze
Upon the orator next mom ; to note his grim amaze,
And with the eye of faith to see the unreported one
Just go off pop like any other wrathful Adam’s son—-
To watch him rive the rag in twain and wish its
owners broke
Which dams his flowing speech with “Mister Miggles
also spoke.”
VICTORIA.

ONE OF HUNDREDS OF TESTiMONiALS : Talbragar, Dubbo, Jan. 6, 1913.
Dear Sirs, —It is now about four months since your Air Gas Machine was installed in my
residence. Its efficiency has exceeded all expectations; for light, economy, cleanliness, as well as
for cooking purposes, it is the best that I know of. My 20-Light Machine provides sufficient
light for all purposes, in addition to supplying gas for the stove. To be able to do all cook-
ing by gas in a country home at a moderate cost is a convenience that must appeal to anyone
making a gas installation, especially when domestic help is at such a premium. Wishing you
all the success that your machine merits. —I am, yours faithfully, (Sgd.) F. 0. WAKE.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
Ballad of Triumphant Indigence.
P inancial complications dog my steps;
■My creditors are numerous as flies;
W ritters and suchlike legal demireps
I ervade my haunts with shifty, avid
eyes;
Mishaps encompass me about like smoke,
Intolerable griefs are always near—
Chiefly because I was so very broke
I lost the girl whom I adored last year.
There was a ghastly period of pain
When all of me seemed one vast aching
heart;
W T hen Sleep, the anodyne, was sought in
vain,
And women, drink, the drama, books and
Art
All failed most signally to bring me peace.
Each jealous surmise, ev’ry racking fear
Proved true before my anguish had sur-
cease.
I lost the girl whom I adored last year.
I do not sorrow now by day or night.
Far from it. Passion’s blinkers being off
I view the lady in another light.
I am enabled cheerfully to scoff
At my past miseries. Dull, selfish, vain
And hard she seems that once I held so
dear,
And I am glad (Illusion being slain)
I lost the girl whom I adored last year.
L’Envoi.
Poverty, people talk of you with blind,
Insensate hate, and shun you like the
deuce;
Yet are you often harsh but to be kind.
Plach deadly shaft you level has its use.
What though the world refuses you your
due!
To me, henceforth, a friend you will
appear.
If only for the reason that, through you,
I lost the girl whom I adored last year.
N.S.W
10-2-4.
On the Mash.
Lulu’s laughter fills her throat;
Lulu’s on the mash ;
Lulu knows the game by rote —
Thus she “makes a splash.’’
Wears her cheap but stylish clothes
With a queenly grace;
On her breast a scarlet rose,
Matching lips of scarlet, blows
’Mid soft folds of lace.
Lulu wears her big, hat
When she’s on the mash,
Big bows on her shoes, and that
Black and amber sash.
Making eyes and dimpling cheeks
Every now and then,
White teeth gleaming when she speaks,
Brown eyes dancing while she seeks
Eyes of captive men.
Lulu keeps her beaux at bay
When she’s on the mash ;
Frowns and pouts red lips when they
Grow too bold and rash!
On the Block at Manly Beach,
Lulu smiles aside
When bold eyes of men beseech,
When they murmur, “She’s a peach—
Bit ov style ’n’ pride!”
Lulu’s full of mystery;
When she’s on the mash
Lulu’s charm and modesty
Boldest men abash !
Youth and love of conquest blent
Brim her heart, a-flood
With a fugitive content,
And a dash of devilment
Tingles in her blood!
Lulu’s dreams are all romance;
When she’s on the mash
Love may come her way by chance —-
Fix her in a flash !
In her eyes a wistful smile
Challenges the men,
On her face no trace of guile—
“ She’s a pretty bit ov style!”
Greets her now and then.
Never doubting God or P ate,
When she’s on the mash
Lulu seeks her destined mate —
“Don’t she cut a dash !”
In her cheap but dainty clothes;
Lure of men’s desire,
In her heart, red as the rose,
Wildly, passionately glows
Love’s compelling fire!
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
The Love of Life.
As a dead man, cold and pallid.
As a drowned man shoreward borne,
They took me from the sea’s grip
In the red, angry morn.
It was little that I thanked them
As I lay agasp for breath ;
I had rather that they left me
To my cold, wet death.
Then they piled a fire of driftwood,
And they made my body warm ;
But my soul was chilled with thinking
Of the night’s black storm.
Oh. they did not guess (how could they?)
Why I trembled so and paled ;
But they soothed me softly, saying
’Twas my hurt bones that ailed.
Had they seen it as I saw it, .
Would the sight have made them wise?
With my fist-bruise on its forehead,
And its wild, wide eyes.
For the Love-of-Life is cruel.
And ’twill fight a selfish fight,
When the howling sea is hungry
In the pitch, black night.
Oh I wish it were my dead self
That the sea had shoreward borne,
When they took me from its wet grip
In the red, angry morn.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty -
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques., Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Mapager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/-’ is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Pfilitical, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 20, 1913.
FRENZIED FINANCE AND MAD
FINANCIERS.
This maddened country is still following
the downward track as regards its finances,
and though the Jew at the other end of the
deal is steadily putting up interest rates on
the spendthrift at this end, there is no sign
of returning sanity. In fact, there is rather
a tendency to put on an .extra spurt as the
animal turns into the straight and the win-
ning post of insolvency comes in sight. These
days there is a sense of loss and vacancy—
a feeling as if an old friend had vanished
from his usual haunts —when the morning
paper doesn’t mention that some fool Trea-
surer! or another has applied to his ancient
ally, 'Bull-Goiienstein, for a little more
accommodation, and is getting it at 4 per
cent, subject to a heavy discount. The day
of 3 and per cent, loans has passed away,
and this country’s rocky credit is back
where it was in the earlier times when it
was little known and consequently little
trusted. Now it is well known, and conse-
quently not too well trusted. During the
last financial year—the one which ended on
June 30, 1912 —the six States got through
£16,325,177 of loan money, and including
Maoriland the aggregate Australasian I O U
was over £20,000,000. This was heroic in
its way, but it is nothing compared with the
prospective total for the current financial
year. New South Wales supplies the most
exciting element in the case. It is blessed
with a Government which clambered pon-
derously into office on its solemn promise to
reduce loan expenditure, then standing at
the comparatively moderate figure—at least
it looks comparatively moderate now —of
3| millions per annum. It was as absolutely
committed to this as to any other feature of
its programme. Last financial year the
same gang of political pirates ran the loan
outgoings up to £5,491,103, and this year
they bid fair to reach £9,000,000. And the
McGowen Ministry shows no shame for this
monstrous fraud and deception—possibly
because it doesn’t know how. Quite a num-
ber of people don’t know how.
* * * *
The interest bill of N.S.W. increased by
£1,101,545 per annum between June, 1901,
and June, 1912, and at the latter date it
reached £8,531,528. In the same period the
interest bills of all the other States put to-
gether grew by only £1,190,707. By next
June the yearly Cornstalk tribute to Cohen
bids fair to reach nearly £3,900,000, which
is a hefty burden and requires a lot of
carrying. On the other hand, N.S.W.’s ac-
cumulated sinking fund is only about half
that of Victoria and one-fifth that of Wes-
tralia. Out of £5,843,542 of sinking funds
which had been got together to counterbal-
ance over £273,000,000 of debt —in the same
way as a beetle partly counterbalances an
elephant the Ma State owns about
£653,981. Certainly the present Ministry
has taken its solemn oath and covenant to
see that the Ma profligate establishes a
genuine, honest, upstanding sinking fund of
liberal dimensions, and some sanguine per-
sons imagined that it might do so, even
though it had promised, but the results so
far are not apparent. Nothing is very ap-
parent with the McGowen Cabinet except
the heavy stodge of its chief, the agility of
the second in command when it becomes a
matter of dodging awkward explanations—
and the ever increasing expenditure.
There are, of course —and it is consoling
to remember the fact —the permanent and
reproductive works, mostly railways and
tramways, as a set off against well over
£100,000,000 of Cornstalk debt. The gross
earnings of the railways and tramways in-
creased during the first half of the present
financial year (30th June, 1912, to 31st De-
cember, 1912) by £214,484 as compared
with the corresponding period of 1911. But
in the joyous process of buying popularity
for Ministers with public money, the work-
ing expenses were run up by no less than
£384,351. So, despite an enormous increase
of business —and the increase was enormous,
the number of passengers alone being nearly
22,000,000 more in the second half of 1912
than in the second half of 1911 —the amount
available to meet the interest hill was
£119,867 less, and the interest bill that had
to be met was a very great deal more. When
the year’s accounts are made up it will pro-
bably be found tnat the biggest of the per-
manent and reproductive works have become
a dead loss. And the means wherewith to
meet the loss are disappearing fast. At
June 30, 1912, the cash in the hands of the
Treasurer was nil, and there were over-
drafts—presumably kept afloat by borrow-
ing from trust funds amounting to
£1,448,230. At January 31, 1913, the Trea-
surer’s legitimate cash was again nil, and
the overdrafts—presumably kept afloat by
borrowing from the trust funds —had risen,
despite heavy borrowing and a great in-
crease of revenue, to £4,600,139. The ordin-
ary revenue for the first seven months of the
financial year (to January 31, 1913) was
£115,637 more than for the seven months to
January 31, 1912, but the expenditure of
the wonderful Mad Ministry grew in the
same period by £1,390,086. The ordinary
revenue promises to be over £16,300,000 for
the year, which is a handsome sum to have
at command, considering that £10,200.000
sufficed and left a decent surplus in 1900,
and that the cost of naval and military de-
fence, invalid and old age pensions, patents,
census, postal and telegraphic charges, etc.,
have in the interim become Federal instead
of State burdens. Yet the deficit for the
seven months to January 31. is £1,251,189,
and the McGowen combination will have to
swear off a good deal of sack and live much
more cleanly if the shortage, when the finan-
cial year ends, isn’t to approach £2,000,000.
It is worth noting just here that, though the
income for the whole seven months shows
an increase, the income for the last month
of the seven (January, 1913) revealed a
serious drop of £92,518, as compared with
January, 1912. If this is the beginning of
a general shrinkage the position is even
worse than it looks, and Heaven knows it
looks bad enough.
Under these conditions it is not surprising
that the cash reserves in the N.S.W. banks
were £2,417,341 less on the last day of 1912
than on the last day of 1911. The propor-
tion of cash, including Government naper,
to liabilities fell with a crash from £26 9s.
7d. per £lOO to £2l 18s., which is a rather
scant guarantee against another panic. In
Victoria the decrease in the cash reserves
has been only £54,317, but then Victoria,
with all its disadvantages and drawbacks,
hasn’t a McGowen Ministry in office.
William Watt may be something of a
political fraud and Murray may be a heavy
sleeper, but neither of them is quite mad.
In both States it is notable that the banks’
holding of Federal paner money has fallen
heavily, and the combined decrease in gold
and paper is over £3,100,000. This is a
very serious weakening of the position, and
it is accompanied by a disastrous slumn in
the gold output of the Commonwealth. Last
year the gold dug up was worth about
£900,000 less than in 1911, and in 1911 it
was worth £1,000,000 less than in 1910, and
in 1910 it was worth £1,050,000 less than
in 1909, and in 1909 it was worth £450,000
less than in 1908, and in 1908. to cut the
story short, it was worth £3,200,000 less than
in 1903. The number of sheep in N.S.W.
has been diminishing of late- —it was smaller
in 1911 than in 1910, and smaller in 1910
than in 1909 —which is not a cheering cir-
cumstance considering how much the public
depends on its woolly product. The esti-
mated wheat yield for 1913 is less
than that for 1912: and 1912 was a
poorer year than 1911 ; arid 1911 showed
up worse than 1910. In 1912 the
oversea imports of the Ma community in-
creased by £4,939,816, while the exports,
which are supposed to pay for them, only
expanded by £809,234. The two, in fact,
are now about equal, and N.S.W. depends
almost wholly on foreign borrowing to pay
the interest on its foreign public and pri-
vate debts, and dividends and rents to ab-
sentee investors.
* * * *
Outside N.S.W. there are no very joyous
happenings to counterbalance the great lack
of joyous happenings inside. The Common-
wealth’s only business undertaking of any
size is the post and telegraph department,
and it is expected to show a deficit next June
for the first time in many years. These are
the figures:—

Yet, in these years no less than £4,700,000
was spent out of revenue on new works.
This money had no interest burden to carry.
Whatever it earned was clear profit, and if
it earned only 2 per cent, there should have
been a visible return of £94,000 per annum.
There is no visible return whatsoever. Cer-
tainly there was great need to improve the
conditions of the postal and telegraph em-
ployees, and a need —not quite so great —to
give concessions to the public. But when
the department becomes a sort of pauper in-
stitution working at a loss it is difficult to
avoid the impression that, in the aunt for
popularity, concessions have been carried
rather far. Then the Australian Govern-
ment, while giving every possible encourage-
ment to the policy of pushing up wages, re-
fuses to imperil its job by improving the
tariff, so more and more cheap labor and
nigger and non-union goods climb over the
barrier to imperil the Australian worker’s
job; and Australia is being drained of
money to pay for them —drained to an ex-
tent which threatens a huge disaster. The
Australian Government feels good in its own
casual thoughtless way over these huge im-
ports, because it collects revenue duties upon
them. But except for the frenzied finance
of the State Governments it wouldn’t col-
lect half these beautiful duties, for the suffi-
cient reason that nobody can gather in the
money that isn’t there any more than he can
get beer out of a tree. The Commonwealth,
by reason of its rail-sitting tariff policy, is
living on loans just as much as the States
are doing, and if the loans give out Com-
monwealth and States will do the Gadarene
swine act together. An incidental side
issue of the case is that States and Com-
monwealth have, among them, 4308 miles
of railway under construction, and 1200 more
miles authorised. So they have a gigantic
liability ahead, the liquidation of which de-
pends mostly on Bull-Cohen’s affluence, or
his trustfulness, or his dislike to take ad-
vantage of a small and shattered relative.
The situation is one that can’t last. The
halo is going to blow away, and the rainbow
to crumple up, and there will of a certainty
be breakages of some sort. What the Aus-
tralian nation in general can do about the
matter is to impress on its hesitating Fed-
eral Government that it must make up its
mind to tariff reform, and that the rail isn’t
a commodious place to sit upon. What the
people of N.S.W. in particular can do is to
tear up the shameless McGowen Govern-
ment root and branch on election day, and
to make sure that no single member of it
ever again gets the nomination of the party
whose principles he has so grossly repudi-
ated. If Labor again gives its official sup-
port to any individual of the gang now in
office at Sydney the only possible inference
will be that the party is itself a liar and a
hypocrite, and that the plank re honest
finance which it put into its platform was
inserted merely to get votes under false pre-
tences. The many votes which the
McGowen crowd secured by its promises of
honest finance were acquired under false
pretences—no political Judas or Parlia-
tary Barabbas ever pretended more
brazenly. And unless the Laborites throw
the unclean thing off the premises they will
deserve all that is coming to them, and more.
No party with a reputation to maintain can
afford to be found dead along with
McGowen and Co.
THE GHASTLY ERROR OF W. M.
HUGHES.
On Friday last a shriek of derision was dis-
tinctly audible, in legal circles, from Port
Danger right through to N.-W. Cape—Friday
being the day on which the Government’s last
two High Court appointments were an-
nounced. Powers and Piddington, regarded
as High Court Judges, are not so much mis-
takes as grim tragedies. The S. M. Herald
is not a journal which is given to
over-plain speech. It mostly dodders
cautiously .round its subject, and leaves
the matter, ultimately, swathed in a
thick inconclusive haze of verbiage. The
Powers-Piddington affair scandalised it
into very pointed protest. So far as Pid-
dington was concerned it neither praised
nor blamed. It damned a friend of years’
standing by dead silence instead of with
feeble and mendacious cheers. As regards
Powers, it mentioned definitely (14/2/T3)
that he lacked “some of the most necessary
qualifications for the position,” and that “in
respect of the necessary equipment of legal
knowledge and professional experience,” he
fell “far short of the reouired standard.”
Those objections apply equally to both the
new appointees. Let them be judged on
their records. Piddington was, till W. M.
Hughes discovered him last week, a more
or less obscure junior, with a modest, in
fact, insignificant practice. Setting aside
the K.C.’s, it would be easy to name half a
dozen men at the N. S. Wales Bar, and many
at the Bar in other States, who are immeas-
urably his superiors. His personal character
is, of course, unimpeachable. On the other
hand he is one of the last whom a colleague
would select as the possessor of a judicial
mind. He possesses no sense of legal pro-
portion. His intellect is, forensically speak-
ing, of the perverse and pedantic order. He
was a “coach” for years, and the mark of
the schoolmaster is still on him in plain
figures. His experience of public affairs
(that experience which the Lord Chancellor
of England wisely remarked at the last
Guildhall Banquet, was “an essential in the
case of good judges”) has been meagre to
put it mildly. He beat Dibbs, on the Free-
trade ticket, in 1593, and for a few months
battled for low-tariffism, in company with
W. M. Hughes and others. Then the elec
tors turned him down, and he went out of
Parliament for life. Incidentally, he was
associated with Hughes as one of the joint
secretaries of the Anti-Federal League
Powers was for six years a Bundaberg
solicitor, and Bundaberg is a fourth-rate town
in Queensland. (The name is said to signify
something about a dog, and some people call
the place Towzer.) Powers got himself
struck off the rolls and became a barrister
The venture was so hopelessly unprofitable
that he presently went back again to the
Gent. One business. He got into the
Queensland Assembly in the ’Bo’s, but did
nothing sensational there. He jumped at the
job of Queensland Crown Solicitor at £6OO
per annum, and. a while later, he fairly
bounded at the Federal Crown Solicitorship.
He has been a useful Crown Solicitor. None
better than Powers to handle a Chow Cus-
toms case, to deal with an unlawful traf-
ficker in opium, or put the acid on a pro-
hibited immigrant. It is possible that he
has been helpful to Hughes in Constitutional
points. The latter, being no lawyer him-
self, is driven to denend almost entirely on
careful “devilling.” However, the probabili-
ties are that Garran, a really sound Con-
stitutionalist, has done most of this work.
These, then, are the men whom the Austra-
lian Government (or rather the Attorney-
General) has delighted to honor to the detri-
ment of the standing of the highest Court in
the land.
* * * *
The evil effects may not be felt by the
nation at once, but that they will be felt,
ultimately, is as complete a certainty as the
human imagination can conceive. Chief
Justice Griffith departs on a nine-
months’ leave of absence in March.
It is more than likely that he will
not return to Australia as a High
Court Judge. The British Parliament re-
cently passed an Act providing for the ap-
pointment of three Law Lords from the over-
sea Dominions. Unless these billets are
handed out on the W. M. Hughes principle
(that is to say, to the most flamingly un-
suitable characters available), Chief Jus-
tice Griffith must get the first offer that
is made. His passing would have gravely
weakened the High Court Bench at its best.
With O’Connor gone, Barton ageing, and
Powers and Piddington on deck, it is no
exaggeration to say that this jurisdiction
will be dragged down to the level of a Dis-
trict Court or thereabouts in many respects.
No sane litigant, with faith in the merits of
his case, is likely to appeal, say, from
Cussen, Hodges and Hood, of Victoria, to
Barton, Powers and Piddington, of the
High Court. It would be like going for
information regarding the Antarctic regions
from Amundsen to one of the Jap “ex-
plorers” who recently braved the horrors of
a Sydney winter at Parsley Bay. It would
be an appeal from an ironclad to a Balmain
ferry boat. The inevitable result must be
that, in a multitude of instances, the High
Court will be passed by. Litigants will pre-
fer to go straight to the Privy Council.
That is a lamentable prospect for a country
which has for long had good cause to be
proud of its State Judges, in the main, and
which has had an admirable High Court
Bench, take it for all in all, during the past
decade. It is also deplorable from the
merely commercial aspect. The men who
were fitted for this big job stand out like
beacons. The names which occur most
readily are Patrick McMahon Glynn, B.
R. Wise. Josiaii Symon and Irvine the
Iceberg. They have all had that public train-
ing, to again quote the Lord Chancellor,
“not merely as lawyers or jurists, but as
nublic men, knowung something of public af-
fairs, which should be insisted on in judges.”
Glynn is a sound Constitutionalist, and a
man of high mentality. Wise is one of the
few members of the Australian Bar
to-day who would be an ornament,
intellectually and otherwise. to any
Bench. Symon and Irvine, whatever
views one may hold concerning their poli-
tics, are indisputably what is known as
“first-rate men.” It is a scandal of a shriek-
ing description that such bright lights should
have been disregarded in favor of mediocri-
ties, not to say incompetents.
* * * *
The explanation of the calamity is to be
found chiefly in the fatal yearning—increas-
ingly observable of late on the part of a sec-
tion of the Fisher Ministry—to win hon-
eyed words of commendation from the Tory
press. That tendency did a lot to afflict
Australia with Yass-berra. It led to the
incompetent bungle about the Federal note
issue. It has now given us an emasculated
High Court. The Conservative attitude on
this question was tersely put bv the Sydney
Telegraph, last month, as follows: —
There is no need to go to militant politics, either
in or out of Parliament, for Federal Judges. Other
things equal, the less prominently a man has figured
in political strife the better his qualifications. _
History, alike in the United Kingdom, in
America and in Australia, proves that pro-
position to be utterly idiotic. We have not
had one really satisfactory Judge in this
country who has been a non-political. Ana
all our big men, from Griffith and Martin
downwards, have come from the ranks ot
“militant politics.” The same applies to tne
U.S. Supreme Court. Rake the
the glittering names which have adorned tne
English Bench during the past century witn
a fine comb, and you will not find halt a
dozen belonging to men that have shunne
public affairs. As a matter of policy merely,
and apart altogether from the question o
common justice, it is the wildest folly °“.t?
part of any Government to bestow the hie ■
est gifts at its disposal, “other thing
equal,” on citizens who have not thoug
their country so well worth serving as tn _
banking accounts, or who have not snot
sufficient capacity to be able to stay tor a -
length of time in the service of the »»_•
Such a procedure amounts to discourse s
the patriot, and putting a premium on s
fishness and national indifferentism. it
impossible to believe that, had either ot
new appointees been in what the t-E
GRAPH calls “militant politics,” he wou
have stood any earthly show of acfiie .
a High Court Judgeship. That is m , ;
another way of saying that they are w


they are because no one knows them. It is
considered, seemingly, in official quarters,
that because they are obscure, and in the
matter of character and achievements wholly
negative and colorless, their selection wiil
prove inoffensive. It may be so in the case
of the paltry few who take their views
ready-made from the reactionary press,
but if the Government considers that
the Commonwealth, as a whole, thinks
this way, it is perpetrating the mis-
take of an aeon. The High Court is
one of those Federal institutions in which
Australia has, for years, taken a particular
and justifiable pride. The administration
which from a desire to truckle to its enemies,
or from mere ignorance or stupidity, so acts
as to weaken this Court, must automatically
weaken itself. The best that can be said for
the man who was primarily responsible for
the disaster that befel last week, is that he
knew no better. W .M. Hughes is not a
practising barrister himself. He is ignorant
of the reputations that are being made and
lost in the Courts year by year. To him,
apparently, Brown, K.C., is on a par men-
tally and so forth, with his junior, and the at-
torney who instructs them is as desirable an
acquisition to the Bench as either. In brief,
he is about as well equipned to choose a
judge as he is to box the discolored horror,
Johnson, or to steer a Grand National fav-
orite. It is, indeed, a misfortune that the
destinies of a great Court should be in such
hands.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Ghost.
A certain spectre, of which little has been
heard during recent years, recrudesced in
Sydney last week, when a procession of un-
employed clamored at Premier McGowen’s
door. These visitants wanted work and
wages and friendly consideration. To them
Premier McGowen spoke kindly, but held
out little hope. He said that his Govern-
ment was already borrowing more money
(with more and more difficulty and at an in-
creasing rate of interest) than any previous
Government. It was doing this to find more
artificial employment on public works than
any previous Government (said works being
mostly additions to a railway system which,
so far as profits are concerned, is most lam-
entably on the down grade). Still he prom-
ised to see —not the Australian Prime
Minister about the tariff, but the Minister
for Works about the building of more ill-
paying railways, with very costly borrowed
money, that is increasingly difficult to get.
It is altogether a curious position. Aus-
tralia is a fairly rich country, and, as it has
only about 14 persons to the square mile, it
offers more chances to its people than any
other habitable land on earth. On the mere
question of land and space the prospect here
is 412 times as good as that in England.
But unfortunately something seems to have
gone wrong. The tariff being absurdly low
as compared with the high wages inside the
Customs barrier and the low wages in some
countries outside, or the wages being ab-
surdly high inside as compared with the
wages outside and the ridiculous baby ob-
struction between the two —it may be put
either way—work in Australia —and especi-
ally in N.S.W., which has a greater faith in
high wages and low tariff than any
of its neighbors —threatens soon to be
very scarce. No community can find
work for itself at making its own
boots and hats and clothes and iron-
work and a thousand other things, while it
insists on importing its boots and hats and
clothes and ironwork, and a thousand other
things from niggers and other foreigners for
the sake of alleged cheapness. The propo-
sition is too obvious to require any lengthy
dissertation or exolanation. During 1911
Australia imported goods to the value of
nearly £65,000.000, which was. in proportion
to population, more than the imports, of anv
other country in the world either in that
vear or in any previous period of recorded
history. That is to say, Australia, which is
Australia’s alleged Labor Government, de-
liberately threw away £65.000.000 • worth of
profitable work by following the advice of
the Tory S. M. Herald and Telegraph and
Argus and Courier, and the Tory Bruce
Smith and William McMillan and
Joseph Cook and their kind. Its sub-
servience to these papers and . people
was disgusting. In 1912 even this very
bad record was surpassed. The country,
under an alleged Labor Government, im-
ported £75,000.000 worth of goods, and
threw away £75,000,000 worth of ~ood,
profitable work, because its wretched alleged
Labor Government insisted on maintaining
the low tariff policy of the Tory papers above
enumerated, and the Tory politicians herein-
before mentioned. New South Wales sup-
plies the principal Tory and Freetrade or
Low Tariff element in the Federal Labor
party, and if a section of the New South
Wales people suffers from lack of work it
is only what it has been looking for. It it
is starving it is onlv what it has been asking
for. It has voted for hunger, and clamored
for hunger, and it seems likely to get hun-
ger. There is nothing to be gained by
complaining about lack of work while the
country insists on giving away most of
its work (as shown b;- the £75,000,000
worth of imports) to foreigners. No amount
of processions, deputations and demonstra-
tions will alter the case. It is based on the
simple proposition that nobody can spend his
monev and retain it; that nobody c ‘
his cake and have it; and that nobody can
be in two places at once. .When a com-
mnnitv has resolved to commit suicide it u
difficult to see what can be done save to let
it commit suicide. There is no power strong
enough to stop it from committing suicide.
Plugging Away at the Old Invention.
Prime Minister Fisher tried it on the dog
at the turning of the first sod of the K
goorlie-Port Augusta railway, by
his hearers that the line from to
Port Darwin was not the best connection
between North and South, and that the
“shortest and most economically sound idea
was to build a sort of coastal road f ora
Darwin into Fisher’s own State of Queens,
land. So Andrew apparent y nPmds to
break faith with South Austrahaifitseems
safp to do so and is sending up .vernal or
jaw balloons to find out how the wind blows.
fliere is something magnificent about this
effrontery. Adelaide is nearer to Port Dar-
W1 j tk an Melbourne, Sydney or Brisbane,
and almost as near as Rockhampton, though
it is farther away in a straight line than
lownsville. . But Townsville and Rock-
hampton are small places on wretched har-
bors, and it is no more possible to build a
transcontinental line from Darwin to either
of them than it is to build a transcontinental
from Sydney to Manly. A direct line from
1 Jarwin to Rockhampton would travel for
400 miles on the seashore, and a direct line
from the same place to Townsville would
travel for 400 miles under water. As for
Brisbane, in addition to being more remote
than Adelaide, it is a smaller city, and is
also far up a very hopeless and awkward
river. The proposed deviation involves: (1)
The practical abandonment of the large sec-
tion of transcontinental line already built
to Oodnadatta and bought from S.A. with
good Australian money; (2) the practical
abandonment of the whole transcontinental
idea in favor of what is much more like a
coastal line; (3) the practical abandonment
of the ideal of a line which will be federally-
owned from sea to sea, in favor of one
which will be largely State-owned; (4) a
great increase in mileage before connection
is made with a decent port, and a big centre
of population; and (51 a shameful breach
of the terms on which S.A. handed over the
Territory—a breach which may be held to
nullify the surrender of that province. But
against all this extra expense, and all this
sacrifice of big ideals, is to be counted the
satisfaction of Andrew Fisher’s parochial
greed to annex the railway for his own
State. The advocates of the eastern devia-
tion stick doggedly to one wooden-headed
assertion —that their route is the shorter.
If it is a question—as presumably it is—of
connecting the Territory with Australia’s big
ports and big centres of population, then the
statement is a glaring falsehood. The map
proves it to be one. The measured dis-
tances, as shown in the map, have been pub-
lished often enough, and as the evidence
can’t be refuted, the deviation crowd simply
ignores it and refuses to sneak about it.
It plugs away with its old invention, and
leaves things at that. Great and wonderful
is the art of plugging away with an old in-
vention.
The Land of Foreign Capital and
Blood.
“Monterey,” on a country which holds 600
millions of foreign capital
Who would believe me if I said that the present
trouble in Mexico is due directly to the Standard Oil
Co ? Few outside Wall-street probably. The unre.t
may be said to have started with the discovery of oil
in vast quantities in the regions then dominated ab-
solutely by Porfirio Diaz. He gave an °Pti° n °X® r
the wells to Sir Weetman Pearson —now Lord Cow-
dray (The size of the proposition may be gauged »
the fact that a few months ago Reuter reported that
20 oil ships were being built for the trade.) The
Standard Oil made its bid for the property, but too
late. As always, it next proceeded to threaten. The
coercive methods which are successful in 99. P ( T cent t '
of cases in U.S. would not have availed in Mexico
under all the circumstances. The Standard Oil put
it in plain terms to Diaz that he must either turn
Peahbon down or face a revolution. . (Three-quarters
of the South American revolutions originate in Wall
street that is to say they are financed there.) The
old man told the giant Trust, in effect, .to go to
Hades However, he made provision against what
promised to be the sharpest and heaviest snag of his
career, bv sending £1,000,000 sterling to P ar 'k.
Then the unpleasantness began. Pearson
took UP his stand beneath the folds of the
Union .Tack, so to speak. He mentioned that any atf r
temot to deprive him of his legally, acquired property,
whether on the part of the revolutionaries, or of any
possible Mexican administration, or of the. United
States would be attended to by Great Britain
In Monterey there lived a brilliant and ambitious
voung man, of considerable wealth and a Parisian
education, named Madero. To him.it was intimated
that if he wished to save his bleeding country from
the yoke of “the oppressor” (meaning the amazing
man who in 30 years had raised it from the status
of a stone-broke, robber-infested wilderness, to that
of an affluent, solvent, and more or less law-abiding
country) the necessary cash would be forthcoming.
The army was bribed on a scale of unexampled splen-
dor Anyone who was worth his salt as a. fighter got
a big price to desert Diaz. A land reform policv
was promised (a measure on something the lines of
lustraH™s graduated land tax) and a free and com-
pulsory education system. These' things however,
were mere gags—padding of an obvious description
The New York share-riggers had long since made back
all they were ever going to spend in fomenting and
prosecuting th* revolution, bv the operations they had
conducted in Wall-street in Mexican stocks. There is
nothing like inner knowledge in this variety of
gamble. In due course, since no man is so great as
to be able to hold his end up, with the pick of a
national army against him, old Porfirio Diaz made
a graceful “get away.”
Madero has been a failure. He is a useful soldier,
and would be a successful administrator in a normal
country. Mexico is not normal just now. It requires
a hand of iron enclosing a weapon of precision—
likewise absolute ruthlessness. Madero is too finick-
ing—in a word, too civilised —for the situation he has
created. During the couple of years that he has held
the fort, the country districts have suffered such a
recrudescence of outlawry as they have not known in
the memory of the oldest inhabitant. None of the
promised reforms have materialised. In a few centres
bull-fighting and cock-fighting have been suppressed,
and that has exasperated the districts affected as
much as Manly would be exasperated if surf-bathing
were prohibited. At the time of writing it seems as
though Felix Diaz, the old champion’s nephew, is a
certainty for the spiky presidential chair. It is too
early yet to say what particular section of Wall-
street capitalists is financing him. All that is abso-
lutely certain is that in various dark offices, some-
where within hail of Fifth Avenue and the Holland
House and the Statue of Liberty, numbers of anxious,
dyspeptic-looking men in loose clothes and absurd
boots are furiously at work tearing grotesquely large
fortunes off the multitude in connection with his
efforts.
What a splendid game it is! If you back a horse
for £lOO, although you may have personally stiff-
ened all the others in the race, the infernal animal
may fall down dead and lose you your money. If you
run a revolution, and are sufficiently affluent to do
it properly, no power on earth can upset the good
thing. No method of betting on a certainty has ever
been discovered to touch it. Of course, there are
the unpleasant incidentals of carnage and assassina-
tion, loot, rapine and general misery; but what are
these to a captain of industry living many thousands
of miles away?
V
Patent Medicine Rogues.
The Australian Government has risen above
the pressure put upon it, and exposed the
“secret remedies” of some of the world’s
notorious quacks as analysed by the British
Medical Association. And extremely stag-
gering is the reading thereof. One of the
popular catarrh fakirs advertises that “over
20,000 people died in the United Kingdom
last year of consumption caused by catarrh.”
To stop the fatality, he is rushing to the
front with a solution containing a trace of
carbolic acid, a pinch of common salt and
water. The cost to him, as the manufac-
turer, of 2i fluid ounces is one-thirtieth of a
farthing; the ,cost to the public is Is. An-
other catarrh (alleged) cure costs the manu-
facturer id. for lioz., but the purchaser is
relieved of 4s. 6d. before the remedy
becomes his. Another specific costs
one-tenth of a penny to decoct and
4s. to swallow. A whooping cough
remedy, stated by the maker to be so com-
plicated that he could trust nobody to make
it but himself, was found on analysis to con-
sist of milk sugar only. No other constitu-
ent was detected. One of the most brilliant
cheap jacks was the maker of a consump-
tion “cure.” He sold two liquids, which
were little more than colored flavored water.
The gull was expected to take a month’s
supply at a time, and it was obtainable at
the modest figure of £2 10s. A kidney dis-
ease “cure,” consisting of ordinary white
sugar pills, cost one-thirty-fifth of Id. per
bottle to make. In the selling shops the
price was one shilling. One of the most
widely advertised ointments for sores,
sprains, open wounds, sore breasts, ulcers,
burns, scalds, eczema, corns, rheumatism,
sciatica and 478 other troubles costs Is.
per three-fifths of an ounce. It is made up
of 14 p.c. oil of eucalyptus, 20 p.c. pale
resin, 55 p.c. soft paraffin, 11 p.c. hard par-
affin and a trace of coloring matter. To
produce three-fifths of an ounce costs the
English manufacturers Id. Here is the un-
lovely description of a patient after a quack
with a cancer “cure” had Been on the job :
In the case of one woman who had been informed
that the cancer had been cured, and that she only re-
quired some tonic medicine to complete the cure, the
surgeon who was called to her when she was in ex-
tremis said that he never beheld anything like it
in his life. The whole breast was a necrosing mass,
black and stinking, the ulcers extending up to the
collarbone and down to the margin of the ribs and
across the middle line. The hand could have been in-
serted under the margin of the dead part all round.
This was a result of the misuse of caustics
(the main standby of this kind of quack),
and is quoted for the particular benefit of
those who may be allured by the big adver-
tisement headline, “Cancer Treated Without
the Knife.” One of the worst: curses is that
known as “female medicines.” It is at once
a most profitable and harmful curse —profit-
able in that boxes of pill mullock which cost
practically nothing to manufacture are sold
at 10s„ 21s. and often more, and harmful in
that many of the nostrums, while useless for
the purpose in view, contain highly danger-
ous irritants which are best _ calculated to
place the quack’s victim within reach of a
neighboring undertaker. The question now
arises in view of the decision of the Govern-
ment of Australia to officially distribute the
disclosures of the B.M.A. regarding these
“patent” medicines: What is to be the next
move? If the proposal is to do no more
than hand copies of the papers to “members
of Federal and State Legislatures, public
libraries and other public institutions, it
doesn’t seem much of a proposal for the
proper protection of thousands of ignorant
and foolish folk. In fact, it seems about as
e-ood an idea of health protection as the
Fisher Cabinet’s idea of a Protective tariff .
Which is darned triffinsr.

The Unwisdom of the Tactics of the
Sharper.
Liberalism has been frankly baffled, _for a
long time past, in the attempt to find an ex-
planation of why it is so widely unpopular.
There are several reasons: but, undoubtedly,
one of them is the party’s congenital ina-
bility to play the game It tells too many
lies, and it doesn’t even tell them well. A 1
the world loves a liar (that is to say, a good
liar) for a time—a strictly limited time.
But a wild, reckless, blundering liar —the
sort who supplies a flaming mendacity and
its complete disproof practically at one and
the same time —inspires nothing but exas-
peration. Anti-Socialism was slain at
birth, half a dozen years ago, by maladroit
inaccuracy. George Reip, his followers and
the Conservative press calmly submitted the
proposition that the intensely law-abiding
WATSON-ites were Anarchists. This conclu-
sion was arrived at by the following line of
argument: Many of the WATSON-ites are
Socialists; but some Europeans who call
themselves Socialists are really Anarchists ,
therefore all WATSON-ites are Anarchists.
The Deakin party was in sympathy with
the Watson crowd in those days. Accord-
ingly all DEAKiN-ites were proclaimed to be
no better than Anarchists. On what the
gydney Telegraph termed the Day or
Power,” Reid came out with a shrieking
appeal to the electors wherein they were
asked to vote for him and his nominees, and
against Anarchy! The constituencies re-
sponded by turning the unreliable old man
down in all sorts of unexpected places. It
amounted, in a sense, to a solemn registering
by Australia of its aversion to having its leg
pulled. But liberalism has learned nothing
from its past troubles. It still trafficks in
the most intelligence-insulting goods. For
example, Beuce Smith, M.P., supplied the
following to the papers last week: —
If the Australian people are watching a great
cricket or football contest, upon which their own
national welfare depended, and the members of the
losing team raised a cry to “Kick the umpire off the
field,” the onlookers would have no hesitation in
writing down those who raised that brutal cry as a
gang of hooligans, who had no desire to play the
game, but only to use brute force, in order to get
their own way. Yet that is the present political
situation
The chief Labor organ of Queensland heads one of
its latest leading articles in the following swash-
buckling language: “The High Court Must G 0.”....
The Australian people ought to know what sort of
men, and what sort of journalism we should have to
navigate our ship of State if they should again re-
turn the Labor-Socialist party to power.
Obviously, the above, if it means anything
whatever, means that in the event of the
Fisher Government being returned to
nower, the High Court will be wiped out.
But who is likely to credit such imbecility?
This Administration had a chance, the other
day, to do itself a particularly good turn as
regards the High Court. A vacancy
occurred. The appointment of a Judge of
known Babor sympathies would have done
away with all excuse for talk about abolish-
ing this Court. The Ministry handed the
seat to F. Gavan Duffy, a Liberal of the
starkest brand. Of its last two selec-
tions, one, at least, is as resolute
an anti-Laborite as there is in N. S.
Wales. The public fully appreciates these
facts. It understands just how much rela-
tion there is between the views advocated
in “the chief Labor organ of Queensland,”
and the views held by the present Australian
Government. That is to say, it knows that
Bruce Smith’s latest effort to confuse
Labor with Anarchy is utterly dishonest.
