Can Australia Ride?
Frank Brown, who should know, swells
with indignation:—
“Cavalryman’s” (B. 6/2/’l3) ideas of horseman-
ship are upside down. He holds that the European cav-
alry man is the ideal horseman, whilst the Australian
is little better than the Jap. It is true that the
average Australian is not a good horseman; neither
is the average man anywhere. The European expert
can ride a well-schooled horse over a fence, but does
this constitute horsemanship ? How does he get on
with a rough horse? He can’t get on one without
assistance. He can’t sit a buck. He is unable to
manage a bad-mouthed horse without a martingale
and curb bit. He rides on a horse’s mouth, and uses
the curb bit as a remedy for all the shortcomings in
his mount. How would he get on chasing a steer
through scrub, with or without a curb bit ? The
result in either case would be disastrous for him.
An Australian horseman would get three times more
work out of his mount than a Britisher, because the
one rides easily with the motion of his mount, whilst
the other sits like a poker, and is a dead-weight on
the gee’s back. The Britisher has only one sort of
seat in the saddle; the Australian rides his horse
according to its humor or action.
Not long ago, whilst I was in England, I saw
cavalrymen, grooms, farmers, etc., trying to ride the
rough horses in the Wild Australian Buekjumping
Show. In not one instance did one of them gain his
seat without assistance. In not one instance did the
riders last two bucks. The newsboys of Sydney or
Melbourne woulcl have made an infinitely better at-
tempt. When the show was touring India, the
different buckjumping contests, held for soldiers,
were invariably won by stray Australians who hap-
pened to be members of the British army.
The shortcomings of British cavalrymen lie in their
riding schools. They are not allowed to ride natur-
ally. If “Cavalryman” would see horsemanship,
let him go to the back country, where riding is still
in the day’s work. He will see men draft their
horses from a wild, galloping mob, in the big stock-
yard, ride them, mouth them, and next day muster
cattle, crashing through scrub, or tearing over broken
crumbly ground on their half-tamed mounts. That
is horsemanship. The outback Australian does with
a wild, green horse what the British cavalryman can-
not do with a well-schooled, quiet one.
To which “Cavalryman” makes answer: —
The trouble with Frank Brown, in this matter,
is that he knows a little —a very little —of what he
writes about. He has probably ridden a quiet sta-
tion hack, now and then, and, when in England,
hired one of those pathetic saddle-horses which one
sees in all their spavined and wind-galled misery at
places like Margate and Brighton. Of course the
European cavalryman (British and Continental) does
not get on well with a rough horse —meaning an
unbroken one. But no one expects him to. The
making of cavalry horses is the work of professional
rough-riders. These gentry did their work so rottenly
for our Australian cavalrymen and gunners the other
day that a number of horsemen were seriously injured
at Victoria Barracks and an official inquiry was insti-
tuted. The Court did not delve into the question of
why the men fell off. All it wanted to know was
why the horses weren’t sufficiently docile and well-
mouthed to be safe conveyances for horsemen of
average ability. No cavalryman is expected to sit
buckjumpers, any more than members of a cycle corps
are required to be able to ride their machines back-
wards, or perform on them the various other tricks
to which music-hall performers have habituated us.
War is not so much a vaudeville show as a business.
As regards steering horses through scrub, most, for-
eign cavalrymen would be about as good at this as
Australian soldiers. Not one Australian soldier
in 10 is a scrub-riding expert. The German, French
and Belgian troops specialise in galloping through
forests and narrow forest “rides,” and are particu-
larly skilful thereat. Of course, none of them would
be as useful at this game as the expert local cattle
man. But then he represents only about 1 per cent,
of our, population, if that.
Fr\nk Brown’s view's about the European cavalry-
man sitting “like a poker,” and hence having only
“one sort of seat in the saddle” and being a dead-
weight,” and a mug generally, betray an abysmal
ignorance of the subject which I had thought was
confined to a few travelling Yanks and suchlike.
The “regulation” seat which cavalrymen practically
all over Europe have to adopt while on parade and
so forth, is well known to have no influence whatever
on their individuality as horsemen in other respects.
I remember seeing Hon. R. Ward (a brother of
Lord Dudley), riding with his regiment, the Blues,
in the regulation style. And a week or so later I
saw him land his horse, Cathal, second in the Liver-
pool Grand National. No one negotiates that course
who can’t ride his horse “according to its humor or
action.” It would not be in the least difficult to
cite 500 well-known Germans, or Irishmen, or Italians
(especially Italians) who are competent to steer a
cavalry horse of fair class over four miles of stiff
country anywhere —which is one of the Drime tests
of a cavalryman’s value to his native land. How
many well-known Australians are capable of that
feat 9 How many squatters, or others who can afford
it go hunting, "or ride at shows, or ride steeple-
chases as we find amateurs doing in other parts of
the world’ In other words, how many of our poten-
tial defenders are educating themselves in an art
which is an essential factor in the winning of battles?
The fact that Frank Brown, .while m England,
saw cavalrvmen. grooms, etc., being flung off trick
horses in the Wild Australia Buekmmping Show
has as aforesaid, nothing to do with the case. Nor
is there anything relevant to the question in the fact
that he saw struv Australians win buekiump con-
tests Buckjump riding is as much a trick as shoot-
ing breakers on a surf beach. With nra-’t'ce any
fairly good horseman of courage can learn it: with-
No Stone Unturned.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We leave no stone unturned to try and discover
offenders and bring them to .justice.— Sub-inspector
O’Donnell, in defence of Melbourne police force.
If only criminals were crabs,
And had a smaller stock of brain,
The Nemesis that flies in cabs
Would not turn over stones in vain.
The rocks of Reason in their way,
’Neath which Crime’s lurking foxes sneak,
Defy their efforts night and day:
Their mental muscles are too weak.
They fill their clothes, hut not their hats;
like high-speed bloodhounds do they hop
In fierce pursuit of Bourke-street “rats” —
An avalanche of flying cop.
But sool them on to something big,
The eager pack is halt and blind,
And in a sad, sore-footed jig
Goes baying failure down the wind.
Night’s draggled cats vacate the streets,
And where their frightened, sad eyes
burned,
A dog unto a hound repeats :
“ They’re noin' ter leave no stone un-
turned!"
They turn whatever stones they may,
And canture legal grubs and ants.
While Crime’s rock pythons coil away,
Or meanly bite them in the pants.
With three dull pounds or so ner week
’Tis vain to bid for mental light
That will on rocky problems wreak
The rage of Thought’s pure dynamite.
The pebble-turner, half-afraid,
Goes down the track by Custom led,
More futile than the scared old maid
Who turns and looks beneath the bed.
Crime flies and burrows, swims and scoots,
And from high mountains drops its bones ;
It can’t be killed with outsized boots;
It isn’t always under stones.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.


THE POLICY OF THE GOVERNMENT
IS BACKED BY THE SOUND

“As good as a trip to
the Isthmus,” declared
hundreds.
Isn’t it a wonderful
science that brought
this great work to
thousands —who other-
wise would never have
been able to see it.
The Picture habit is an
enlightening habit.
out practice an Arthur Nightingall or Roddy
Owen would be defeated in short order.
Frank Brown’s suggestions that- I should go to
the back country to learn about horsemanship, and
that I should visit cattle stations to see green horses
well handled are both beside the point. In the sheep
country, notoriously, 90 per cent, of the inhabitants
never see a horse that a child couldn’t ride. Mostly
they either drive in sulkies or buggies or use bikes.
Putting aside the exceptional brilliant rider who is
chiefly to be found on a few remote cattle stations,
and in travelling buckjump shows here and there, I
say seriously that Australians are poor riders. It
is bad patriotism to shut one’s eyes to the fact.
Since the art only comes intuitively to a very few,
and since, also (owing to the way our young men
are swarming into the big cities) opportunities even
for these marvels are becoming increasing difficult,
it behoves the Government to get.busy in the matter.
Our trainees should be systematically taught riding
by practical men. They don’t need to learn to head
off cattle, or subdue outlaws. But they do need to
be able to urge a fairly well-broken and sound horse
over an average line of country—the sort of country
that the soldier is liable to encounter in war-time.
Failing this, they will make a dreary show when the
hour of trouble strikes.
“P.X.”: It is not necessary to call an
honest egg-laying hen a peacock in order to
say that it is a good and useful fowl; and
one doesn’t reflect on Scott’s fine human
qualities when one says that nine-tenths of
the stuff with which the newspapers have
been pulling the public’s leg is ridiculous
guff. “These,” says jthe newspaper leg-
puller invariably—“these are the men who
have made the Empire what it is.” They
may have done a bit; but men of the very
opposite qualities have done vastly more—
the men who stay at home, and keep their
noses to the grindstones of trade and manu-
facture and social reform. Germany is
great, too —how? Where are its Polar ad-
venturers? Obviously, from their absence,
these men are not essential to greatness —
modern greatness, anyway. One spectacled
scientist, stewing in a garret over a new
process for extracting wealth from some
old form of dirt is of more consequence
nowadays, from the national point of view,
than a "score of seekers after the bubble re-
putation of finding a Pole whose discovery
won’t fill one hungry citizen’s stomach or
add a pound to the strength of his arm. If
the possession of men who can make heroic
dashes to the Pole or the sea-bottom and
such-like leads to national greatness. Nor-
way ought to be about the greatest nation
in the world; for it can still produce its
Amundsens and Nansens, who appar-
ently do this kind of job bettter than
anybody else in the world. But Nor-
way isn’t a “great” nation in the sense the
nublic’s leg-pullers mean when, they maunder
about England’s greatness; in fact, it is
largely because it is best fitted, to do the
Amundsen roving job that it isn’t great.
And a nation of Scotts, while no doubt it
would be picturesque, would be of still less
account, seeing that it would just fail where
the other easily succeeded.
Nielsen, the inconvenient, not to say hos-
tile, colleague whom his brethren of the
N.S.W. Labor Government sent to America
with £4 per day for “expenses,” appears to
have submitted a sort of. final report. He
states that he attended a dry-farming con-
gress somewhere, but few details have been
published. Still, he remarks that dry-farm-
ing is important because more than one-half
of the earth receives less than -20 inches of
rain per annum. Seeing that much more
than three-fourths of South Australia re-
ceives less than 10 inches per annum, and
most of the balance less than. 20 inches, and
yet S.A. manages to be a big wheat-raiser,
the statement doesn’t look like surprising
news. Nielsen was also instructed (at £4
per day for “expenses”) to inquire about
the possibility of getting farm immigrants
from the U.S. His verdict is that they can
be got if Australia (1) pays two-thirds of
their passage money to an Australian port;
(2) pays all their travelling expenses in
Australia; (3) guarantees that suitable
land in sufficient quantity will be waiting for
them on arrival; (4) guarantees that one-
third of the said land shall be cleared and
fenced. Whether the land is to be a free
gift or is to be oaid for by the dry-farming
immigrant doesn’t clearly appear. . Anyhow,
all this seems a very poor proposition to pay
£4 a day “expenses” for.
A crowd of silly women (there were no
less than 35 signatures to the letter) wrote
to the Sydney daily papers last week clam-
oring to the effect that the alleged increased
cost of living during the last year or two
was due to the tariff duties —which have re-
mained practically unaltered since 1906 save
by the reduction of duties on British goods.
The 35 wild ladies assured the community
that Freetrade would put everything right.
And, curiously enough, in that same issue of
the Telegraph appeared the cabled sum-
mary of a Board of Trade report stating
that in England fl will now only purchase
as much as 16s. 3d. did a few years ago.
Why on earth doesn’t Freetrade keep down
prices in England? Isn’t that country sup-
posed to be a bright and shining example of
Cobdenism in all its glory?

“The Wickedest Wife in Egypt,” a Melo
Donna or Belladrama, or something rather
similar or vaguely thereabouts, was pro-
duced by Julius Knight and his company at
the Criterion last Saturday evening in an
atmosphere that caused a fall of 10 inches
of perspiration on the Sahara in three hours.
Spectacularly, “Bella Donna,” adapted by
.Tames B. Fagan from a novel by Robert
Hichens, is worth seeing, but though the
setting of the last three acts is magically
effective, “the spell and the smell of Egypt”
are fortunately missing. The only smell is
the smell of Turkish coffee, and the spell
does not spell success. For Saturday’s fail-
ure —from a critical standpoint, for the audi-
ence was generous of applause—two persons
were responsible, James B. Fagan, the
adapter of the play, and Miss Irene Browne,
who puts the sugar (of lead) in her hus-
band’s coffee.
» » * *
In extenuation, it may be conceded that
to adapt a play from a popular novel is
difficult; but this adaptation is of an inad-
missible clumsiness. The whole of the first
act is flat and dull. In order to tell the
audience that Dr. Meyer Isaacson is a doc-
tor, Fagan introduces a patient, who has
nothing whatever to do with the plot; and
the act closes with the bare information
that Nigel Armine is going to marry Mrs.
Chepstow, a woman with a variegated and
much-darned past. All this could be easily
indicated in Act Two, at which point the
play should commence. Things certainly
“ BELLA DONNA,” AT SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Ophthalmic histrionics. The evil-eyed hus-
band poisoner (Irene Browne) comes within
range of the penetrating virtuous eyes of Dr.
Julius Knight.
begin to move then. Mrs. Armine, whom
Nigel married to reform, but who married
him only for a title which he didn’t get,
is bored by her dull husband, and has be-
come infatuated with a brown man, Mah-
moud Baroudi, a Sphinx-like personage
whom she rapturouslv embraces. Appar-
ently Baroudi keeps something Solomon-
like in the way of harems, but Mrs. Armine
evidently fatuously believes that on her
appointment to the position of chief concu-
bine, or chief wife, the rest of the ladies will
be sent straight back to their mothers. That,
however, is not the brown man’s, or any
brown man’s, way. In order to save him-
self trouble, Baroudi suggests that Bella
Donna, the name under which the lady had
become notorious, should gradually poison
her husband. The idea appeals to her, and
on the dahabeeya lent them by Baroudi the
poisoning is carried on through a brown ser-
vant formerly belonging to Baroudi. But
the celebrated Jewish physician, Meyer
Isaacson, Nigel’s greatest friend, arrives.
His suspicions are aroused, and in a series
of stirring melodramatic scenes he pits his
will against that of the desperate Lucrezia
Borgia. Isaacson has to fight her, an in-
competent local doctor and Baroudi’s ser-
vant, and in the end administers the knock-
out to them all. Three weeks later, under
Isaacson’s treatment, Nigel is cured, and the
desperate wife defiantly confesses, to her
husband her plot as the only way in which
she can free herself to go to Baroudi. But
this arrangement does not suit the brown
person, whose policy is to remain in the
good graces of the English. Calmly he turns
the white woman down, and goes back to his
harem. And Bella Donna, in a charming even-
ing gown, goes out by the back gate. The
people who have not read the novel are left
under the impression that she went into the
kitchen garden to cull onions or something
for supper, but those who have had
Hichens’ version of it assert that she wan-
dered away into the desert and was bitten
to death by wild camels or undomesticated
hyenas. If so, Fagan should either have in-
dicated in the dialogue in the creaking third
act that the hyenas were waiting for her
under the second palm tree on the right, or
put up a signpost in the garden, with “This
Way to the Desert. Tourists are warned not
to prod the Hyenas ; they are WILD.” Then
the audience could go home comfortably.

Miss Irene Browne, possessing all. the
charm necessary for the part and a highly
satisfactory framework on which to drape
the sort of gowns that bring a heart-felt
“Oh!” of admiring envy from three-fifths
of any audience, lacks the personality for
such an exacting character as Bella Donna.
She cleverly indicates, in the unnecessary
first act, the unstable and voluptuous cha-
racter of Mrs. Chepstow, but in the big
scenes with Isaacson she so over-acts tha.t
the secret which a woman engaged in deli-
berately poisoning her husband must be able
to keep is too preposterously obvious. And
from her first encounter with the doctor she
keys her emotion up to a pitch that leaves
her nothing in reserve for her climax. Thus
her gestures became mechanical, and her
tricks of elocution familiar long before the
end of the third act. Miss Irene Browne
fails, too, to express that variegated and
much-ventilated past of hers. She presents
Bella Donna as a spoilt child. The part is
too big for an actress of her present experi-
ence, despite her present charm. Julius
Knight, finely made up as a Jew, plays the
part of the Hebrew physician with convinc-
ing artistry. Martin Lewis, as the poi-
soned husband, plays this straight part with-
out an error. His dry manner, probably
natural to himself, is well placed in this in-
terpretation of a dull, decent, unsuspicious
husband. As a brown personage, Hugh
Buckler keeps his dramatic powers in
commendable restraint, stressing the inscru-
tableness of this modern Rameses. Hamza,
the accessory to the crime, says “Yes” 38
times with dramatic effect, but Victor
Tatnall, as a brown Cook’s tourist guide,
fails to suggest the smell and the spell of
Egypt. Misses Grace Armitage, Noble and
Dorothy Cumming have little to do, but do
it. But the finest piece of character-acting
in the piece is given by that sure artist,
Leslie Victor, as Ibrahim, the brown ser-
vant of Bella Donna. This degraded figure
will be readily recognised by anyone who
has seen his kind. His grace, his insinuat-
ing tongue, his fawning gestures, are por-
trayed by this fine artist without more than
the necessary suggestion of his hideous
character. It is a triumph. The other
characters require no brick of censure.

The combined picture showmen of Sydney
will join forces to further the hysteria
(which characterises the British people and
their descendants at odd times) aroused
over the Scott catastrophe. Next Satur-
day at the Town Hall they will provide a
special show in aid of the general fund, when
some moving pictures (doubtless taken on
the spot) of Scott and his party at the Polar
base will be shown. Professor David and
Lew. De Groen will assist.

William Anderson’s boy scouts, in odd
sizes, are still holding the fort under Gen-
eral Eugenie Duggan at the Palace (Syd-
ney) against the representatives of law and
order and of villainy, who are working toge-
ther to down virtue and trample on its ear.
Last week The Bulletin expressed itself
as being doubtful that the scout foolery had
been in the original script of Jo Smith’s
“Bush Girl.” As a matter of fact, it was;
and this paper hastens to say so before sus-
picion has time to undermine anybody else’s
security.

Everybody is flocking to see “The Sun-
shine Girl” and her talented and, in many
cases, beautiful associates at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), and Nobody has a complaint to
level at her or them. When Nobody raises
objections to a show the management is as
delighted almost as though it had been
assailed on moral grounds by one of the
churches. Either phenomenon stamps the
production as a success.
The National Amphitheatre is staging a
sort of pocket bellowdrammer hight “The
Counsellor.” It is of U.S. origin and fea-
tures two of Amurika’s best-known stage
products, viz., a Bowery gyurl and a strong
resourceful lawyer. Miss Bessie Slaughter
is back again, with her vast contralto voice
in good working order. De Lea and Orma
continue to pervade the premises to the
great diversion of the crowd, and Schepp’s
Circus is still making good.

“The Kerry Gow” (the programme calls
it “Cow”), now flicking at the Crystal Pal-
ace. is identical with “Sweet County Kerry,’’
at the Sydney Royal. At the Royal if you
want to dodge Allen Doone’s songs it is
necessary to go outside, and then you may
miss something. At the Crystal Palace
there are no songs to dodge. So the film
gets the verdict from that great jury which
prefers a quiet life and wants to get the
thing through under the hour. There are,
of course, other things at the Palace —“topi-
cals” and “comedies” and the usual little
love affairs ; but the Cow from Kerry is the
pick of this week’s herd.

To date George Cross, at the Adelphi, has
swallowed five gallons of poison, yet, on ap-
pearances, is still able to take other nour-

been sentenced to 375 years’ hard; and Nel-

The first Australian-made kinemacolpr
films were shown at Spencer’s Lyceum this
week. They comprise scenes in and around
Melbourne,' including the scurrying crowds
at the railway stations, with other views of
the Botanic Gardens, Albert Park and St.
Kilda, the last mentioned showing a receht
regatta. The films are realistic; and it is
only a matter of time when the railways
will complain of lost custom. The Cowes
Regatta and “From Carnarvon to Conway
Castle” were also shown in color. “Aurora
Floyd,” the chief item on the. general list,
was a flickerised version of Miss Braddon’s
yarn of the rich banker’s daughter who
eloped with the groom, and was, of course,
unhappy ever after. The thing was done
quite as well as it deserved to be.

After a highly successful season at the
Palace (Sydney), Allen Doone and his assits
(which, curiously enough, include his fet-
ing abilities, to say nothing of his singing)
moved up town to the Royal, onenin" a
season tlmre on Saturday night with “Sweet
County Kerry.” This is the third time the
piece has been put on in Sydney within a
brief period of time by Doone (once at the
Adelphi, once at the Palace, and hoW at the
Royal). It looks much better on the Royal
stage than it did on that of the Palace, and
the farm scene in the first act on Saturday
was a good and artistic piece of work. Since
the original production there have been
some changes in the cast, but there is little,
difference in the acting, or little acting in
the difference; if there is, it cannot be dis-
cerned with the nude eye. Doone sings
again. Apparently nothing will stop him
but death, and then the fellow who. wrote,
“Oh, grave where is thy victory?” will have
a light break in upon him.

and Miss Billie Barlow will bash the up-
roarious tambourine, and Miss Blanche
Browne and Miss Jessie Lonnen sound or

“As You Like It,” as we don’t like it,
showing a fat Rosalind with a puckered
chin, transpired at West’s, Melbourne, last
week, and received the cold Antarctic eye.
Shakspeare is not too suitable for biograph
production, and the presentation of one of
his classics with a waving arm or a rolling
eye trying to convey the sense of the spoken
word, ought to be near enough to irrever-
ence to count for sacrilege in the eyes of the
numerous adorers of the Bard. A pleasant
subject, however, was “What Happened to
Mary,” apparently an instalment of a serial
story wherein Mary, a stage aspirant, tries
to arrive at affluence with strict regard for
her virtue and uneasy avoidance of a liber-
tine’s kiss. An instructive picture also was
the film displaying the amputation of the
Panama Isthmus —an operation involving
much machinery, cuss words and perspira-
tion.

An astounding medley of Indians, cow-
boys and matrimonial results the latest
Andersonian drama at the King’s (Melb.).
It’s title, "The Queen of the Highway,” re-
fers to a lady clothed in Turpinesque gar-
ments, who appears to be abiding in the
neighborhood of the Wild Horse Tavern,
Western States of Amurka, together with a
gold-mine promoter or wild-catter, who stole
her from her husband 15 years previously.
At the time of her elopement, she deposited
her infant daughter with the landlady of
the pub aforesaid, but the circumstance
faded from her memory, and now she is sur-
prised to learn that Jess the Prairie Flower
is her own lawful offspring. Incidentally,
she makes red-hot love to Bob, the 1 ony
Postman, a virtuous young man who has
won Jess’s virgin heart, but, as a rule, she is
busy robbing the mail coach. To the Wild
Ilorse Tavern comes the long-left husband
of Bell Diamond (the Matilda Turpin in
question), for the purpose of running the
mail-coach robbers to earth. This gentle-
man was a baronet when the lady bolted
from him. Nowadays, he is merely Roy
Redgrave, dressed somewhat like an eques-
trian pierrot, and known to fame as Ihe
Dude Detective. He shouts drinks for the
cowboys whilst planning detections, and
in the course of concealing himself, he dis-
covers the identity of his wife and child.
Finally, he ambushes in the mail coach with
a brother sleuthhound of the law, who shoots
the fair Turpinette next time she holds up
the vehicle. Also, the same handy-man de-
stroys the paramor, # and all peace.
Apart from the professional duties and
private family affairs of the Dude Detective,
a lot of awful bellow-dramatic interest at-
taches to Bob, the Pony Postman, and a
jealous half-bred spinster, who wears wig-
wam skirts and sometimes wishes to murde
Bob’s best girl. Also, there is a full-bred
Indian with a criminal weakness for “ re ‘
water, and a dismal Jew pedlar, and others.
Two of the others —Helen Fergus and
Charles Taylor—impersonate a pair of orth-
odox Western characters in an easy, natural
way, wfflereas all the rest are creatures o
absurdity, Dalgleish, as the postman, being
the least’ incredible. Half a dozen scenes
are a credit to Rege Robins and the mail
coach and equestrian parts of the
ance have never been more effectively man
aged outside of a circus. In point of horse-
manship. “The Queen of the Highway is a
commendable show. As regards mechanical
construction and grace of form, it compares
unfavorably with the common, or boarding
house, sausage. __ #

Half an hour has been cut off the first-
night prolongation of “A Midsummer Night’s
Dream,” but the waits between the acts
and scenes continue to be gloomy features of
an otherwise bright entertainment. Oscar
Asche’s prodigal display intervals is
widely commented upon. “Kismet,’ An-
tony and Cleopatra,” “The Shrew” and now
the “Dream” have tended to get the public
mind obsessed bv a sense of impending cur-
tain at the Royal, and in this case the ‘rag
seems to be the one and only subject of sus-
tained interest in the performance. Nothing
else hangs together, as it were. The im-
mortality of “A.M.N.D.” lies in its com-
plexity, of course. Nobody wants his Grace
the Duke of Athens and his Court followers
to be less unreal, or the alleged Athenian
boobs to be less Elizabethanly English, _or
the Fairies to give a reasonable explanation
of themselves on supernatural grounds. In
this democratic age it could be wished that
the fairies had come tripping to Athens for
some larger cause than the nuptials ot a
Dook, but their perfervid loyalty can be
overlooked. If they were less frivolous, they
would be less convincing, perlmps. Under
present conditions, they are quite convinc-
ing—thanks to Shakspeare —which is more
than one can say for the prehistoric asss
head that befalls Oscar Asche in the third
act.

AT POVERTY POINT.
Sarah Bernhardt’s accumulated years seem to
have hit her suddenly in America, like those of the
f rozen Pirate that C. Russell wrote about. She
knocked up completely in Chicago, and (the pathetic
farce of it all!) apologies for the performance on
account of ill-health were made on two occasions.
The promoters of the tour, who disgorged heavily in
advance, were looking mighty sick at latest accounts.
“Hullo” : In “Bella Donna,” Turkish coffee is made
on the stage. The present scribe has seen and drank
Turkish coffee made in the bazaars of Constantinople
and Smyrna, and it was not made that way. Yet Mah-
moud Barondi was looking on and seemed satisfied.
In Turkey each cup is boiled in a separate pot; in
“Bella Donna” one pot serves for three cups. On
the stage the roasting of the beans —the most neces-
sary part of the process —is not shown at all. And
in Turkey each cup has a thick layer of coffee
grounds, which, of course, is not swallowed. In
“Bella Donna” the grounds are not poured into the
cup. Mahmoud Baroudi evidently didn’t know
everything, though, perhaps, that is the way Turkish
coffee is made in Egypt.

“Circle”: Leslie Victor’s clever character-sketch of
Ibrahim, the coy house nigger who fawns round
“Bella Donna” and the Robert Hichens play, at
Sydney Criterion, makes it clear to me why the
white man has never got out of the habit of kicking
niggers. Ibrahim droops and shuffles round the
poisonous premises with a rose depending from his
mouth, and his discolored head tipped at a languish-
ing angle. His sticky drawl, “Everything my lady
wants she must ha-ve!” would infuriate an elderly,
self-respecting sheep. I guess that a nigger ot the
Ibrahim brand, on being violently kicked from be-
hind, would immediately fall on his knees and
gratefully lick some of the polish off the kicker s
footwear. Still, one needs to be quite sure ot his
nigger before one has a shot for goal. . One ot the
most shaken men I ever met was a frail little Eng-
lishman who had, years before, gone out to repre-
sent the dominant Power in India in the calico line.
One day he reckoned the time was ripe to boost the
dignity of the Empire by booting a too-clamorous
nigger. He booted with a will; but, unfortunately,
the concussed turban happened to be one of the few
unkickable types. He was an Afghan or something
of the sort, and you might as well kick a boa-
constrictor. The Englishman was rescued in time by
the police; but what he had already received made
a twisted man of him for the rest of his life. But
I guess Ibrahim would be a safe target.
“Way Verley Penn”: Mabel Hardinge, bossess of
a dramatic co. at present perpetrating ‘‘East Lynne
and other atrocities at the Adelaide Tiv., gave her
own treasury a knock one recent evening by deciding
upon “No show to-night.” Seems that, a few
minutes beforfe the curtain was due to go up, one or
two ac tors demanded cash in advance, or we won t
play to-night.” The treasury was quite sound, but
the hold-up was too much for the raven-headed
manageress; so she promptly closed down, and next
day filled the places with other people.
“Melb.”: Concerning Rignold’s “Midsummer

“S.S." : De Biere, in a chat with the Argus on
the art of conjuring, showed himself as expert as a
prominent politician in the kindred art of talking
without telling. What De Biere did not tell was the
fact that the art and mystery of his business are
based on the capacity of transfer attention. If you want
to do something with your right hand, inveigle
public interest to your left. Fool the crowd into
believing your tricks are depending on all sorts of
appliances and means but the very ones that are
necessarv; trick them with fakements and shapes
that really don’t belong; bother them with boxes
and bits of string that are not in any sense the
essence of the mystery. The quickness of the hand
deceives the eye, but does not do it half as well as
the glibness of the tongue. An illusionist must set
his audience thinking—but thinking the wrong
thing.
Olive Berkley, remembered in Melbourne as a
good little Lord Fauntleroy over 20 years ago, has
apparently concluded the theatrical career which she
had fitfuilv pursued in America until she was biffed
bv Adversity and counted out. According to a New
York journal, she was recently in trouble in connec-
tion with a few trifling trinkets, allegedly taken
from a local department store. “When arrested
she said she had slept in Bryant Park for three
nights, and had been without food for three days.
And, having no stockings worth mentioning, she
had painted her legs with blacking—a shadow, so
to speak, of the vanity of the profession of which
she was a plump, pert ornament in the long ago.
Apropos the elevation of the music-hall. Recently
two New York gunmen were sentenced to be electro-
cuted for the murder of a notorious Hebrew person,
and the Judge had hardly committed the pair for the
fatal shock before William Hammerstein was offering
their wives 250 dollars a week each for a vaudeville
stunt. He reckoned that the pronounced homicidal
tendencies of the husbands gave the ladies some title
to public favor in ’Murka, so he waved his roll of
bills in their faces and expected them to close. The
females, who, by the way, are known as “Mrs. Lefty
Louie” and “Mrs. Gyp the Blood,” said it was
“really too much.” and indignantly refused. What
they meant, of course, was that Mr. Hammerstein s
offer was really too little; they couldn’t let their
feelings be lacerated for less than 500 dollars a week
each. When the last mail left Hammerstein was
considering their suggestion.
“J.F.H.” : Negro minstrelsy has been revived on a
colossal' scale at the London Palladium. There
are no less than 120 burnt-corked coons in the first
part. Twelve corner-men —bones and tambourines —-
address the old-time conundrums to the “interlocu-
tor” ; and 40 banjoists ping-pang like 750 hives of
infuriated bees. The oldest member of the com-
pany is “Little Thomas,” a corner-man with a tam-
bourine. He is 75, and he joined his first minstrel
troupe in London 60 years ago. For 27 years he
was with the Mohawks at the Agricultural Hall ;
then he joined the Moore and Burgess Minstrels, at
the now-vanished St. James’s Hall, and was with the
combination until it finally disbanded.
“Tony”: Despite the dictum of those who persist
that the picture-show will see eternity in and out,
1 assert that its day is waning already. A visit to
any Melbourne picture-house will bear this assertion
out. The audiences are small and cold, and often
obviously bored. The struggle to warm them up
with new matter is evidenced in strained efforts
after effect. Industrial and films still attract
attention, but the glassy optic is turned on the
eccentricities of Asshead, Tweedlefool and others,
while the cowboy drama barely gets a hand. One
show invites custom by giving its patrons a pot of
tea; so far beer has not arrived, but the time is
near when it will be needed to lure the jaded citizen.
And beer mav do the trick ; for beer will take most
people anywhere —especially if it looks like free
beer.

State Minister Trefle of N.S.W. got a
peal of thunder off his chest last week when
he declared that sheep were developing into
a curse, and were keeping Australia empty
of people. It is a wonder he didn t class
sheep and wheat as the twin curses of the
country, and recommend that they be both
obliterated on the ground that they take up
too much space. When Trefle has got his
second wind, it may perhaps dawn on him
that it certainly isn’t because of sheep that
there are no big city populations alongside
Port Stephens, Jervis Bay and Twofold
Bay. Asses are the cause of the trouble.

A grievance of chorus girls and others, which
unionism is expected to wipe out when it has secured
a little firmer footing in the profession, is the pre-
sent necessity of wearing extremely insanitary and
disagreeable costumes, which become “painfully evi-
dent” to the wearers and those associated with them
towards the end of a long run. —Daily paper.
These intimate complaints arouse
Great sadness in the Johnnie’s breast.
We’re not a class inclined to Wowse,
But such a state as this allows
Occasion for the fierce request
That Totties in the singing rows
Be given frequent change of clo’es.
That pretty little girl in blue
We’ve oft declared to be a dream,
She dances with the chosen few,
And looks “too sweet.” Alas! how true
Things are not always what they seem !
The lightsome fairy and the fay
Are short a weekly washing day.
VICTORIA.
STALLS.
Exit Becke.
A once-brilliant writer passed out when
Ivouis Becke died at a Sydney hotel last
Tuesday morning. He was not an old man
in years, being only 55 (one biographical
volume made his age about 10 years
more, but that seems very improba-
ble), but by reason of prolonged ill-
health he looked Very old and weary
long before his exit. His birthplace
was the peaceful town of Port Macquarie,
one of the old, dead places of New South
Wales, but he left it early, and never ex-
pressed any great desire to go back. It was
among the Pacific islands, where he served
variously as trader, pilot and Kanaka re-
cruiter, that he accidentally found his real bus-
iness in life. Or, rather, it was found for him,
for though he returned loaded to the gun-
wale with tales and legends of brown islands,
and brown girls, and brown sins and brown
gods, it had not yet struck him to put them
on paper. It was at the invitation of J. h.
Archibald, then editor of this paper, that he
besran to blast his reminiscences out of him-
seif—at first slowly and painfully, but after-
wards with ever increasing facility. Becke
was a born writer, but, curiously enough, he
lmd no small chance of going to the end ot
his earthly pilgrimage without finding it out-
lie began his public career with the Poet
and Palm” series in The Bulletin. \ery
earlv he went to England, where he rushed
out * many books, ranging from works ot
genius to pot-boilers, but though he made
much money, very little of it stuck to .him.
Coin departed from him casually, and the
exact track it had taken he seldom remem-
bered. When he returned to Sydney a year
or two ago, he was in poor circumstances
and miserably broken in health, and ill his
latter days very little good luck came his
way, or in the way of his wife and two chil-
dren. In his strenuous period his _ favorite
recreation was whale-fishing. It isn.t the
kind of sport that can be met with every
dav, but Becke whaled as opportunity
offered, and no man can whale much oftener
than that. _

THE TEMPTATION OF ST. ANTHONY.
NEW YORK, Saturday.-—Rev. W. H. Selleck, of
North Yakima (Washington) objected to seeing in
shop windows corsets mounted on what he called
voluptuous female forms, these being the dummy
wooden busts generally used. Mayor Splawn said
that the clergyman should not let a mere wooden
form excite him so.
Melbourne Age has burst out with some
time-worn suggestions for making the best
of the city’s tramway system in its three
remaining years of life. The marvel has
ever been that these suggestions—offered in
The Bulletin long ago —were not adopted
as a matter of course. Everyone knows
that the cables of the Melbourne Tramway
Co. could have been profitably worked at
greater speed, but the question of increased
power is not worth discussing now that the
co.’s lease is nearly run out. Ways and
means of saving time, and getting more
journeys out of each car under present cable
conditions, are still worth considering. Cars
on all the lines stop at the bidding of any
individual at any point of the route save a
steep incline or decline. There are no “sta-
tions” on the line, but at every intersection
the car stops twice —before and after it has
crossed the other line —and stops, as a rule,
an absurdly long time. “No haste and less
speed” has been the motto of the M.M.T.
Co. from the beginning. The cars are run
on the go-slow-and-stop-often principle for
the convenience of old women who can’t be
bustled. Apparently the management is
composed of old women who can’t be
bustled.
On Saturday, last week, the postal and
money-order service of Melbourne was par-
tially suspended in order that a small min-
ority of the postal employees might go pic-
nicking down the Bay with a number of
non-employees who also took the “day off.”
The usual speeches by the usual officers of
the Department were reported in the same
old press, and the public said “D—n!” as
of yore. Also, a host of employees who
couldn’t go to the invaluable picnic and
wouldn’t have wanted to go if they could,
were bitterly satirical on the subject-of Jhis
annual foolishness. The authorities and the
Lord only know why post offices should be
closed, and an ordinary delivery of letters
be denied to the public on this particular
Saturday. Railway employees have their
periodical excursions, but the train service
is not stopped to make a station-master’s
holiday. If the Government supplied the
Melbourne Metropolitan Gas service, as it
should, the community would not have to
go gasless for a time on account of a de-
partmental function. It may be argued that
the G.P.O. issues warnings to letter-posters,
whereby those who read the warnings can
avoid possible calamities that may befall
the unwarned, but everybody, without ex-
ception, is threatened with the chance of
having to suffer in mind or estate from the
unpardonable picnic.
It isn’t drink that makes the N.S.W.
Ministry look like that, but the struggle to
decide what to do with Federal Government
House. The Ministry was pledged to abol-
ish the State Governor, and it wasn’t
pledged to do anything at all to the Federal
Governor. With its amazing capacity for
doing anything except what it has promised
it proceeded, of course, to get into a condi-
tion of wild applause over the State Gov-
ernor and to eject the Federal one. And
now it wants to show that it threw out the
all-Australian Excellency, not because of a
mean desire to be nasty to the Common-
wealth, but because his premises were
wanted for a high public purpose. Already
about 25 public purposes have been sug-
gested, ranging from a monkey-house or
something of the sort to a conservatorium
of music, but none of them look quite high
enough to be feasible. Also, the Cabinet
can’t agree within itself over the monkey-
house or anything else. Probably the best
idea would be t© pull down the house on the
ground that it is aged and out of date, and
add the gardens to the Domain. Then a
great load of building material would be off
the Ministerial chest, and the argument
would have to end for lack of any place to
argue about.

A cheerful casual, irrelevant person, who
transpired lately at the North Melbourne
Court, indirectly poured mud on Willie
Watt’s announced intention or threat' or
promise to introduce a Bill that would put
the charities of the State on an amalgamated
and financially sane basis. He came to
court at the request of his wife, who desired
him to give her something to live on, and
genially explained that he wasn’t able to do
hard toil, but was just then engaged in
“running what was called the Relief Mis-
sion.” He was the secretary, and “had col-
lected about £3 last week.” Inquiry about
this precious mission elicited the statement
that defendant’s wife “was one of the com-
mittee, but did not attend regularly; in fact,
no meeting had been held yet.” Of course,
there were no books, vouchers or anything
of that sort, and a cold, sceptical P.M.
ordered the blessed secretary to hand some
relief to his wife or go to chokee.
“Henry H.” writes :
The final verdict upon the Scott expedition to the
Antarctic will probably be that it was a muddle.
Scott was brave and in deadly earnest, but there is
no evidence that he was specially fitted to stand the
Polar temperature. Of the two men whose break-
down delayed the expedition on its return from the
Pole, and led to his complete obliteration, one was a
naval seaman with a Welsh name, and as his Majesty’s
bloated ironclads seldom go to the Pole, he probably
had little experience of the conditions. The other
was a captain of the Inniskilling Dragoons. The
dragoons are not addicted to charging the bear in
Greenland, so his qualifications are also doubtful.
His epitaph set forth quite truly that he was a
gallant English gentleman, but if he had been a
Siberian fisherman or a Russian whaler from Arch-
angel—-well, he might have seemed less picturesque,
but he might have struggled through, and the great
thing is to struggle through. Apropos, almost the
only man in the outfit who doesn’t get special bou-
quets thrown at him for his courage, and who isn’t
called “captain” or “doctor” or even “Mr.,” is
Dimitri, the Russian dog-driver. Yet it is men-
tioned quite casually that, when the biologist and
the dog-driver reached One-ton Depot (it was 11
miles from here that the Polar party perished) the
Russian was quite willing to go sledging into the
blizzard and the unknown single-handed, and carry
provisions to the returning wanderers, if by any
chance he could find them. Possibly he didn’t know
that he was proposing anything very special, but
to the ordinary person his idea looks marvellous. It
was people like this obscure doggist to whom Amund-
sen owed his success, and it was probably to brilliant
and gentlemanly amateurs that Scott owed his
death. The episode vaguely recalls that of the Burke
and Wills expedition across Australia. Its leader
was an imposing Irish personage who had seen ser-
vice in the Austrian cavalry, and afterwards became
a police official in Melbourne. His cavalry experi-
ence didn’t help him to find kangaroos to eat, and
his police experience didn’t furnish him with a clue
in the search for water, and his city experience was
wasted among the immeasurable distances. So,
one way and another, the show came to grief.
Count de Fleurieu, who was in Australia
a year ago, to look for traces of old French
navigators, has “made good” with the S.A.
Government in one matter of little conse-
quence. Just a century ago Baudin (just
too late) and Flinders (just in time) met
at Encounter Bay; and the name commem-
orates the meeting. The Frenchman, as he
THE. OBVIOUS.
FOOLISH G LADYS (ringing up): “Is that you, Evelyn? I’m speaking through the telephone !”
strolled along the coast, had given names to
most things; Fleurieu, whose ancestor was
Minister of Marine at the time, has traced
about 250 of them. Many of the features
have struggled along since without any offi-
cial name at all; and Fleurieu asked that in
such cases the French labels might be used.
The Government, reckoning up that it would
cost nothing, smiled sweetly and agreed.
The consent applies in eight or nine
cases, the most important being that
of the peninsula which stretches out below
Adelaide towards Kangaroo Island and ter-
minates at Cape Jervis. S. Aus. never real-
ised that this solid slab of territory, was a
neck of land almost surrounded by water;
but the Frenchman can have his way, and
henceforth it is to be Fleurieu Peninsula.
♦ * * *
Minister Thomas’s weakness in the case of
Mrs. Hop Poon Gooey, who, by the way,
has not yet, as far as is known, returned to
her beloved Flowery Land, has naturally
brought him a crop of similar applications
or disasters. The latest concerns the two
small daughters of a Chinkie wearing the
shocking name of O’Hoy, of Hongkong and
Bendigo. O’Hoy, it appears, was born in
Bendigo, and is, of course, entitled to dwell
here if so minded; but he went to China,
captured a “responsible position” in a ship-
ping office, married an almond-eyed damsel,
and presently acquired two replicas of him-
self and his wife. Last year, O’Hoy seems
to have discovered an idea that the climate
of Bendigo would suit his children’s health,
so he sent them thither—they being then two
and three years of age respectively—in the
care of an aunt. There doesn’t appear any
visible reason why they should have been
allowed to land at all, but they were, on an
exemption of three months. Before that
time was up, the usual interesting event
happened to the aunt —these Chinkie ladies
seem to be whales for interesting events —•
and a further exemption was granted. Now
the Department wants the three —who are
now four —to return whence they came, but
O’Hoy has announced his intention of com-
ing to dwell here permanently, and wants to
know if he is to be separated from his be-
loved children —whom he hasn’t seen for
nearly the half of their lives. Also, he
wants an exemption granted to his wife
with the usual interesting event to folloAV,
presumably. The time has arrived, or is
ripe, or something, for Minister Thomas to
brace up his backbone, stimulate his White
Australian principles, fan his zeal into flame
and say “No” with such emphasis that not
even to the subtlety of an Asiatic mind can
there be found one trace of “Yes” in the
larsre negation.
Another strike is threatened in Maoriland
■ —in fact, it may be in progress at this
moment for all this paper knows to the eon
trary. The Christchurch General Laborers’
Union is responsible. Two lads having been
fined for refusing to attend drill, that union
passed an ordinance urging upon citizens
“to do all in their power to lessen the birth
rate until the Defence Act is repealed.” On
the whole, The Bulletin is inclined to
agree with the Christchurch General Lab-
orers’ Union —anyway, to the extent that
its members should practise the principles
of St. Anthony. The man who won’t under-
take the defence of the country, and is pre-
pared to use what influence he has to keep
other people from learning how to defend
the country, shouldn’t have any children.
The Koreans were men of that kind, and it
would have been better for a whole lot of
young Koreans, by reason of their awful
fathers, if they had never been born. But
as a guarantee that they believe in what
they say, mightn’t something more than a
mere resolution be asked of the members of
the Christchurch Laoorers’ Union?

of parties.

THE SUFFERING SEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As was anticipated by the departmental officers,
the shipment of bananas from Fiji by the Levuka
proved on its arrival on Friday night to be unfit for
human consumption, and 19,000 bunches and 8000
cases, representing another 16,000 bunches, were
thrown overboard in the bay. —One news item.
The passengers by G.M.S. Scharnhorst, which ar-
rived in Sydney yesterday from Bremen and Ant-
werp, via Southampton, had a unique experience
whilst crossing the Indian Ocean. The Scharnhorst
had crossed the line, and was about half-way to
Colombo when she ran into a submarine disturbance,
which caused her to quiver from stem to stern.
-—Another news item.
The Sea, white-handed, to her washing jobs
Rolls on, and like a washerlady sobs
Her old, old sorrows to the sun and stars,
And falls like thunder on the sandy bars.
Maudlin and grumpy, with the Wind’s wide broom
She scours the cliff-face and each rock-bound room,
Or spreads her suds upon the tilted sands,
And croons old ditties no one understands.
When grey and drunken with the Winter’s gin,
She rages hag-like till some ships fall in
And swell the dreadful litter on the floor,
So like the washerlady’s home ashore.
They heave decayed bananas at the sea;
With ancient powder, too, they’re very free.
She takes it all because she has no choice;
But you can trace resentment in her voice.
What are the wild waves saying? Why, the Flood
Complains about your rotten fruit and mud !
About the ashes that the liner leaves
The old dame too continually grieves.
She shudders sometimes in her ancient bed,
And shakes the vessels passing overhead.
Not one fleet Second passes by but we
Pour some sad rubbish in the suffering Sea !
V.S.W.

“Quipster” : In spite of Bakespeare, there s
a world of force in a name. The Indian
adepts chant the sacred word “om,” and its
vibratory power is immense.. In “Toorak,
too, quite a world of vigor is probably hid-
den, and awaits discovery by vibratory
analysis, whatever that may mean. I
shouldn’t wonder if a man couldn’t cure him-
self of paralysis by properly intoning it.
Toorak! Notice how suddenly it termi-
nates, suggesting the crack of the horsewhip-
power again. Oh, it’s a magical name.
Even magistrates are influenced tay it, as in
a recent case at Prahran (Vic.). A chim-
ney-sweep named Winter sued a Captain
Somepumpkins for 255. for work and labor
done, the work including tlie “curing” of
smok .Y Hues. To anyone who knows the
arin( >yance of smoky chimneys, the price
won se em exorbitant. The captain, more-
over, admitted that he agreed to pay the
price, but lie defended the claim because tlie
sweep took advantage of his inexpert know-
ledge. The work occupied only two hours,
and the idea of paying a mere chimney-
sweep 255. for two hours’ work was mon-
strous. The captain might pay a surgeon
50_ guineas for half-an-hour’s work—one
guinea for the work and 49 for knowing how
to do it—but that is a different matter alto-
gether. And, anyway, Captain Somepump-
kins lives in Toorak ; and the blessed Gon-
stitution would peter out if Collingwood
were permitted to triumph over Toorak. So
the Court said that 10s. was a fair tiling ;
and Toorak breathes heavily again. Still!
next time Toorak talks about the shameful
way the laboring classes dawdle at their
work, somebody ought to be on hand to
remind it of the evil consequences of hurry-

* * * »
Miss Adela Curtis, the latest caterer for
wealthy cranks in London, has the audacity
to palm off the ancient custom of the Mahat-
mas as a new medical religion of her own.
The Mahatmas sit all day brooding on Vir-
tue for the immediate salvation of them-
selves and the gradual regeneration of man-
kind in general. The cult of Miss
Curtis is the “Silence Cure,”
whereby the patients are supposed
to fill their minds with good
thoughts, and thus banish diseases
from their bodies. The initial
weakness of the theory is in the
assumption that a patient who
tries to think of “charity” or “be-
nevolence” for 15 consecutive silent
minutes is bound to acquire a de-
sire to practise what he tried
to think of. Churchgoers can
hardly avoid a measure of silent
meditation on Christian endeavor-
ing, but their endeavoring in 99
cases out of 100 doesn’t produce
two pen’orth of Christianity. Fifty
average churchgoers are less kindly
in disposition and less healthy in
physique than 50 godless citizens
who devote their Day of Rest to
gardening, mixed bathing and
swapping yarns with their friends.
The Church, considered as an in-
ducement to silent curative medita-
tion. is an admitted failure. It
benefits only the fractional propor-
tion of worshippers who crave for
religion as boozers crave for alco-
hol. In the others it makes no real
difference unless it makes them
dangerous hypocrites. What the
old Church has failed to do no
modern Meditation Cult will ac-
complish. The Church does get a
fair chance to put in some go- <
work, inasmuch as most people are driven,
coaxed or attracted to Divine Service at
one time or other in their lives. But
silence-curing, like faith-healing, interests
only the spiritual-minded and crankily dis-
posed minority, even as a temperance lec-
ture interests teetotallers only.
Melbourne City Council, although a slug-
gard for useful work, moves a lot faster in
ornamental work than the State Govern-
ment. anyhow. The demolition of the awful
old Immigrants’ Home, adjoining the Alex-
andra Gardens, off St. Kilda-road, which
had been delayed for years by the Public
Works Department will be no sooner com-
pleted than the City Council will have gar-
deners on the job of beautifying this new
reserve. Where the old Home lagged super-
fluous the Council will erect an artistic
superfluity in the form of a statue of Ed.
VII. The City Fathers can rush things
when they like. But the State Govern-
ment! Many months ago Premier Watt an-
nounced that the stolen corner-piece of the
South Yarra Domain had been restored to
the public, but up to now it has remained as
private as ever. The boundary fences and
sub-divisional fences are still there. Not a
move has been made towards giving back
this large tract of original park land to its
rightful owners. Horses and cattle are
grazed on the land, festive gatherings are
held within its gates, and the vast enclosure
is practically the private estate of a care-
taker.
* * * *
The narrowness of Chapel-street and the
impossibility of cramming more wheels into
it, is worrying the councillors of 1 ran
(Vic.). One genius has propounded an idea
remindful of topsy-turvey Chester (Eng-
land). Chester has its shop windows on the
first floor, and the sidewalks are on the roots
of ground-floor premises. The proposal in
P’ran is to reverse the order of things and
shift the shop fronts back 10 feet, thus allow-
ing the first floors to project over the street
as they did in the Cold Country in the times
of the Saxon Heptarchy and later. The
weak spot about the notion is that, while it
roofs in the pedestrian Public during wet
weather, it takes no account of ngbt,*]
air and safety from fire. Also, there is the
chance of the projecting upper storey col-
lapsing on to the pavement below, and rais-
ing Cain.

The other evening Sydney Sun printed
this telegram:—
Mrs. Scott, who is on board the R.M.S. Aorangi
bound for Maoriland, has received a wireless message
conveying news of her husband’s death.
But that wouldn’t do for a community which
had been worked up to hysterics in the in-
terests of a great circulation; so the Even-
ing News presented the thing in this form :
Mrs. Scott has now learned of her bereavement.
The wireless operators, after many attempts, suc-
ceeded in getting in touch with the Aorangi, on
which Mrs. Scott, unconscious of disaster, was speed-
ing to Maoriland to meet her husband.
Through the air the message quivered, and over-
took the vessel.
The dread words were rapidly deciphered, and the
news conveyed to Mrs. Scott.
The quivering message that overtook the
vessel and the dread words that were rapidly
deciphered are all quite Marie Corellish, not
to say Charles Garbage. But what an un-
healthy idea it is that this sort of heated
and bounding slop is what the community
is craving after. #
“0.K.” writes from a common-sense point
of view:—
The pathos of ill-fated Captain Scott’s farewell
letter to the British public is in its egotistical appeal
to egotism. He and his companions were meeting
their death, he wrote, in the attempt to complete a
task “which has shown that Englishmen can endure
hardships, help one another, and meet death with as
great a fortitude as ever in the past. ...Hau we
lived, I should have, had a tale to tell which would
have stirred the heart of every Englishman. . . .but
surely, surely a great rich country like ours will see
that those who are dependent on us are provided for
properly.” A fair claim, forcibly expressed, from
the point of view of every individual egotist. A
Polar explorer, fully conscious of the dramatic
heroism of his position, turns the limelight to his
death scene. He demands proper provision for the
dependent ones because they were dependent on Ins
conspicuously plucky self and equally plucky com-
panions. Every man of some importance personally
thinks that his nearest and dearest are entitled to
special consideration on his account after his de-
cease. They must be properly provided for. Ihe
millions of persons of no financial importance don t
hold this belief, or only in a very modified form, dur-
ing their life or when they are passing out. Society
has not allowed them to flatter themselves so much.
The great and wealthy country which poor Scott was
able to address in the mass, is mostly populated bv
strugglers and starvers, whose sufferings -heroic
sufferings in many cases —are commonplace occur-
rences to which Wealth and Influence pay no heed.
Even the obscure people who get into the news-
papers after death by having sacrificed their - lives m
the attempt to save life, rarely leave their families
“properly provided for” by public subscription Or
else it is that the public considers a very small sum
of money a very proper provision for a commonplace
widow and five commonplace children. For the toil-
ing millions in the mass there is no egotistical hope
that anything they can do will ever be worth any-
thing worth mentioning to their families. Ihey die
in battle on land or sea, and nobody notices that the
widow’s pension—if she gets one—is a miserable
pittance In short, the life and death of the average
impecunious Englishman is pathetic, because he ex-
pects nothing from his “great and rich country, and
[s made to feel the unimportance of his fate, whereas
the pathos of Captain Scott’s letter to the nation
is in its admission of his sense of his fate s im-
portance. .
From The Bul-
letin’s Old Sol-
dier :
The Balkan allies
should begin to feel
the pinch badly from
now onwards. So far,
those in high places
have concealed the
real losses from the
general public. They
must have been tre-
mendous. For ex-
ample, in the case of
the Bulgarians, they
may be set down at
anything up to a third
of their total force
(say) 130,000 men,
killed, wounded, and
dead of disease. In
the heat of battle the
full weight of the
tragedy is not felt.
It is when the units
come to be reorgan-
ised that the extent
of the horror is
brought home to all
hands. It is some-
times an advantage
for a general to com-
mand an army of red
raw recruits, who have
only learned their
marching drill. Know-
ing nothing of the
war game, they will
advance in the face of
a deadly fire till half
of them are down,
and then, if there is
an officer still on his
legs to lead them,
the}' will continue
rushing forward till
those who are not shot
fall exhausted. The
demoralising result is
only seen when the
fragments are gath-
ered up next day.
The reducing of Ad-
rianople—to which the
Allies are pledged—-
is a stiff proposi-
tion. No doubt the
ring of forts, thrown
up by Blum Pasha in 1877, is too close to the
town to protect it from bombardment, but no amount
of damage done to the town will do much good or
harm to the men in the bomtr-proof shelters under
the ramparts of the forts. Firing on the town hurts
no one but the civil population, and everyone of
them who is killed will be simply one useless mouth
the less to feed, and one more daily ration for the
soldiers. Since the best chance the Allies have of
entering Adrianople is by starving its defenders, their
bombarding tactics are about the worst possible.
Meanwhile, with Indian, and possibly African, Mo-
hammedans subscribing to Turkish loans, and some
of them volunteering for service, the Unspeakable
One is looking a good deal cheerier than he was last
month.
“Montalex” supplies a millinery note: —
That “dejected kangaroo” item (B. 30/l/’l3) encour-
ages me unto disrespectful remarks about the Com-
monwealth Flag. In the first place, a disastrous
chromatic effect is produced by breaking-up the red
field with so many little scraps of white (stars).
Also, if the stars of the Southern Cross had been
arranged decoratively— i.e., straightened up so as
to form a perfect cross —the big white star under the
union might be tolerated as part of the decorative
scheme. But, since the cross is pictorially pre-
sented in all its askewness, the aforesaid big star,
not being in the actual celestial picture, has no right-
ful place in the composition. I notice that the Com-
monwealth device appears occasionally now upon a
blue ensign, but, though this gives a vastly better
color-effect, it is unfortunately not the official Com-
monwealth ensign.

ELECTRIFIED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Nikola Tesla recently startled the New York
Board of Education with a proposal to make dull
children bright by saturating them with eleetr’Vitv.
—News item.
The lightnings of the gods are oiled and curled,
The thunders tamed and taught to be polite
\\e send them on their errands round the world
Linking the dawn to dark, the day to night.
Thor’s loud electric hammer mostly deals
Its blows on instruments that buzz and click,
And Jove’s fierce anger turns the busy wheels,
Or learns of sage Marconi some new trick.
The everlasting tinkle of the bell
Invokes tall curses on the telephone,
The phonograph with nasal bleat and veil
Our gladness to the evening stars makes known.
Known to the planets on their silent way
Are all the lights that glitter on the wire,
Where beer or baking-powder loves to spray
The darkness with its advertising fire.
We press a button, and, with trembling zeal,
The potent voltage answers to our call ;
Unseen, unheard, its spirit loves to steal
Across the insulators on the wall.
Our cabbage gardens we electrify,
We stimulate the cauliblossom white
With that same power that erstwhile shook the
skies
Before Ben Franklin caught it with a kite.
No more the dullard and the strait-browed fool
Shall ignorance with fetters cold restrain,
We galvanise to life the laggard school,
And pour electric knowledge in the brain.
Soon may old Venus clutch her filmy skirt.
Call back her Cupid and her ancient doves,
While ordered currents teach us how to flirt,
And coils intensify our various loves.
Ami e’er the brief year has grown obsolete,
Dynamic force may spread a wider wing,
And, framing golden verse of even feet,
Teach some ecstatic poet-wheel to sing.
Till shod at last with patent rubber shoes,
The world, in non-conducting fabric drest,
May find conditions suited to its views,
And let the lightning work and take a rest.
N.S.W.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Shorthorn”: Giving evidence before the
Royal Commission on Sydney’s food supply, M. A.
O’Callaghan, the Government dairy expert, made it
clear that practically all the South Coast and Cam-
den districts were infected with tubercular bacteria.
It is cheerful information, as most of Sydney’s “pure
country milk” comes from these localities. But,
apparently, O’Callaghan is not anxious to make much
effort to stamp out the horror. He admits that the
tuberculin test is absolute, but would only apply it
to suspicious cattle—whatever that may mean—be-
cause if it were applied generally, and “30 per cent,
of the cows reacted, the economic loss would be
about £1,000,000, if the animals were destroyed.”
So it is more humane, in the expert’s opinion, to
kill off infants and infect health v men and women
than to interfere with the beastliness of Private En-
terprise.
A LONG DISTANCE EVENT.
Recently near Roma (Q.) a selector sent in
eighty-odd miles for a parson and other mar-
riage requisites. Herewith the emissary re-
turning triumphant.
“Eryander” : Duke Kahanamoku’s experi-
ence with a conger (B. 6/2/’l3), will surprise no-
body who knows the brute. The British species,
C. vulgaris, has really some sea-serpentish claims,
reaching sometimes 12ft. in length and weighing
well over the cwt. It is a very powerful and savage
fish, and, when one is hauled aboard an open fishing-
boat, great is the circus. The colossal eel darts
around like lightning, snapping viciously in all direc-
tions, while the men are tumbling over each other
in the endeavor to keep their feet clear of the jaws
and to get in a blow on his tail. It’s no use hit-
ting at his head, but a comparatively slight bash
on his back premises knocks all the fight out of him.

“0.K.” : This week ends the close season
for wild duck in Victoria, and in a day or two the
gradual extermination of the State’s most toothsome
feathered race will be advanced another stage. A 14
days’ extension of the time during which youthful
ducks may cultivate their flying powers, has given
great offence to rhe “sporting” gunsters of the Vic.
community, but it has hardly served to remind the
public in general that the birds are becoming fewer
every year. Probably the fact that there is no close
season for the sale of dead N.S.W. ducks in Mel-
bourne, accounts for public ignorance and indifference
on the subject. One of these
years Victoria —and N.S.W. a
while later —will be surprised
to learn that the local supply
of “game” food is utterly ex-
hausted. The spread of human
population diminishes the
number of unprotected breed-
ing and feeding grounds for
wild fowl, nor are there any
protected enclosures where
they can increase and multi-
ply, and be periodically mur-
dered in moderation. Wild-
fowl seem doomed to disap-
pear from the face of the less
uninhabited States of the Com-
monwealth, though they will
presumably get reasonable
measures of Government con-
sideration in Queensland and
the Northern Territory ere
“sport” can do its worst.
Even this hope will be blight-
ed if fashion should decree a
craze for wild duck and goose plumage in women’s
hats. It is up to all good Australian gourmands, who
love birds for their flesh’s sake, to keep a jealous eye
on the millinery of expensive womankind, and refuse
to shout a luncheon for any such as may be guilty of
wearing wild-duck-wing trimmings.
“Eryander”: Re “Jamtin’s” inquiry about
moon-blindness (B. 30/l/’l3). He may be easy'; he
isn’t at all likely to have his eyes obliterated by the
moon, because moon-blindnes3 (selenopia) occurs
only in persons constitutionally disposed that
way. I have known three cases —all in tropical
waters. In one instance, our boatswain, coming
aboard, “tanked,” at Sourabaya, fell asleep on the
deck under the full moon. In the morning he was
nearly stone-blind, and called himself every kind of
objurgated idiot for having risked the “blinkers.”
“Always get ’em,” he said, “if I sleep in full moon-
light. Three bloomin’ davs before I get my sight
properly back!” The effects, however, stuck by
him for a week, and many old Columbuses believe
that the sight is not fully restored until the moon
is full once more.
“Sluice Box,” from a place where the
mails go at odd times: Either “Wal” (B. 5/9/’l2),
or the bloque who gave him the informa-
tion that the stone pestle which was unearthed at
the Lakekamu goldfield (Papua) weighed over 201 b.,
never saw it, or he is a better judge of the weight of
tinned bulamakau than stone. Its weight is 7-jib.
This is not the first stone pestle that has been un-
earthed in Papua. A few, similar in shape though
not so large as the one found at Lakekamu, have
been unearthed by miners on the Yodda Valley and
Gira goldfields. Beneath about 14 ft. of earth and
wash, and resting on the conglomerate bottom of a
small terrace, situated about 60ft. above the present
level of Ironstone Creek (L.G.F.), a carving from
diorite, representing a bird’s head and neck—simi-
lar to a swan’s—was found. Then from beneath
about 12ft. of wash, and lying on the conglomerate
bottom of another terrace on the right bank of the
same creek, a diorite club-head was unearthed. In
shape it could be likened to a large napping-ham-
mer with the ends sharpened. It may have been
used for adzing out sago pulp from the palm stem.
At any rate it was not used by the present race of
natives who inhabit the hills behind the Lakekamu
—-known as Kulcukuku. The latter do not use
clubs of any description, so far as is known by the
few white men who have as yet come in contact
with them or visited their villages. \
“Chloe”: Apropos the note on “Some
Musics” (B. 23/l/’l3). The Hebraic type of islander
mentioned by “S. S. Severn,’ is conspicuous in the
Western Solomons, and is intensely musical. The
voices have a flute-like, yet very melancholy, quality,
and there is the pleasing combination of full tone,
fine range and perfect ear. The village sing-songs are de-
lightful. No matter how spirited and fierce the air
may be, it will have a chorus or cadence in a mourn-
ful minor key, the whole giving an effect of finished
artistry. At Rubiana, some children in the mission
school have been taught to sing from a numerical
notation ; and though I have taught choral singing
for many years, I have never witnessed anything like
the quickness of these little savages. Three and four
“part” harmonies were read perfectly after a few
minutes’ scale-practice, and all with a fine apprecia-
tion of nuances and of delicate and difficult intervals,
which would only be attained by the brighter members
of an ordinary school choral class—and never, never
by the bulk. It was after meeting the Western
Islanders that Mendana, the discoverer, named the
island group. He assumed that these islanders were
descended from Jewish slaves, and that in the island
ranges would be found the mines from which Solo-
mon drew his wealth. And perhaps the old navigator
is right. One has only to see features and gestures
to know that Semitic blood is there, and even the
speech betrayeth it. And they can certainly make
music which would charm away a madness worse
than that of Saul, the old farmer-king of Israel.
THE OLD PUSH AND THE NEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You will find, when over forty, man was made but
to repine,
And I sadly sit reflecting on that sinful past of
mine ;
W’hen the trade that I’ve forgotten paid far better
than the pen,
And when I too, for a season, was a leader amongst
men:
When in townships on the Mountains, in the nearer,
dearer Bush,
/ —bv virtue of my “writin’s”—was a captain of a
push.
Then the city pushes flourished—lived ancj flour-
ished as they ought,
For they blanked the world they lived in, and they
worked, and loved and fought;
And they worked for carcase butchers; “blanked”
and worked the whole day long,
Or they worked in iron foundries, they were big
and they were strong:
Feel them bleedin’ muscles (blimy!) , black with
grime and red with rust —
And they belted out their clobber and they belted
out a crust.
They were faithful to a clinali, they were loyal to a
pal,
And they stuck to their old Geyser, and they stuck
to their old Gal:
She must have the splosh (Gorblimy) and his sledge
goes clonk-and-elonk —
For the (bleedin’) rest is married, and the old ’un’s
pins is cronk.
And right here I’ll have to tell you, though it pains
and grieves and shocks,
That the old Gal and the Geyser were his parents on
“th’ Rocks.”
\nd perhaps he’d tell you proudly, as he wiped away
the grime,
That the old ’un was a grafter and a tough ’un in
his time.
That he did his share of scrappin’ and he’d won his
share of scraps,
And he took his share of “tangle” (and his “gruel,”
too, perhaps),
And he worked for his “old woman” when her
“lamps” was gettin’ dim,
And he stuck to his own old ’un when his pins went
crook on hipi.
And —well, what can man do better e’er he joins the
married ranks,
Than be loyal to a cobber and to deal it out to
Blanks ?
(And his clinah on occasion—when it might be good
for her)
And to work for his old people and their sacks of
“furnicher” ;
To be good to his “old woman,” who has suffered for
his sins,
And to battle for his old ’un when he’s shaky on his
pins?
When the push fared forth to battle —when their dig-
nity was hurt
Over Stinker’s bit of muslin or the Captain’s bit of
skirt—
Then the cobbers got off early from the workshop
and the dray,
And they togged, and took in “ballast,” and they
hurried to the fray ;
Then the fringed and bustled Helen found the purest
kind of joy
Where blue metal flew like blazes underneath the
walls of Troy !
Oftentimes the Sunday picnic, by the bay or river
reach,
Heard the howl of many demons from the cliff and
from the beach,
And they ran and screamed and fainted, and maybe
they said their prayers,
But their buns were not the object, and the danger
was not theirs ;
And the Sunday-school conductor found his courage
when he knew,
It was but “the Rocks” descending on the serried
ranks of “ ’Loo” !
There were anxious eyes, my brothers, watching
every ferry boat,
On the day when “ ’Loo” and “Redfern” had the
Rocks push by the throat;
When the sullen Glebe was neutral, and when Water-
loo turned tail,
And the Push, by misadventure, had embittered
Annandale —-
Watching from the scrub at Clontarf —and they did
not watch in vain—
For belated “Stinker” Blucher, and our Allies from
Balmain.
Those were lively days (Gorblimy!) as compared
with days like these —
And the old push? Some are fathers of respected
families!
For a sign of what they had been it would be in
vain to search,
For their boys are steady tradesmen, and their
daughters go to church:
And they little think that Father, sitting smoking,
in the past
Was the captain of a push and wore the clobber of
his caste l
Bob-tailed coat and bell-mouthed trousers, wire-
rimmed hat of long ago,
Boots, with stern-posts well amidships, that were
laced up from the toe.
Biled-rag Sundays, with no collar—a red riband for
a tie—
And a fringe well oiled and plastered! That was
Dad in days gone by!
Brand-new uniform on Sunday, and the old one all
the week, ' Mfe
Worn to show he was rib “manker,” worn to show
he was no sneak.
What is this? Ye gods fishes—weedy—-more
than undersized ;
Weak and humped and bottle-shouldered— Man’s
descendant realised !
Neither cripple, dwarf nor midget, nor trained ape,
though, be it said,
Dressed to ape the latest fashions: with a straw
pot on its head.
Seemingly for ever idle, still its clothes are new, and
yet,
From its mouth for ever dribbles the eternal cigar-
ette.
Cuffs and collars mostly spotless—clothes well made
and shirts well “done”—
Say! what mother bore and keeps it ? Say, what
father owns it son ?
See it hiding from the sunlight, see it hiding from
the stars ;
See it, with its fellows, drinking ginger-beer in
public bars !
Magpie minded: yet they’re harmless, though you
meet them one to ten—
Only fit to flock and gibber, after hours, at drunken
men.
’Tis the newer “Push,” my masters, shaming you and
shaming me,
Born of newer times and cities and our blind pros-
perity ;
Only gathered where our critics peer in at Aus-
tralia’s gate—
Reason, they, why Foster Frasers think that we
degenerate.
There’d have been a screech and scatter, ruined
togs and mended ways,
Had a Blanky Bloke met fifty, in the old, Gorblimy
days. .
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
