Christopher Mudd, travelling botanist,
writes wrathfully about Meiklejohn’s new Geography,
published 1909, on which young Australians are
being fed up into contempt for their magnificent heri-
tage. Meiklejohn, who does not appear to have ever
been in Australia, writes that Australia is mostly
desert, the most desolate and inhospitable part of
the globe. Wrong! shouts Mudd. He insists that
there is no desert at all, and he has been pretty well
all over the continent. Prairies, steppes and downs
are not desert —not Sahara. Forty years ago all the
now-fruitful Victorian mallee could have been bought
for a song. And even the Ninety Mile Desert Mudd
found a botanical par? lise. Like thousands more,
Mudd believes that whole armies of people have
been scared off Australia by the infernal lies told
about it. First the desert began at the Blue Moun-
tains. Then it was pushed back to Dubbo and Coon-
amble. Then it receded to the Darling. Then—
where the devil is it ?
“Billy T.”: They told me that the only
accommodation at Tallow Flats was with Andy Fel-
lows, at Oak Homes. There seemed nothing wrong
about that; and I resolved, if the tariff was right,
to stay a week at Oak Homes. The coach put me
down at Willowbank, where there was no bank and
no willows; consequently I was almost prepared to
find, after a walk of 91 miles, which seemed
I9i, that there were no oaks at Oak Homes.
But I really was hurt to find that there
was no home either. That is to say, no home
in the ordinary civilised sense. There was a large,
stringy-bark humpy, divided' into two small rooms,
and these were inhabited by Mr. and Mrs. Andy
Fellows, five Fellows children, and a lodger named
Whittle —an immigrant who was looking for good
potato land with a rich quartz lode in it. Before
evening another lodger arrived. He was carrying a
swag and had two dogs. Fellows had three dogs,
13 pigs and a cow. The cow did not want to live
in the hut. It had a roof of boughs on four sticks
all to itself at the back. I slept in one compart-
ment along with the two other lodgers, and most of
the dogs. Mr. and Mrs. Fellows slept in the next
room. What became of the' little Fellows and the
pigs I cannot say. We had mushrooms for tea and
supper. We had mushrooms for breakfast. At about
11 o’clock another stranger arrived, looking for
lodgings. lie was a prospector representing a city
syndicate, and pegged out the north-west corner of
the kitchen and dining-room at Oak Homes. There
was yet another lodger to help us with the mush-
rooms at dinner, and the four corners and the middle
of the kitchen were appropriated. When the fifth
man arrived Fellows rubbed his hands, and said:
“Cripes, if things continues looking up like this I’ll
have to sell the cow.”

What does a wombat eat? Professor
Stirling, of Adelaide Museum, is asking. He says
that the natural history books mostly credit it with
eating roots; but he has doubts. He does not want
mere information, but “authoritative evidence.” So,
if anybody can send along a wombat with a big root
in its mouth, let him not delay, but leap into the
breach like Hen. V. at Harfleur or thereabouts, in the
year thingumbob, as shown by the late George Rig-
nold on his big white horse at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney).

“Montalex”: In view of “Te B.’s” lively
item (B. 6/2/’l3), I rise unwillingly to demolish a
very common error. “T.B.’s” porpoise is not a por-
poise. He is a dolphin—not the so-called “dolphin-
fish,” but a cetacean ( Delphinus delphis). The por-
poise’s scientific label is Phocmna communis; and,
though he is a cousin of the dolphin, the two differ
widely in appearance and habits. The porpoise has
neither the pig-snout nor the pace of the dolphin;
his teeth, unlike the other’s, are few and far between ;
and, being presumably of a “sayrieus” disposition,
you never catch him frivolling about a vessel’s fore-
foot in mid-ocean. He is essentially a “coaster,
and lays to his book.

“Vigilans” deposits some crocs, from N.
Australia: —
During a big flood I have seen crocodiles at the
telegraph station on the Katherine River, 200 miles
from the sea. They have been shot over 13ft. in
length there. In a deep waterhole four miles further
up I have shot them 7ft. and Bft. in length. To all
appearances these are the estuarine species. In Kim-
berley there are some very large crocs, in the billa-
bongs. These are harmless as far as I know, because
I have often bathed in the lagoons where they have
been seen sft. in length. When the dry season comes
on and the water disappears, the reptile -buries itself
in the mud and remains there until the next rains.
I have seen crocodiles on the Fitzroy River (West
Kimberley) 12ft. in length, as far up as the cross-
ing, 210 miles from the ocean. They get in the
deep waterholes when the river gets low, and remain
until next flood. It is a good job the baby croc, has
a few enemies laying for him; first, the birds as
he runs down the sand after being incubated, and
then the fish as soon as he takes a header. Other-
wise, Australia’s northern waters would be so thick
with crocodiles that the animals wouldn’t have room
to turn.
“Mallarapoonie”: It has been said that
the camel is the one dumb brute in creation that can
count. I believe it is true, though I don’t know the
elephant, and ought, perhaps, to exclude him. The
camel gauges his load with a critical eye while you
are putting it on. If it is the same old load —the
load of yesterday, or the week before last —there
isn’t much complaint; but if there is the slightest
addition to its bulk, or the least increase in its
weight, his language is terrible. He gauges the
loads of all camels kneeling in his vicinity, and
compares them with his own. He counts the pack-
ages that go upon his own back, and those that go
upon the backs of adjacent camels, and the Just
and Rightful Burden of the Adjacent Camel should,
in his mind, be always heavier than his own. Bulk
and weight are synonymous with him; but, if any-
thing, he prefers weight. Bulk looks more appalling.
At Lawlers (W.A.) one morning, we had completed
loading a string of camels, and were preparing to
“uplift” for the day’s march, when I discovered an
unpacked hurricane lamp. As I stepped across for
it, every camel-optic in that kneeling train was
turned upon me. Each beast knew that the lamp
had to be carried; and not one of them but was
devising an excuse for shirking the exertion of hump-
ing it. The camel to whom it rightly belonged
pretended not to be looking. The candle-box in
which it was usually packed was already firmly
strapped on, and, as I did not wish to disturb the
load, I walked, with an air of finality, toward
another camel, which was comparatively lightly
loaded. He bubbled like fury when he saw me
coming in his direction. I tied it on his pack,
and though it made an additional weight of only
about 200 z., that camel was peevish and upset for
the rest of the day.
* * * *
“Mallarapoonie”: I can tell “Inchiquin”
(B. 2/l/’l3) something about those “runaway
holes” he mentions. When a small kid in Western
Victoria I, too, used to marvel at the amount of
water swallowed by the subterranean intake holes
that occur so plentifully along the basalt belt to-
wards Mount Gambier. Since then, however, some
of the mystery of them has been cleared up. The
spare fluid is generally wholly absorbed by porous
strata, or is engulfed by subterranean cavities that
are practically without limitation; or it has direct
outlet, by way of fissures and underground channels,
to the sea; or it may issue again at some lower
level in the shape of spring-water. A remarkable
thing is that it is always gravitating, always circu-
lating, in abeyance to some hidden natural law.
Probably the greatest region on earth for “runaway
holes” is the ancient coral formation of Papua.
Porous, fissured, hollowed into a million caves, there
are places there where whole rivers may be swal-
lowed up, and the rumble of waters falling into the
bowels of the earth may be heard afar off in the
dead of night and other occasions. Many and
marked are the instances of intake holes in Aus-
tralia, from the limestone cavern country behind
Camooweal, where an extensive subterranean lake
was recently discovered at about 300 ft. below the
level of the plains, to the quaint cave-region
near Cape Leeuwin. Numbers of Australia’s inland
lakes—especially those of North Queensland and the
Northern Territory—are nothing more than intake
areas for the artesian water supply.
“Pick”: Besides gradually exterminating
all ground birds, the fox is responsible for a notice-
able decrease of the rock wallaby. This interesting
and acrobatic little chap at one time inhabited the
Shoalhaven (N.S.W.) gullies, the ranges of the Mur-
rumbidgee, and the Snowy River valley in thou-
sands. His domicile was the caves and fissures of
the cliffs, and when startled from adjacent feeding
grounds, he would reach home by the shortest cut
even though he had to make a hurried descent of a
perpendicular wall of rock hundreds of feet high.
At the Bungonia Look Down, I have seen troops of
them bouncing from ledge to ledge down the face
of the precipice, where apparently there was not
foothold for a cockchafer beetle; but I never saw
one fall, though the limestone was smooth as a Wow-
ser’s tongue from the constant friction of their feet.
Even in those days they were much preyed upon by
the fur-hunter and the dingo; nevertheless they mul-
tiplied exceedingly until the advent of the Cold
Country curse. Nowadays they are rarely seen, and
their homes are plentifully occupied by the sneaking
wretch with the brush. Taking it for all in all, the
man who first liberated the fox in Australia was a
fair cow.
“Vigilans” : The latest southerner to throw
up his job in the Northern Territory is Mr. J. T.
After a year there he finds the place climati-
cally intolerable. White men cannot work there, he
says; in fact, it is cruelty to animals to work horses
in such a Sheol-like hole. To one of Ramsay’s type
and antecedents, this no doubt seems sufficiently like
sober fact. Before going to the Daly River he was
connected with the agricultural branch of the Educa-
tion Department in Victoria. He had had no more
tropical experience than a Scotch immigrant. The
man who has spent a number of years there, or who
is a native, does not notice the things which horrify
the Ramsays of this world. I, for example, spent my
young days in India and China. Later, I was for a
few years in the southern States of Australia,
and then I put in six years in North Australia.
Although I had malaria fever in Asia, I never had an
attack in North Australia. I consider the Territory
climate far and away healthier than that of either
tropical India, China or Malaysia. To say one cannot
do more than 50 per cent, of the work that he could
do in a temperate zone is absurd. But it is absurd to
commence farming when you have no market. The
one and only way to develop the country and to test
it is to build the Transcontinental railway and de-
velop the metalliferous areas. Whites will go there
fast enough then and to stay there. We do not require to
be retold that white men have been navvying on the
Pine Creek railway and mining for over 20 years
without leaving the country. Mr. Nieman, a chemist,
spent six years in the Daly country with his wife and
family. Although a southerner he speaks well of the
climate. Moreover, Mr. Nieman had never done
laborious work prior to going north. He worked at
mining and prospecting on the Daly River, and lived
for two years at the old French mission station on
the banks of the Daly. Speaking from experience, I
reiterate that I never had better health in my life
than I had during my two years in the coastal areas
of the Territory.

“Leichhardt”: If “C.G.F.” (B. 23/1/13)
asks any bushman from the Flinders or upper Dia-
mentina to identify his red-bellied snake, the reply
will be: Tiger. This may not be the correct name,
but the bright-hued land eel is known from Winton
to Hughenden and Richmond, and all the open plain
country out to Cloncurry by no other name. The
black rings that “C.G.F.” speaks of are visible only
for a short time after the snake has shed its skin.
When they disappear the back is left an even black
color all over. I have never met the variety away
from open downs country. He seems to have an
objection to heavy scrub, preferring to make his
home in the black soil plains. During a big flood in
1899 on Eastern Creek (Flinders Water), I saw more
of these snakes than any Outback run ever produced.
Every limb that was above water-level had at least
one snake perched on it, and four or five of us ac-
counted for 43 reptiles in one day within a quarter
of a mile of the homestead.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Amundsen, the finder of the South Pole,
has turned sea-wanderer again. He now
proposes to blow the dust off the old Fram
and have a try for the North Pole. If he
finds it the world will probably be much in-
clined to hail him as the original discoverer,
and then the wonderful Norwegian will have
a pair of Poles to his credit. Dr. Cook is
blown out. Peary’s claim has always lain
under a big shadow of suspicion. That
United States hustler deliberately left the
last of his white companions behind him, and
went on accompanied by only a few Eskimo,
who probably didn’t know a Pole from an
elephant. He returned to his white com-
panion with his own deafening and unsup-
ported assurance of success, but a compari-
son of times suggests the curious circum-
stance that if he reached the Pole he travel-
led more than twice as fast when he had no
white man to audit his figures as he could
when his figures were audited. And that
discrepancy hangs round like an embittered
ghost to this day.
BUCHANAN, South Australia's latest Judge.
Sussex-street, the narrow lane down which
the soldiers of Sydney’s early garrison used
to scamper for a morning dip. in the salt-
water, is going to see big changes in its
best-known business. Long ago, when pro-
duce agents were even more ravenous than
they are now, a hard-working youth called
John See got up from his father’s farm on
the Clarence and said he would go to Sydney
and act as agent for his plundered kith and
kin. The venture succeeded, and soon John
See’s boats ran between the Clarence and
Richmond rivers and Sydney, and year by
year increased in size and frequency. Then,
having conquered the local world of pump-
kins, John determined to also conquer the
world of politics—and did it so far as to
become Premier of N. S. Wales. A little
later the end arrived, but he left several
well stuffed bags of money behind him. Now
it is notified that the old association of John
See with the Northern Rivers trade will end
with March 31 next. The firm will go on
doing a railway commission business, but
the little boats and the big boats that
whistled at the river banks for the bulging
sacks and crates will pass into other hands.

Herman Milford, the new Paymaster of
N.S.W. Works Department, is a grandson
of the stately but somewhat atrabilious Mil-
ford, whom Lord Stanley appointed to the
Supreme Court Bench of N.S.W., and whom

the Ma State.
W. E. Smith, the big Sydney printer, went
lately to a better land, leaving a huge pile of
money. And though he had lived 33 years
in Australia, and had made his money here,
the British officials at Somerset House claim
to collect duty on the whole estate. By a
brilliant after-thought they say that- even
Smith’s 33 years absence from England
doesn’t affect his “English domicile.” If
this tale holds good, the position seems to
be that every person living in Australia who
chanced to be born in the Bull islands will
be liable to pay probate duty, and probably
income tax as well, to the Bull Government.
Apparently there are strenuous times ahead.

on his return.
Died in Adelaide, at 93, John Carr, farmer
and pastoralist. and member of the S.A.
Parliament as long ago as 1865, when he
had Adam Lindsay Gordon for a fellow
member. As Minister for Public Works in
the . third Hart Cabinet, and Crown Lands
Minister under Colton, he had a lot to do
with settling farmers in the previously un-
considered North, and also with the con-
struction of railway lines, notably the first
section of the transcontinental north from
Port Augusta. He was a strong and early
pro-Federation man, hut was out of harness
by the time the fight became practical. Lat-
terly, as a voluminous letter-writer to the
papers, he has been chiefly notable for driv-
ing the teetotallers frantic. It jarred them
terribly to have the loathed vigneron and
brewer supported by this God-fearing patri-
arch, who insisted that he stood for Temper-
ance, and was always ready with texts to
support his dicta.
There is at least one humorist in the
Commonwealth public service. In the Gov-
ernment’s official telegraph code the Pie
Commissioner is labelled Clackdish.
A couple of years ago J. M. Macadam,
for very many years manager of the Atlas
Insurance Co. in Sydney, was thrown out
of bis stride. An uncle, Sir Theodore Mar-
tin —a friend of “the old Queen’s” and
chronicler of the Prince Consort—died and
left him a fortune of £60,000 or so. Mac-
adam, of course, gave up his steady job in
the city, went to England and mixed with
new people without getting any comfort out
of it, returned to Australia, found himself
steadily feeling no better, went to Maori-
land to recover after some extra-strenuous
excitement with a daughter’s wedding in it,
and died there last week. He was a good
insurance man spoiled. For years he was
chairman of the Underwriters’ Association,
and for probity and sound judgment stood
on the top rung of the ladder.
Henry Wrixon, the gilded ex-President of
the Yic. Legislative Council—the gilt came
from his wife, a daughter of “Money” Miller
—has just turned out another of his strag-
gling, heavy-footed political novels in Lon-
don. It is called “Edward Eairleigh Frank-
fort,” and is published by Macmillan at a
fabulous six shillings. The thing is evi-
dently intended to be a satire upon the State
of Excelsior —the Australia of to-day—but
the characters are mere lay figures, and, as
in all Wrixon’s elephantine work, the
human interest is, ;spothered.

According to the current London papers the un-
defended Divorce Court list contains the cause Coun-
tess Cowley v. Earl Cowley, a petition for restitution
of conjugal rights, and Buxton v. Buxton and Earl
Cowley, a husband’s petition for divorce. , Lord
Cowley bolted with Mrs. Buxton the other day from
the neighborhood of Market. Harborough, where they
were hunting the uneatable fox. Hence the above.
He has crowded a heap of incident into his, brief
career. He began early by having £lO,OOO torn off
him for breach of promise to marry a Gaiety girl.
His only objection to making an honest 1 woman of the
lady lay in the fact-that he had become enamored of
another. After a perfect herd of thrilling and danger-
ous experiences in the field of Eros, he suddenly- de-
cided to wed and settle down. His wife presently
taxed him with excessive affection for one of her
friends. Divorce proceedings were started. To save
the lady’s name or something, he gave facilities for a
successful suit, with one of another class as co-re.

original home-burster’s identity being disclosed. De-

At a Sydney Harbor outing last week a
group of ancient mariners, 17 in number,
were photographed. The oldest of the party
was Captain A. W. Jack, aged 84 years.
Captain J. M. Banks came next, with 83
years. Then followed Captains W. Jack,
Napier and Sheed. each SO years. A ’long-
shoreman, Alexander Kethel, was 80, too;
and J. Johnston, the sole survivor of the
Dunbar wreck (August, 1857), acknow-
ledged the same span.
A. B. Piddington, w r ho has just got the
surprise; of his life and a High Court judg-
ship at the same time, is the son of an
Archdeacon whom The Bulletin remem-
bered for many years as Turkey Pidding-
ton. It happened this way. When George
Dibbs represented Tamworth in the Assem-
bly he invited the Archdeacon to one of his
meetings ; but the cleric replied that he had
a very fine turkey for dinner, and he thought
he would enjoy the bird more than the
speech.
J. E. Warburton, a grandson of the ex-
plorer Warburton, is in Melbourne, looking
around after 20 years spent on Erldunda
station. His nearest touch with the world
when he is at home is by way of Charlotte
Waters DepQt, on the overland telegraph
line. Of course he is going back ; meantime,
he has been trying to convince Defence Min-
ister Pearce that there is no place in the
world better fitted for the breeding of the
indispensable war-horse than the neglected
heart of Australia.-
“Cog.” recalls some duels: —
Bulletin (13/2/’l3) says that when Madden be-
came Chief Justice, Frank Gavan Duffy took his
place “in rivalry to Purves.’’ That rivalry was en-
tertaining, to say the least of it. It was the rivalry
of the blusterous bulldog and the teasing terrier, the
savage and the suave, dignity (with lapses) and impu-
dence. While Purves was bellowing forth an argu-
ment .with more or less offensiveness, Duffy sat
watching his Honor’s countenance, twirling his own
mo. (a Maddenette), and giggling in light ripples
to himself on general principles, and to keep the
Bench on its guard against the blizzardous Q.C.
When he rose, it would be with a delightfully ar-
tistic impersonation of A Small Barrister Almost
Speechless With Laughter. Sometimes Purves would
sit growling personalities through Duffy’s laughter-
splashed reply. Often he slipped outside pro tern.—
presumably because he required all outdoors to hold
his thoughts.
Now we come to the most affecting part
of the story—where the hero has to say
“Good-bye”:—
There was a clamor of farewells when the steamer
began to move away. Late-comers rushed to the
side of the pier and shouted their good-byes. Then
cheers were given, with much waving of hats and
handkerchiefs. Tears welled in his [the hero’s] eves
and ran unchecked down his cheeks. He turned his
back and buried his face in his handkerchief until
the cheering ceased.- When he- turned again his lips
moved, but he could not even shout his thanks,
with a forlorn smile, he fust waved good-bye.
No, it is not an extract from the latest
novel bv C. Garvice; it is merely a para-
graph from a report An .Melbourne Argus.
And the emotional bloquo was not bora
Ronald Blownipe, overcome with grief at
his severance from the Lady-oQlara. hut Vic-
toria’s Strong Man, Premier Willy Watt,
who was just leaving for a" four months
jaunt to the Cohen Country, to interview his
uncle regarding a little matter connected
with a loan.
THE HARNESSED HUSBAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Melbourne Divorce Court Chief Justice
Madden said there was urgent need of judicial power
to administer imprisonment or the lash upon hus-
bands for gross rascality and brutality. —News item.
Firm is her grip upon the lines
The parson gave into her hand ;
His legal harness winks and shines
As he goes trotting through the land.
Convention’s winkers bound his sight;
He cannot shy or look askance;
And, o’er his back, by day and night,
There cracks the Whin of Circumstance.
The leather chafes his patient back;
The white froth flies from Honor’s bit;
But he must keep the heavy track,
While in the family coach they sit.
The brakes squeal going down the hill,
For nothing’s left to evil chance;
She drives him with uncertain skill,
And cracks the Whip of Circumstance.
When at the wayside trough he drinks
The cool, sweet beer, his lawful load
Pulls on the reins, The driver thinks
He should not want to leave the road.
Reluctantly, with dripping mouth,
He turns like some departing ship
To brave once more the high-road’s drouth,
Urged on by that infernal Whip.
But now, beside the driver, see
A Judge with legal fervor grafts,
Flogging with splendid savagery
The animal betwen the shafts !
Ilis nine-thronged whip is raising weals,
As with hi? strong right hand he hits!
(LOOK OUT. That horse has got two heels.
He’ll kick pour dashed old coach to hits.)
N.S.W.

1911.. 112,417 12i „ ~ 37,500 17,654 57,263 541,370
1912.. 109,956 15 ~ ~ 45,000 8,932 56,054 597,424
This is one of the institutions that Maoriland is
proud of, and ought to be. A co. which in five years
puts by £198,927, besides writing more than £30,000
off its Consols and other securities, while it divides
but £lBO,OOO among its shareholders, is for that
fact alone entitled to great respect. Its business is
practically world-wide ; and that, of course, while it
may mean big figures, is also liable to mean heavy
losses. In 1906 it had a bad knock through the
’Frisco disaster, which cost it well over a quarter of a
million ; but the reserves are already £150,000 greater
than they were before that trouble happened. The
speed with which reserves have been accumulated is
largely due to the fact that the co. has done very
little in the way of capitalising profits; hence a very
fair dividend has not swamped a tremendous amount
of money. Now, however, with reserves equal to
twice the amount of the paid capital, it is proposed
to give the shareholders a plum. The capital is
£1,500,000 in 150,000 shares of £lO each, of which
only £2 has been paid. The directors’ proposal is
to capitalise £1 per share ( £150,000). The effect
will be to reduce the shareholders’ liability by that
amount. Still unless some tremendous succession of
disasters happens, that really means very little;
the trouble that would wipe out the reserves and
compel the co. to call £6 per share would leave
shareholders too dazed to notice that it wasn’t £7
per share. A more practical consequence will be that,
without putting up the dividend beyond 15 p.c. and
attracting the hostile attention of the insuring public
and others thereby, the directors can put 30s. in-
stead of 20s. into shareholders’ pockets.
It is to be hoped, though, that the directors will
not rush up the dividend precipitately. As a matter
of fact, the business has grown so fast that the risks
in proportion to funds are pretty large, notwithstand-
ing the comparative modesty of the dividends hither-
to. This little table tells that tale: —
Funds in
Proportion to
Premiums. Funds. Premiums.
1895 .. £267,917 £449,551 168 per cent.
1900 . . 342,036 481,485 141
1905 . . 425,991 640,916 150
1909 . . 608,283 755,019 124
1910 . . 605,509 784,107 129
1911 . . 638,007 841,370 131
1912 .. 676,097 897,424 132
The figures have thus steadily improved during the
past few years; but 132 p.c. is still below the
standard. To reduce the risks by reducing the busi-
ness is not to be thought of; and to get in more
capital from the shareholders would probably be un-
popular, though there is no earthly reason why it
should be, seeing that under precisely similar cir-
cumstances bank shareholders respond eagerly to such
calls. If shareholders don’t put in more money, the
only thing to be done is to keep it in by sitting hard
on dividends and laying up the profits.
The next table shows the progress of the business
in some other respects:—

Premiums have practically doubled in 12 years—a
very fine performance. And if there is any increase
in the expense rate it is almost microscopic. It is
now distinctly under the average, while the loss rate
is a bit above it —no doubt the combined result of
a big reinsurance business. The funds produced last
year £37,723 in interest —more than enough to pay
p.c. on the old capital of £300,000. Of the
underwriting profit of £77,262 (charging all ex-
penses against underwriting), only £7277 was divided
among the shareholders: the balance was retained to
strengthen the co.’s position. Things like that are
not likely to escape the notice of the big insurer;
and they ought not to escape the notice of any in-
surer, even the little one. Finally, it is to be hoped
that the increase of capital by the mere transfer of
reserves won’t incite the directors to adopt any other
policy.

Life Insurance Co. of Australia:—
Total
Nov., Income. Expenses. Claims. Outgoings. Deficit.
1912 £10,989 £18,918 £3715 £22,633 £11,644
The co., incorporated in Sept., 1911, commenced busi-
ness in November; but the first few months were
spent in compiling tables and buying the office door-
mat. The figures cover probably nine months’ busi-
ness-getting. Early in the proceedings the co. took
over the National Provident Life Assurance Co., a
small concern which had been hunting in the by-
ways. For the goodwill the co. paid £8000; what
it got for the money isn’t at all clear. Neither the
directors’ report nor the chairman’s speech at the
shareholders’ meeting threw any light on the subject.
Apparently, after being “successfully carried on for
many years,” the National Provident had 5615 con-
tracts, representing an annual income of “over
£10,000” ; but there is nothing whatever in those
figures to suggest that it was worth paying £BOOO
to get the business. More information should cer-
tainly be g'ven about transactions of this nature;
and, incidentally, the new law ought to see that it
is given. For this reason. An insurance co. is
formed, and the interested people put up, say,
£50,000. With the bait of that paid capital they
attract insurers. But presently, while not wanting
t(j abolish the bait, they want to get their money
b3f?k. They therefore establish a new co., or two
or three cos., and presently sell the goodwill of those
concerns to the parent co., for £30,000. They thus
get the £30,000 back; but the paid capital in the
original concern is not affected —on the surface.
The only difference is that the capital, instead of
being represented by cash, is thereafter represented
by probably worthless paper. It is, of course, not
for a moment suggested that anything of this kind
has happened in this case. But the supposititious
case suggests that managements of insurance offices
should not pay large sums of capital for goodwill
or other paper assets, without disclosing the whole
position, and showing that they got value for their
money.
The expenses for the first year are naturally very
heavy. And it ought to be kept in mind that, in
view of the purchase of the Provident Co.’s outfit,
this can scarcely be regarded as a first year’s busi-
ness. The fact that, notwithstanding the purchase,
expenses absorbed the whole of the income and
£7929 into the bargain—making a total of .173
p.c.—is something of a reflection on the purchase;
and for the same reason the chairman was not
entitled to compare this co.’s first-year’s business
with that of various other cos. which didn’t buy
other people’s goodwill, etc. The whole of the re-
marks made under that heading may therefore be
ruled out as inapplicable.
The paid capital is £44,000, represented by
143,000 shares of £l, paid to 55., and £8250 calls
paid in advance. Of that £44,000 the co. has
£23,867 in liquid assets and investments; it has
another £930 in stationery and furniture. The
other £19,203 is either represented by paper (good-
will), or has gone in exes, during the year. The
prospectus, by the way, said that 10,000 shares paid
to 55., plus £5OO in cash, would go to the pro-
moter. Presumably this £3OOO is included with the
“preliminary and establishment exes.,” charged to
revenue account. It is doubtful whether it should
not have been shown as a further goodwill item —
the shares, anyway. It is too early yet to speak
of the future. At least one Australian proprietary
or semi-proprietary insurance office has done
remarkably well; and the Life Insurance Co.
of Australia has power to take for its
shareholders the same 10 p.c. div., plus 20
p.c. of the profits. The thing, of course, is to make
the profits; and in competition with purely mutual
offices it has to show intending insurers that 80 p.c.
of its profits, available for policy-holders’ bonuses,
amount to as much as 100 p.c. of the profits in the
mutual office. To do that it will have to work at a
very low expense rate; and whether it can do so
remains to be seen.

The reserves are composed of these items: Redemp-
tion fund, £23,813; fund for renewals and contin-
gencies, £11,795; ditto, equalisation of divs.,
£4500; unappropriated profit, £1667. Now, some
of these may not be very good reserves, and the pro-
fits added to them may not be true profits. For in-
stance, if a machine is being worn out in producing
a certain profit, then the amount put by to presently
replace that machine must be deducted in order to
arrive at the true profit. But in this case it is pretty
certain that some profit is written off apart from this
fund. This supposition is supported by the fact that
whereas freeholds, leaseholds, plant, etc., stood in
the books on December 31, 1911, at £114,123, six
months later the item was down to £111,338. At
present the figure is £117,699, or £6361 more;
but as the very profitable ice plant has been enlarged
and cold-storage additions are in progress, it is pos-
sible that a good deal more than £6361 was spent
during the half-year, the excess being charged direct
against revenue.
A further view of the co.’s progress may be got
from these figures:—
Dec.,’o4. Dec.,’ll. June, T 2. Dec., T 2
Liquid assets £45,090 £78,761 £100,755 £105,201
Liabilities .. 29,949 20,091 21,386 26,194
Surplus.. £15,141 £58,670 £79,369 £79,007
Thus, in eight years the business has (1) paid the
dividends shown above; (2) provided for all ad-
ditions to plant: and (3) added £63,866 to the
surplus of liquid assets. On the surface it looks
like an excellent performance. The thing we don’t
know is whether any of that £63,866, and if any,
how much, ought to'be regarded as a set-off against
depreciation of plant, leaseholds, etc. that is to
say, a set-off additional to the undisclosed profit
which may have been put into new plant, etc. Some
years ago (1904), it may be remembered, a re-
valuation of assets was made, and over £llO,OOO
was written off. At that time the whole of the re-
serves went, and, moreover, capital was cut down
from 20s. to 12s. per share; but things improved so
much later on that 6s. of that was written back
again (1912). And if the Mort family felt that
way, values could be either written up or down again
to-morrow. Whether it is an advantage or not for
one family’s interests to be able to dominate a co.
the investor can think out for himself. Certainly he
ought also to reflect that, in this case, the family
with the big interest doesn’t belong to the market-
rigging persuasion.

Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic
Power Co. :

Dec., Profits. Dividends. Off. serves. Deficit.
1900.. £16,498 10p.c.= £11,250 £lOOO £4248 £1.193

Reserves
1903 . 13,499 10 „ „ 11,250 2249 1,754
1904.. 13,889 10 ~ „ 11,250 2639 4,393
1905.. 13,260 10 „,, 11,250 2010 6,403
1906.. 14,187 10 ~ „ 11,250 2937 9,340
1907.. 13,092 10 „., 11,250 1842 11,182
1908.. 12,636 10 ~ „ 11,250 1386 12,568
1909.. 11,268 9 „,, 10,125 1143 13,711
1910.. 12,300 8 „,, 9,000 3300 17,011
1911 12,909 8 ~ ~ 9,000 3909 20,920
1912.. 13,145 8 „ „ 9,000 2469 1676 22,596
Profits have, apparently, improved a bit more; but
it is doubtful whether there has been any real im-
provement. It will be noticed that for years past
nothing had been written off. There was a Plant
Renewal and Contingency account; but nothing was
debited to it. If renewals were made (and after over
20 years there must have been some renewals) the
cost was debited to Profit and Loss ; and the visible
profit was reduced accordingly. Now, it seems, another
practice has been adopted, the cost of renewals being
debited to Renewals account. The effect, of course, is
to first puff up the visible profits, and deflate them
afterwards. This shows the gross revenue for the
June-December half-year:—
1906 .. £12,975
1907 .. 12,161
1908 .. 12,352
1909 . . 11,429
1910 .. £11,852
1911 .. 12,129
1912 .. 12,089
There is not much comfort to be pot from that list,
especially in view of the immense number of new
premises that have been built, and the great number
of old premises that have been re-modelled, in Syd-
ney during the last few years. Evidently hydraulic
power has found a keen competitor in electric power.
Five years ago the assets apart from plant,
freehold, water supply and rights and conces-
sions ( £15,000) were valued at £9846, and the co.
owed £1143 ; net liquid assets, £8703. Now the
liquid assets are £11,337, and the liabilities £4076,
leaving £7261 net, or £1442 less than five years
ago. Meantime, however, £12,732 has been put into
property, plant, etc. —mostly into converting the
Waterloo water-supply into a freehold. On the one
side, then, we have this: the co. has distributed its
dividends, paid for current renewals, and put £11,290
net into assets. On the other side there is so much
of five years’ general wear and tear as is not covered
by current renewals. Probably the lay-by more than
covers that general depreciation; but the surplus
can’t be anything very substantial for a co. with a
capital of £112,500. Certainly, on those figures, one
wouldn’t be justified in expecting an increased divi-
dend, though, on the other hand, there is no reason
to expect a reduction.

So far as the transactions of the past six months are
concerned, that is as near to it as this paper can
get. After the manner of gas cos. this one didn’t
give much information about its new issue of capi-
tal. All that is quite plain is that the paid capital
has been increased from £160,000 to £208,000, that
£5B in new-share premiums has been received, and
that £31,841 or thereabouts has dropped out of the
reserves. Six months ago “applications for shares”
stood under capital for £15,933. What has ap-
parently happened is that of the £48,000 of new
capital, £16,000 represents money paid by share-
holders, while £32,000 was capitalised; in other
words, the shareholders got a bonus representing two-
thirds of the new issue. The few pounds of premium
capital apparently came from people who were
prepared to pay £lO or thereabouts for the few odd
£5 shares represented by fractions. This being so,
the 12 p.c. div. is really as good as £l4 4s. p.c.
would have been six months ago. Or if the £20,000
capitalised seven years back is taken into account,
the present div. is equal to a shade under 17 p.c.
The shareholders certainly have nothing to complain
of. The time may, however, come when the con-
sumers will rebel; and then probably the recent
N. S. Wales legislation, or something more drastic,
will be repeated in Queensland.
Brisbane has had money to burn these last few
years, and this co. has had a fine time in conse-
quence. This shows one aspect:—
Dec., 1905. Dec., 1912.
Liquid assets ..£41,673 £60,022
Liabilities. . . . 3,967 6,373
Surplus .. ..£37,706 £53,649
Meantime, apparently, £16,058 of cash has been got
in from shareholders. As the liquid surplus has
grown in seven years by £15,943, it follows that
practically the whole amount of new capital is still
in hand. In other words, all extensions of plant,
mains, etc., effected during the past seven years have
been paid for out of surplus profits. And what those
extensions have amounted to mav be roughly judged
by the fact that in the second half of 1905 the
revenue from gas, etc., was £23,435, whereas last
half-year it was £38,734, or more than two-thirds
greater. It is evident from this that the co. has
worked itself into a very strong position to meet any
turn of the tide. And in Queensland, with its erratic
seasons, nothing is surer than that the tide will turn.

The co. has still six years to go; then it must hand
over its works to the State without payment. Its
capital during the greater part of its life was
£200,000; the table shows that £150,000 of that
has been returned, and as there is already more than
enough cash in hand to pay back the other £50,000
the directors’ plain policy now is to distribute prettv
well all the profits as they are made. The past
couple of years have been extraordinarily profitable,
the result chiefly of a very big consumption of water
in the concentrating works at the mines ; and that,
again, has been the result of the development of
new zinc processes, coupled with a natural rush to
make the most of the boom in metals. This paper
doesn’t know how long the boom will last; hence it
doesn’t know how long the rush and the inflated profits
will last. Then there is the Government water ser-
vice now under construction at Umberumberka, a few
miles on the other side of Broken Hill; that ought
to be supplying its wet goods in a couple of years
at the outside. Though this co. will retain most of
the mine custom—which is vastly more important
than the town custom—the competition naturally
won’t do it any good. However, though 120 p.c.
divs. (on the reduced capital) may for the above
reasons be out of the question, something very solid
should still be paid; even £15,000 per half-year
means a div. at the rate of 60 p.c. p.a. on the shares
written down to ss. And even if no further assets
are acccumulated—that is to say, if all the profits
are divided as they are made—there should be some-
thing like 10s. or 10s. 6d. per share at the end, be-
cause in addition to the cash and liquid assets ( 6s.
6d. per share), there is about 3s. 6d. or 4s. per share
represented by meters, stores, property and machin-
ery, which will be available either for sale to the
public or to be taken over by the Government at a valu-
ation. It is possible, no doubt, that the co. may have
to cut into current profits to effect some renewals
during the next six years ; but one can be sure that
the co. will not spend a penny more than is neces-
sary to see its term through. If half the div. paid
last year is averaged for the next six years, the
total will be £lBO,OOO. Add £BO,OOO available
for final distribution. Total, £260,000 =£l 6s per
share. But on the present value of shares (225. <sd )

But though capital is £25,403 more than it was’lo
years ago, the money put up by shareholders amounts
to only £2642. Meantime liabilities have been re
duced by £11,976; a further £53,909 has been put

period have been only £25,968. And, besides the
profit shown above as written off, considerable sums
have been put into repairs and renewals and charged
direct to working expenses. Even allowing that
plant of this kind depreciates quickly, the co. appears
to have made ample provision for every reasonable
contingency ; and the whole thing reflects most pru-
dent management. Given average conditions, the
dividend on the increased capital should be available
without causing the co. any discomfort at all.

l n °^ er 10 P.000 shares. No doubt this lot,
iiKe the first lot, will be sent out by degrees. Times
are supposed to be good, and, in theory, moss should
be growing on the “Monty Peet’s” doorstep. But it
doesn t work out that way in practice. The borrow-
ing disease is infectious. The State borrows and sus-
tains a boom. In the artificial prosperity thereby
created, Brown does extra-well, and thinks he will go
on doing extra-well. He discounts the future, there-
iore, by borrowing to buy a motor-car. He isnT
earning up to motor-cars; but Jones’s wife doesn t
know what Brown is earning; she only knows how
he is living. So Jones has to borrow, too, and plunge
into a motor-car likewise. The community becomes
permeated with the spirit of drawing a bit on next
y*? r f expected prosperity. The “Monty Peet” proves
this by its figures. In 1905, when things were just
beginning to look up and when borrowings ought to
have begun to look down, the co.’s advances were
£82,949. At the end of 1909 thev were £99,732.
On December 31 last thev were £130,222. Seven
years ago the co. owed £7785 to depositors and
others, and had £7452 in the bank. To-day, W

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Sydney Taxi-Cab and Motor Import
—Six months ago shareholders in this concern
got their third div., at the rate of 10 p.c. p.a. ; up to
t hat time every half-year had produced its divi-
dend. This term they get something else. The
previous half-year ended with a credit balance of
£1348 at profit and loss. The div. absorbed £593;
the net loss on the past half-year’s business was
£1152; so the current half-year started with £397
on the wrong side of the sheet. The position looks
bad. The loss on cars plying for hire was £439;
to that, of course, must be added a proportion of the
heavy administration exes. ( £1556). Even then it
is doubtful whether enough is charged to cover de-
preciation. The running plant was valued at
£5759; from that £385 is written off, being less
than 7 p.c. for the half-yea?'. Few car-owners would
regard that as enough. If it isn’t enough, then the
co.’s real loss was greater than that shown. The
co. is also in the motor import and time-payment
sale business. On this account there is £9635 owing
to the co. Add land and building (Kent-street),
£7072 ; running plant, £5374; and stock, etc.,
£5136 —a total of £27,217 for these four items
alone —and it will be seen that the paid capital of
£12,717 is inadequate. The balance is owing on
mortgage, overdraft, and to sundry creditors. The
co. certainly has had a good many law-troubles during
the half-year, and these may have interfered with
the organisation; but it is pretty plain that the
whole thing wants overhauling and putting on a
better basis.
* * * *•
Shareholders in Tooth and Co., Ltd.,
Sydney brewers, now know the exact quality of the
plum that was promised them at the last half-yearly
meeting. The revaluation of the assets, which was
then proceeding, has been followed by a decision to
issue one 6 p.c. cumulative pref. share for every two
ordinary shares now in existence. Part of the
£550,000 thus called for will come out of reserves ;
the balance will be represented by written-up values.
The share market has already • discounted this bonus
—too heavily, in fact. Recent quotations have been
about 555. The 6 p.c. cumulative pref. shares will
be worth, no doubt, 245. Halve that = 12s. Deduct
that from 555., and 435. is left to represent the value
of the ordinary share. And 435. for a 20s. share,
carrying a 10 p.c. div., means a return of only £4
13s. p.c. which can scarcely be regarded as sufficient
in view of the fact that this hop plant can’t be ex-
pected to yield a further crop of plums for many a
day. The investor who wants a 5 p.c. return all
round could afford to pay 525., plus 9d. or so to
represent accrued dividend-earnings. The half-year
ends in March.
* * * *
The Commonwealth Bank has shifted
some of the privately-owned banks in one small par-
ticular already. The P.E. institutions charge ss.
half-yearly on all accounts, big and small, but the
Federal bank announces that no charge will be made
on credit accounts of over £5O, and on advance ac-
counts. This idea has been adopted by a few of
the other institutions ; and there is very little doubt
that the rest of them will have to follow suit.
* * * *
The underwriters (John Darling and his
friends) will have to take up the whole 100,000
Broken Hill Prop, shares to which they put their
names. In connection with the iron and steel pro-
position, 240,000 were offered; and the public ap-
plied for only 74,511 in London and 43,208 in Aus-
tralia. The trouble was the doubt in the public
mind as to whether the State will not presently go
into the business; and if it does, by far the biggest
customer will be at once lost to any private pro-
ducer. And, unfortunately, the co. was not at liberty
to report the conversations which General-manager
Delprat has had with various people on this subject.
*’ * * *
The B. H. Prop, is losing no time over its
iron and steel enterprise, and already has the founda-
tion of its works well in hand. Meantime, there is
a little trouble in S. Aus. over the co.’s proposal to
build a new railway from the quarries at Iron Knob
to the coast of Spencer’s Gulf. Port Augusta has
had the trade hitherto (so far the stone has been
used only as flux), and naturally it is kicking. But
the Government is not disposed to interfere.
* * * *
Understood that the Grafton copper mine
(N.S.W.) is suffering still another change of man-
ager. There seems to be something wrong some-
where—probably in the direction of undue interfer-
ence with the managerial plans. According to re-
cent information, development was sadly behind-
hand ; and that may be the cause of the trouble in
this instance. If it is it can be taken for granted
that it wasn’t the manager who put his foot down
on the more energetic opening-up of the mine.
» • * •
There re-appears from Oblivion, into
which it slipped two or three years ago, the inches-
wide copper show at Lobb’s Hole, some miles in the
wilds beyond Tumut (N.S.W.). At one time it
had unbounded confidence in its ability to produce
copper at £33 per ton, landed in Sydney. For
though the ore was only in a ribbon, it was almost
pure chalcopyrite. Unfortunately, in a very brief
period, the premature furnace chewed up all there
was to chew, and debts rapidly mounted. The mine
has been under water since last August; the last
work done was by tributors, who gouged out a few
tons of 25 p.c. ore, The aforesaid reappearance,
however, is only to make a request for another
six months of quietude. A report about good ore
left in the winze at the 200 ft. level will make a
handy yarn for some hoary-headed oldest inhabitant
at some far-off date.
• • * •
Lloyd’s Copper (N.S.W.) is getting back
to its old stride. The concentrating mill is run-
ning, and two out of three furnaces have been
warmed up. And the copper market chooses this, of
all times, to slump badly.
* * * *
Westralia exported £271,617 worth of
minerals other than gold in 1912. Copper and tin
contributed most. The aggregate export to December
31 was £3,168,166, of which £1,113,142 repre-
sented copper, £1,051,155 tin, £283,848 silver,
£40,587 silver-lead ore and £679,434 miscellaneous.

Associated Northern’s profit for January
exceeded £7OOO, and from now onward monthly
and l £7ooo re expeoted to fluctuate between £6OOO
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) showed a
profit of £6548 for November, and the local paper
whooped accordingly. Nothing was said about the
drop m the December surplus to £2768; but when
the January figures revealed a loss of £3OO, it was
recognised that it was up to somebody to explain
things. Hence it is announced that unpayable stone
was mined against the instructions of the manage-
m®. • u Tlle real facts probably are that the co.,
which has next to nothing of a bullion reserve for
sweetening, unexpectedly got on to a rich patch in
November, and hasn’t had a similar stroke of luck
since.
* * * *
“Bar None”: Re Great Boulder, “Bar
One (B. 23/1/T3) proves nothing except that he
doesn t know what he is talking about. Blown out
as regards costs and sundry other contentions, he
tries to bump the management for its alleged in-
capacity and sloth. Well, here goes with the second
barrel. In a little over 17 years the co. has done
nearly 22 miles of underground development and
nine miles of diamond drilling at a cost of about a
million sterling. As a result of this work it has
recovered about 8} millions sterling and distributed
nearly half of it in dividends. If there is any mine
in “Bar One’s” stable that can beat this record, let
him name it or hereafter shut up. Now, a word or
two about (1) the 3600 ft. x 500 ft. “untouched” area
east of the main workings, and (2) Hamilton’s bore-
hole development. As a sort of last resource, both
may ultimately receive attention, but so far the co.
has found plenty to do with its money elsewhere.
All the work done to the eastward tends to prove
that the country in that direction is as poor as
Lazarus; and as for the “6ft. of 9dwt. ore,” cut
east of Hamilton’s shaft, which “Bar One” says
ought to be worth more prospecting, the drill core
showed this formation to consist of veins of quartz
and schist embedded in granitic rock, and to assay
B*dwt. The fact that it is 512 ft. from the shaft
and 1800 ft. from the surface, has deterred the man-
agement from getting into closer touch with it for
the time being. No doubt “Bar One” and his
friend, the Man in the Street, would rush it like a
bull if they had charge ; but, fortunately for share-
holders, such an awful calamity is never likely to
happen.
• * * •
Emeralds of a kind have been discovered
at Poonah (W.A.). Some of the best stones have
been sent to a London gem expert. Meantime, one
weighing 32 carats has been tested by the Govern-
ment chemist, who reports that one-third is value-
less and the remainder so fissured that the largest
gem that could be cut would weigh only a fraction
of a carat. Three other stones, under two carats,
had a much better aDpearance, but were defective
through small internal fractures.
* * * *
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : Great Unknown, at
Marda, has gone the way I said it would, and “Peg-
matite” and other interested persons said it wouldn’t.
By a mere coincidence, no doubt, the shaft was sunk
on the richest part of the shoot, and a drive was
put in along a floor of good stuff. Beyond and
below these workings values have dwindled.
* • * #
State Mining Engineer Montgomery re-
ports that recent developments at 370 ft. in the
Associated Northern Co.’s show at Ora Banda (W.A.)
are pleasing to the eye. He says the rich shoot
exposed at and above that level has a permanent
look about it; and as telluride of gold is about the
only mineral likely by oxidation to produce the
spongy gold showing in some of the specimen ore,
he thinks there is every chance of telluride being
found among the sulphides. These, he estimates,
will be reached in the next 50ft. in depth.
* * * •
Under the stimulus of high prices* Queens-
land is rejoicing over an enormous increase in its
mineral output. The quarterly return to December
31 shows £751,931 for minerals other than gold,
coal and precious stones, as against £419,885 for
the last quarter of 1911. Copper output increased
from 5296 tons to 7503 tons, silver from 143,1200 z.
to 217.6960 z., lead from 445 tons to 1458 tons, wol-
fram from 142 tons to 185 tons, and alluvial tin
from 191 tons to 200 tons.
* * • *
Owing to the expansion of copper mining,
the figures of the Townsville-Cloncurry railway (Q.)
are racing. The earnings last year were £496,471, or
£93,116 more than in 1911. There used to be a
long slack season on the line that creeps back from
Townsville; but, owing to the transport of metal,
coke and stores, the wheels are very seldom idle
now.
• * * *
Gilmore, the N.Q. tin mine, which six
years ago started a meteoric career by paying
monthly dividends out of the sink of the prospecting
shafts, has come to a rocketty end. At the last
general meeting of shareholders the chairman inti-
mated that the mine had been let to tributors. The
first thing the tributors did was to clean up the old
145 ft. level, and they got enough metal in the pro-
cess to start a rumor that they were on a 3ft. lode
of rich ore. The patch, however, was only the drip-
pings from the truckings of the early-day 25 p.c.
crushings. The chairman mentioned at the meeting
that sulphuric acid in the water corroded the pipes
of the pumping gear very rapidly. This, coupled
with the presence of copper in the tin, argues the
proximity of a sulphide body. The main shaft is
down 400 ft., and shows a splendid footwall that has
never been broken into —and now probably never will
be.
* * * *
Died last week Lewis Thomas, who did
more than any other man to open up the Ipswich
fQ.) coal mines, now the most prolific in the State.
It was an almost desperate struggle, and the old man
used to say that for 14 years, though he slaved at
the job, he drew less than the poorest-paid man on
the wages sheet. Then things turned his way. A
gTateful district rewarded him by electing him its
M.L.A.; but he did not care for that, so the Leg.
Council swallowed him.
* * * *
Mount Cuthbert (Q.) sends good news
i Kalkadoon mine. The crosscut at the
150 ft. level has struck the lode at a rich spot;
assays give 25 p.c. copper over 3ft., and the bore
shows ore of fair grade for a width of 6ft. A
crosscut is also being driven on the Warwick Castle
formation, and as the shoot is a strong one near the
surface good prospects will probably continue for
a while. Mount Cuthbert itself is, however, a warn-
ing not to go too much on values near the surface,
for its deepest level carries stuff that would merely
make good feed for a concentrating mill.
* * * *
Charters Towers has secured two State
subsidies—£2ooo for Pfeiffers’ Day Dawn and
£3OOO for Brilliant Block. But as work at
deep levels mops up a deal of money, it is practi-
cally certain that fresh capital will be required, if
only to conform with the conditions of the loan,
which are that Pfeiffers shall spend £ for £. The
conditions with regard to the Block loan are not so
clearly stated ; perhaps the heap of money spent in
fruitless driving and crosscutting has been con-
sidered. If, in extending No. 4 level to the south-
west, the Brilliant Deeps follow a good reef beyond
their boundary into the Blocks, there will be no
difficulty in raising fresh capital, for the Deeps are
proving that payable stone lives down, and are also
sinking below No. 5 with a view to opening a still
deeper level. The developments at the New Queen
Cross lease, on the Moonstone reef, continue good,
and the crushing going through (about 200 tons)
is looking well.
Last month Mount Morgan saved 94650 z.
of gold in 631 tons of blister copper, or 15oz. per
ton. The previous month gave 13oz. Probably en-
couraged by improved smelting practice, manager
Magnus is throwing more silicious gold ore into
the furnace. In all 16,480 tons from Mount Morgan
and 3629 tons from Many Peaks were treated; total,
20,109 tons ore, from which was got a return of
£77,329, or £3 16s. lOd. per ton, taking copper at
£6O.
* * * *
A race to find petroleum by boring has
been started in Papua by C. H. Locke, representing
the Commonwealth, and F. Griben, of the British
New Guinea Development Co. The latter co. was
formed in London by J. G. Jenkins, ex-Premier of
South Aus., and others, to exploit Papua, as Burma
and Borneo were exploited by London corporations.
The Commonwealth Government having decided to
prohibit boring for oil in Papua by Private Enter-
prise, Griben and his drilling rig were sent to Yalaila
River, 125 miles N.E. of Port Moresby, by the Gov-
ernment. C. H. Locke’s boring plant, built by the
Goldfields Drilling Co., at Melbourne, can go to
2000 ft., while Griben’s is limited to 1000 ft. Locke
has reported to his department that he is sure to
get oil at depth, the geological surroundings being
favorable. To make quite sure of this, the Govern-
ment has asked India to lend it an oil expert from
Burma to visit Papua and report on the geology of
the Valaila field. The main conditions are identical
in Burma and Papua, viz., tertiary rocks, saddle
reefs, limestones, sandstones and shales, while oil
has been found oozing from joints in the surface
rock, and brine is also present. If oil be struck in
Papua, it will most likely be also found across
Torres Strait, in the Northern Territory. On this
assumption, an American oil-man, named Halley,
has formed a small syndicate in Melbourne with
£5OOO capital, to explore the Territory for oil.
Three members of the Victorian Cabinet hold shares,
and the enterprise is being backed by a wealthy
newspaper proprietary. But £5OOO won’t go far;
in fact, it is unlikely to go much farther than the
wharf at Darwin. However, it’s a beginning—the
genesis of a new industry for Australia, perhaDS.
People who were in the ill-fated MacArthur River Co.
are likewise putting up a few pounds to test the
Victoria River country for oil.
* * * *
“Geology” Herman sends out a glowing
report on the possibilities and prospects of Victorian
brown coal. His predecessor, Stirling, was thought
to be barmy on brown-coal, whereas what he did was
to foresee that the power-gas engine would change
everything. In Germany and Russia brown coal
drives many a mile of train, and provides fuel for
a multitude of factories. Victoria, with Newcastle
just by, giggled over Stirling’s enthusiasm; but it
started to get serious when a group of German Jews
proposed to gobble up the best bit of Morwell. If
Herman be right, their presence wouldn’t matter
much —provided the State doesn’t let them put their
greedy paws on to a monopoly of electricity in Mel-
bourne. Herman figures out that Victoria has be-
neath its onion and cabbage plots a brown-coal area
of 2000 square miles, containing 3,000,000,000 tons
of mineral. Some of the seams aggregate 750 ft. ;
others are as thin as a bar sandwich. Averaging
them at 50ft., the foregoing total is arrived at. Then
discarding three-quarters of their contents, and giving
a value of 4s. per ton to the balance, a national asset
of £200,000,000 appears. Yet there are stinking
fishites who allege that little Victoria hasn’t a
mining or an industrial chance left. Peter Mcßride,
thinking the reverse, does his last big administrative
act by preventing the alienation of any more coal
land.
* * * *
H. E. Rowe, when president of the Cham-
ber of Mines of Victoria, hit the miner pretty hard
over gold-stealing. The Bulletin now asks him to
retain his attitude as a purist, and say if he is doing
the fair thing in pushing on the liquidation of the
Long Tunnel Co. The Pearsons and the Firebraces
having carefully squeezed every bean they could out
of the Long Tunnel, is it not their duty to
put a little cash into the field? Dare he say that
to deliberately throw Walhalla into poverty and idle-
ness, when (vide Herman) it is far from worked
out, is a worse crime, in view of the dividends
reaped from it, than for the individual miner to
now and then nip a bit of gold? Let the Pearsons
and the Firebraces keen out of the voting re the
liquidation, and see which way the majority of the
shares will go. Can Rowe think it is worth fighting
to get such people a final dividend out of a sale of
plant, seeing the stacks of divs. they have raked in?
Is it compatible with the high interests of mining
(which Mr. Rowe, as a leading spirit of the Chamber
of Mines is pledged to support) to jump a co. of this
kind into liquidation? Mr. Rowe, as president of
the Chamber of Mines, would have known it wasn t.

The quaintest thing ever seen in prospec-
tuses was issued lately by the New Caledonia G. M.
Syndicate, N.L. There are three names on it, in-
cluding that of “George Stevenson (late mining
warden of N. S. Wales for the past 30 years).
“The lode,” says the quaint prospectus, “is about
half a mile wide. The length of lode is about
eight miles. The lode consists of ironstone gossan.
The average yield of 14 samples is loz. 15dwt.
Samples cannot be picked—lode all the same.” And
all that the syndicate asks is that the public
shall put up £l5O to obtain titles and bring one
ton to Sydney. For that £l5O the public can have
one-third' of a mine with a lode half a mile wide
and eight miles long, and all of it going loz. 15dwt.
gold to the ton. The Bulletin’s own idea is that
this is the identical propertv from which the rich
uncle in Fiji gets all his millions. #
“Mallarapoonie” : The nrtesian well-bprer,
who gropes down into the innards of this whirling
globe of ours with about a ton of steel sinker on the
end of an amazingly long wire rope, is finding things
in North Queensland. Besides water and coal seams
and bedrock (which mostly leads to curses) and other
items, one of him while boring in the vicinity of
Lake Yarrowmere—which is also known as Lake
Buchanan, and is merely a large salt marsh, the be-
loved home of nearly all the pelicans west of the
148th meridian—lateiy punched a hole through what
seems to be a very fine stratum of salt at a not-too-
great depth beneath the surface. This is the first
true salt-bed gone through in Australia, as far as
writer knows. Speaking of well-boring, there pro-
bably isn’t a more monotonous job on earth than that
of an artesian well-driller. Right round the clock,
month in, month out, he and an asthmatical engine
are mostly occupied in lifting a ton or so of steel bar
and letting it drop dismally again in the same place.
Still, even he gets his surprises. To say nothing of
the unexpected, though enlivening, arrival of boiling
water on the scene, a borehole may go off suddenly
at any time and blow the roof away. Also, it may
take, unaccountably, to exuding inflammable gas;
so that, when the monotonous person, who sits drear-
ily near the top end of the indefinite hole, attempts
to light his gradual pipe about 2 a.m. of a slow
morning, he is liable to get a lot of tardy whiskers
and dilatory back hair unexpectedly singed.
