A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 17, 1913.
My Dear Arini.—
It is not likely that much banging of the
big drum will be needed to bring in subscrip-
tions to the fund now being collected in re-
sponse to Captain Scott’s last appeal. The
whole nation appears to be responding with
a rush. We in Sydney feel a more intimate
connection with the lost explorer from the
fact that this was the last patch of civilisa-
tion worth mentioning visited by the deceased
Pole-finder before he left for the ice. Many
of us met him and Mrs. Scott when they
were staying at the Australia, and she was
deservedly popular with her own sex. Rather
fragile in appearance, and reserved in man-
ner, Mrs. Scott, when here, managed, with-
out effort, to convey the impression that she
was Somebody, whereas so many people im-
press you as being half of Nobody. Her
personality crystallised itself into sculpture,
and, after much diligent probing, the lady
was induced to show photographs of some of
her work to those Sydney acquaintances who
knew she used to be Kathleen Bruce. Two
years ago that was. She seemed strung up
to a high pitch of endurance even then. The
shadow of the coming tragedy lay in her
sombre eyes.
Mrs. Wilson, widow of the perished scien-
tist, is in Maoriland, whither she journeyed
to meet the Terra Nova. Having, like most
scientific folk, a contempt for fashion and
display, Mrs. Wilson took a second-class
berth, and, expecting to meet her husband,
had not even a return fare. Of course, that
is not worth mentioning, for the want of
money will certainly not be felt by any of
the women and children connected with the
lost party. But it serves to show in what a
confident spirit Mrs. Wilson set forth, and
how cruelly that spirit has been disap-
pointed. Mrs. David point blank refused
to go to Maoriland to meet the expedition in
which Professor David discovered the South
Magnetic Pole, because she didn’t wish to
tempt Fate. She felt that had she gone to
meet him he would not have been there to be
met. When you are playing against Fate it
is best to take no chances.
The visiting Jap warships fill up Farm
Cove nicely at present—and the favorite
viewpoint to see the smoke-belching grey
fighters is from the grounds of ex-Govern-
ment House —the old mansion that lost its
job. It looks really queer to gaze on the
scene from the Manly boat. Instead of
British ships lying foreninst green lawns
sacredly reserved for vice-royalty, and empty
of humanity, which was the spectacle pre-
sented by our naval station aforetimes, now
the ships fly the flag of the rising sun, while
Hoi Polloi roam at will over the verdant
swards, and recline at ease under the shadow
of the sacred pines planted by royalty itself.
Jap senior officers and those of flag rank
bear a Pooh Bah air of proud exclusiveness,
but the junior officers and middies, or cadets,
or whatever they call them, mingle' freely
with the throng, and apparently extract as
much fun out of the visit as the band of
crepe on the left arm, denoting mourning for
the late Mikado, will allow. On Saturday
a party of youthful officers went to Manly,
armed with kodaks, and had a real good
time snapshotting the surfing girls and being
instructed in all the arts of life-saving,
running out the reel and other aquatic
sports. The boys from far Nippon were not
shocked at the surfing girls at all, because
in their own country they sensibly leave off
their clothes while they bathe. Only, they
don’t bathe in the sea. Their sense of
cleanliness can be appeased by nothing less
than parboiling themselves in one of their
numerous public baths after the fashion of
the heated old Roman of Imperial days.
Some of the North Steyne Life Saving
Club posed for the Japs on Saturday, and
were photographed standing behind their life-
reel. These sun-tanned heroes looked as
brown as the Japs themselves, only a differ-
ent kind of brown. The Nipponese is a
yellow brown. The Sydney surfer is a red-
dish burnt umber shade. However, Japs
and Australians made a happy party, the
visitors apparently giving the Japanese equiv-
alents for “eulogistic remarks” on the life-
saving display. There was a beastly surf
running under the strong nor’easter, and the
man who was to be subsequently “rescued ”
swam far out into the Pacific. It all looked
most heroic, and obviously impressed the
strangers immensely.
Sometimes the Department of Public In-
struction feels that it needs interpreting.
Then it issues circulars and other literature
easily understanded of the people, explaining
why it does things. One has just been put
forth with the appealing title, “A Word to
Mothers.” Feminine parents of girls who
have successfully wrestled with their qualify-
ing certificate examination are urged to
send these girls, or others who sufficiently
resemble them to pass muster, to learn house-
work at the new Domestic Superior Public
Schools. This leaflet is as persuasively
worded as the patent medicine ad. which is
thrown over the garden gate by the peri-
patetic distributor. “The fear of dirt is the
beginning of housekeeping”—so runs its un-
written law, and on that it bases its plea to
the girls who have obtained their Q.C. to
continue for some years at school, learning
how to make home happy in after years.
Almost the last appearance of Lady
Chelmsford on a Sydney platform will be
her promised attendance at the annual meet-
ing of the Benevolent Society, which is billed
for Wednesday next week.
Captain Arthur Webber, hon. sec. of the
League of Ancient Mariners, had a goodly
muster for the annual outing of the League
on Saturday last. Actors, for a holiday, go
to the theatre; in the same spirit, sailors
invariably go to sea for a pleasant change.
So the League foregathered at Fort Mac-
quarie, and voyaged to Rushcutter’s Bay,
where it had a luncheon in the Naval
Dep6t, by the courtesy of Commander
Brownlow, R.A.N. (please don’t omit the
“A.”) Then the Ancient Mariners cruised
around the harbor, and each one aired his
own _ particular pet scheme for effectively
lighting the same. It was a very jovial
gathering of genial souls, although two very
sad events of the nautical year were per-
force honored in silence —-the loss of the
Titanic and the death of Captain Scott and
his mates.
Mrs. W. A. Holman was in Paris at latest
dates, _ waiting for her “Will,” along with
her sister, Una Kidgell. W. Holman was
to meet them from Australia, via Marseilles.
The Kidgell girls are all born inkslingers.
They have a family paper, the Co-operator.
First Ada (Mrs. Holman) had it, then she
handed it to Una. Now Una has passed it
on to Stella, who still holds down the job at
No. 1 Hunter-street, Sydney.
Mrs. Piddington, wife of the new Justice
of the High Court, has been staying for
some time at Endsleigh Gardens, London,
but will soon leave England with her hus-
band for Australia. At Christmas and New
Year, Mrs. Piddington’s friends were pleas-
antly reminded of her existence by receiving
very prettily-done little water-color sketches
of various spots visited by her on her travels.
This is quite the newest thing in Christmas
cards, being more individualistic than the
ordinary affair, and for those who can ac-
quire the art of water-color, it is a much
more distinctive way of complimenting a
friend.
A Lenten announcement gives forth the
engagement of Miss Maisie Ebsworth, only
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Geo. Ebsworth, of
Ocean-street, Woollahra, to Mr. Hutchinson.
This gentleman comes to see his bride-elect,
braving all the horrors of the tunnels on
the Southern railway line, for he lives down
South. Miss Ebsworth, whose father can
afford a handsome dowry, is quite a young
girl.
News was received by cable on the 12th
of the sudden death from heart trouble of
Mr. James Martin Macadam, in Dunedin.
Many years business manager of the Atlas
Insurance Co., chairman of the Fire Under-
writers’ Association, and member of the Fire
Brigades Board in Sydney, he filled a con-
spicuous position in the business world. A
Scottish elder, he was a prominent member
of St. Stephen’s, Phillip-street. Most people
know that the Macadam family is related
to the late Sir Theodore Martin, K.C.8.,
K.V.D., a much-esteemed personage in the
estimation of the late Victoria and her con-
sort. Sir Theodore was Mr. Macadam’s
mother’s brother. His father was an Edin-
burgh banker. As a young man Mr. Mac-
adam had an adventurous life. He went to
sea with an uncle, Captain Martin, was ship-
wrecked, turned prospector in North Queens-
land and in this State, settling at last in
Orange. Thence he came to Sydney. One
daughter was recently married to Dr. C.
Wallace Weihen, of Macquarie-street, and he
leaves another daughter and two sons. Mrs.
Macadam, who had gone with her husband
for the Maoriland trip, returns this week
by the Ulimaroa. The deceased was a keen
bowler, and every afternoon saw him at the
City Bowling Green.
A letter comes to me with the heading
“R.M.S. Orontes” on the way to Colombo.
One of the passengers is Premier Scaddan,
of Westralia. Lest anybody should forget
that he is such a high and mighty much-
ness, this is the inscription on his deck
chair: “Hon. J. Scaddan, M.L.A., Premier
of West Australia. Passenger to Naples
via Orontes.” The letter doesn’t state
whether the chair was extra strong in order
to bear all that glory without breaking
down. Scaddan is of huge stature, and is
followed by a tame sec. in the bantam class.
Dr. Mary Booth is very angry with some-
body, the same being Mr. P. Board. Her
anger is justified by events. Dr. Mary is in
Sydney, having arrived from Melbourne per
motor-car. She travelled with some friends.
They took four days over the trip, and Dr.
Booth says she is so glad they did, because
she saw N.S.W. in a new light. The scenery
all the way from Albury was simply en-
chanting. If such a lovely route existed in
the United States there would be reams of
literature written about it, and folk would
go all the way from Australia to see the
beautiful sights, but as it is just south of us
along the Illawarra-road, nobody marks it.
But the scenery has side-tracked me from
the plain tale of Dr. Mary’s indignation.
The reason is that Dr. Gertrude Halley was
not given the new billet of schools medical
officer, which has been handed over to Dr.
Willis, although Dr. Gertrude has to her
credit good work in the same job under
another name. She was, up till her resigna-
tion, schools medical inspector, which means
practically the same thing in the way of work,
plus extra kudos and salary. Dr. Booth
says, and she is not the only University
woman to say it, that professional prizes
open to both men and women invariably go
to men in this State, although in other coun-
tries, including some of the Australian
States, women are far preferred before men
in work of the kind done by Dr. Halley. It
is a very antiquated, moss-grown, medieval
opinion to hold now. In some ways, our
own dear mother State is right away back
in the Dark Ages. “Now, be a good little
girl and don’t come bothering me to be a
school inspector or anything, and you’ll have
a bun and a dollie,” is the way in which
our officials talk to medical women.
Mr. Board believes in educating women
where his own feelings are concerned. His
daughter Ruby, M.A. of Sydney University,
is now a distinguished member of the Tilley
Institute in Berlin, a most up-to-date estab-
lishment, and one that stands out even in
that city of enlightenment, where education
is treated seriously. There Miss Ruby
did marvellous feats, studying for and pass-
ing in six months an exam, in the Ger-
man tongue which usually takes stu-
dents a year or more to prepare for.
The Tilley Institute is run by an Australian,
brother to Mrs. Donohue. It aims at teach-
ing German language and literature. _ Stu-
dents must board and lodge in the Institute,
and take a vow to speak, read, write, think,
dream, swear, in no other language than
German. Another Sydney girl now in-
carcerated willingly and to the tune of £3
per week therein, is Miss Annette McClellan,
who occasionally sends a pleasing screed to
the S.M.H.
The Dorset Men Beyond the Seas have a
strong contingent in our city. They had a
festive gathering lately on the Lady Hamp-
den. Miss Amor, Mr. J. Amor and Mr. Weir
made music for the party. Lady Chelms-
ford is patron of this society, which boasts
as its energetic hon. sec. Captain H. Caines
and Messrs. Edward Thorpe, Leonard Last,
and on the ladies’ committee Mesdames
Hare, Warren, Waygood, Ward, Brierley
Hunt, Caines and others, none of whom
could be classified by old Middlewick in
“Our Boys” as “Inferior Dossit.”
“Bella Donna,” the new play at the Cri-
terion on Saturday night, revealed Miss
Irene Browne in some clothes and a dust
and desert atmosphere recalling the best
suburban gardens in this present dry spell;
Mr. J. C. Williamson in his box;
and a bad man, Mr. Hugh Buckler, in even-
ing dress and a fez. A dusky Semite (I
suppose he was a Semite) in faultless even-
ing attire and a fez cap topping all gives a
thrill to the most hardened playgoer, for the
costume means something in playland. It
stands for a real scoundrel. The correct
black suit shows the outward culture and
polish of the Asiatic who has been at Eton
and Oxford, and swallowed the veneer of
Western civilisation at every pore. (I am
writing in haste, and if there is something
wrong about that, I can’t help it). Clap a
fez on his head, and you have his black,
hard, age-old savagery beneath it all. In the
present case, the man is an Egyptian, but
it’s just the same. The fez fits him all right.
Then he adds a cigarette loaded with opium
and other drowsy syrups, and he is the worst
ever. You know what to expect, and you
are not disappointed. The fezzed cigarette
smoker never goes back on his tracks, but
keeps straight on from crime to crime till
his path is strewn with dead, and you would
think a motor-car had just passed.
“Bella Donna” (for plot of which please
see “Sundry Shows”) has a most convincing
touch in the second act, a touch which every
dweller in sunny Sydney recognised as quite
local in color. The scene is a drawing-room
in Mrs. Chepstow’s villa in Egypt. A desert
wind suddenly arises. A door slams. The
lamp is blown out. You can often get the
same effects when a warm westerly blows
abruptly into a kerosene-tin shanty at Water-
loo. A woman who saw the play on Sat-
urday night, having witnessed the London
performance, with Geo. Alexander and Mrs.
Pat Campbell in the cast, declared that that
puff of wind made her feel homesick. They
don’t have much wind in London, not enough
to blow away the fogs.
“Revealed” is the right word to apply to
Mrs. Chepstow (Irene Browne) in the play.
She is the type which men always say women
describe as a “bold cat,” Mrs. Chepstow has
to take many tickets on herself as an ex-
tensive fashion displayer. But present
fashions are revealing, don’t you know. The
woman who saw the play in London de-
clared Miss Browne to be much more cov-
ered than Mrs. Pat Campbell, who, on a sur-
face measurement of satin to the square
inch, was very sparsely disguised. It-is the
fashion now to wear one leg outside the
skirt, and Mrs. Pat wore it so. Mrs. Pat,
whose eyes are still dazzlers, has grown
very stout. Her costumes in “Bella Donna”
would pass muster with a Bondi alderman,
as they were strictly neck-to-knee. but he
would not allow her to loiter on the beach
and’ take a sunbath in them. Miss Browne
was really clad. Her clothes covered her all
over.
These facts about “Bella Donna” at St.
James’s, London, were told me by Mrs.
Percy Hunter, who left Bullopolis in a pea-
soun fog on December 20, to return to An
ti-alia with her two boys, in preferencef
going for Christmas .to the chilly
Grindelwald. in Switzerland, whither tl
ski-ing, bobsleighing, skating and such '
drawing many Australians. Sometimes +£!
white drift of joy is 14ft. deep at Grinds
wald.
About the new fashions Mrs. Percy Hun
ter had much to tell me, but the worst nf
up-to-date fashion copy is that, unless it is
censored until it is, as news, valueless as 8
Balkan war cable, it is unfit for publication 4
“I saw some hair-raising styles in Hvde
Park,” she said. “There was a woman
there one day dressed in Gobelin blue satin
which was wranped as tightly round her
from her bust to her knees as a sausage
skin on a sausage. Over this she wore a
tunic of the same color ninon, which was
absolutely transparent, showing her chest
shoulders and arms. The skirt was split im
one side and her stockings were quite visible
to her knees as she walked. She was typi-
cal of the most advanced modes. Both in
London and Paris there was a craze for
grey hose —silk, of course. Nobody could
fail to see that they were grey, because
there was hardly any skirt covering them!
Sometimes the skirt buttons all the wav
down the back from waist to hem, and half
way from the hem upwards it is left unfast-
ened. Low-necked frocks are always worn
Collars are seldom to be seen, and in cold
weather furs of the most priceful descrip-
tion are piled on over flimsy ninon and lace
gowns.
The oft-quoted contrast between riches
and poverty in London, struck Mrs. Hunter
nowhere more forcibly than at Epsom last
year, when she went to the Derby with a
coach-load of Australians, including Mr. and
Mrs. Denison, Mrs. J. S. Brunton and
Harry Gullett. Many others “also ran.”
As a Society par. this is rather belated, but
as showing the cleverness and agility of the
London pickpocket, who, as Mrs. Hemans
would say, “has all seasons for his own,” it
is worth while. The party were all on top
of the coach, high up from the ground. Just
after the race, during which all eyes had
been fixed on the course, Mrs. Brunton
missed her bag, a velvet, gold-clasped affair,
containing much coin and some trinkets.
She had had it on her lap, and only lifted
her hand off it for a minute. But that minute
was enough for a prowler to leap on the
wheel and make a haul. Mrs. Denison had
a brooch snatched from her lace collar at
the same time. She was quite unconscious
of her loss, until in the fuss made in looking
for the handbag, somebody told her that her
lace was dragged away from its moorings.
The unerring hand of these invisible thieves
was what impressed the Australian women
most. “We noticed then the people who were
lurking around the coaches. Such awful
creatures they were, they made me
shudder, hardly human they seemed in their
utter filth and degradation. It was a sight
that brought the tears to my eyes, and made
me thank God we don’t have people like the
London submerged tenth in Australia.”
The King, gently but firmly taken in
charge by the Queen, was. at that Derby.
“George and the Dragon,” the Londoners
call our Sov. and his purposeful spouse.
Another bit of royal news purveyed by the
same traveller is that the Princess of
Prussia, only daughter of the Kaiser, a
gentle-looking girl of 18, is deaf and dumb.
This is according to common report in Ber-
lin. The same affliction sits on little Don
Jaime, second son of Albhonso of Spain.
Baritone Charles . Rolfe goes to Europe
in May. He proposes to spend a year or so
abroad, picking up hints from other song-
birds in foreign aviaries. The inevitable
complimentary concert will be hurled at him
some time in April, unless he. dodges very
actively or defends himself with the thick
end of his umbrella.
Kate Rooney sends a postcard, which deals
out a sepia-tinted nicture of the modern
uses to which the old Pump Room at Bath
has ascended. Here, where Beau Nash
once drank the waters and aired his finery,
the first orchestral concerts in England
were given in the 18th century. In January
Kate sang in this domed music reservoir, at
two concerts, all the Tschaikowsky Lieder.
The orchestra digressed into solid helpings
of Wagner in the intervals —and great was
the applause thereof. In Bristol the Sydney
contralto uplifted her organ tones at a Sun-
day concert, in the presence of 3000 per-
sons, who had obviously wandered away
from the evening collection elsewhere.
Pianist Florence Menkmeyer. who used
to be known here, frequently digresses into
song with considerable success. She has
been giving double-barrelled exhibitions or
her prowess in both arts to Parisian audi-
ences.
Concerning infantine faith in Santa Claus,
a London ha’penny paper has industriously
snapped the envelope a small child dropped
in a pillar-box. It bore the wild and whirl-
ing superscription : “To Dady Christines w
Heven Bank of Englend please put it down
the cleanest chimbley.” A callous G.PM
official marked it “insufficiently addressed.
Yours, pro tern.,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 17, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The select Kooyong gathering rejoiced
loyally, on Wednesday, when the Melbourne
team, headed by the G.-G., beat Camperdown
at polo. Caps soared into the air, and ex-
pensive parasols waved hilariously. Help
Burnham did a lot for his side ; while Secre-
tary Wilberforce, from State Government
House, and Ernest Bruce occasionally re-
minded the spectators that they were not
there merely to decorate the ground. The
Denman lady, clothed in white, drove her
own car to the scene at an early hour. The
Barttelot couple sat behind her as passen-
gers. State Governor Fuller, accompanied
by a pale blue lady, supplied applause at
appropriate intervals. Fair-haired Mrs.
Percy Chirnside petrolled down from the
Werribee manor house to watch hubby war
unsuccessfully for the Camperdown side.
The watching crowd included McArthurs,
Gnmwades, Staughtons and all the local
nobility.
The Denman lady intends, later on, to in-
vite owners of antique treasures to send
them along to an exhibition which will in-
clude her Ex.’s own hne collection. Her
chief hobby is collecting the odds and ends
left by past generations. She has a keen
eye of the intrinsic value of any old thing,
and is reputed never to make mistakes about
periods. Federal Government House is
scattered over with tapestries and other
ancient things harvested by her Ex. She is
convinced that there is a lot of good stuff
stowed away in Australia, and this she
hopes to tempt from its hiding places.
The little G.-G. looked pale and tired, on
1 uesday, when he journeyed to flat and
gritty Port Melbourne _ to farewell Auntie
I hipps. He was officially chaperoned by
Chamberlain Nevill. Quite half a dozen of
the Best People oiled or trained down to
wave to the pretty traveller, who was almost
completely obscured by a mass of flowers and
three golden-haired Fuller nieces frocked
gorgeously in purple. The Fuller nephew,
with hatless head, broiled in a vivid green
wrapping. Maggie Stirling’s girleen snap-
shotted Auntie and her group in a cosy cor-
ner of the deck. The voyageuse finally faded
away amidst a tangle of bouquets and
novels. All her friends seemed to have plun-
dered the bookstalls for their parting gifts.
The soldier brother in India, towards
whom Auntie Phipps is now steaming, could
not agree with the conclusions of some mili-
tary examiners, and there was, in conse-
quence, some doubt about the trip. How-
ever, at the last moment, a cable arrived
instructing her to come along, the warrior
having secured his bungalow for the pro-
jected six weeks’ visit. Perhaps the ex-
aminers saw their error and backed down
like those of a medical student I once knew.
Having come into violent collision with an
irritable old professor, over some trivial
point, my friend coldly remarked: “Well,
sir, that may be your opinion. It isn’t
mine!” The professor reeled in his chair.
Then he busied himself with his papers, and
muttered in a choked voice: “Well, well! I
suppose I’m wrong as usual.’’ The grudg-
ing apology pacified our fourth-year friend ;
but when the lists came out, he was annoyed
to find that the foolish and vindictive old
gentleman had omitted to pass him.
Motors whirred round town on Monday
morning, carrying the newly-arrived Empire
Trade Commissioners to hospitable enter-
tainment. The three days’ rest before the
departure of the Maoriland boat was to be
filled in with a round of receptions and
amusement. Serious work begins when they
return. Federal Government House dined
them on Monday evening; Tuesday was
occupied with motor trips, and a theatre
party in the evening; and the University
booked Wednesday up to the last moment
before the boat cast off. Chairman Sir
Edgar Vincent gained a few extra hours by
expressing overland from Adelaide. He
secured a bed at the grey-towered Govern-
ment House. With him came Mr. and Mrs.
Tom Garnett, who motored right away to a
family party with daughter Mrs. Geoffrey
Syme. The rest of the visitors arrived in
more leisurely fashion by the Medina, on
Monday morning.
Emma Sugden, daughter of the master of
Queen’s College, was married last week in
England to Pierre le Couteur, cricketer and
Rhodes scholar. The young man has been
to Oxford, and is to fill a chair at Perth
University. Emma was one of the first
pupils of the University Conservatorium.
She can work both ’cello and violin with
facility, and must be accounted a good, all-
round fiddle sawyer. If she tried, she could
probably get a tune out of a crosscut saw
and an ironbark log.
Mrs. Colonel Sellheim lately gave a fare-
well party to Mrs. Legge, who goes to join
her colonel husband in England. He is
beginning a four years’ appointment as Aus-
tralian military representative at the Brit-
ish War Office. The energetic Australian
soldier lately expressed a wish that there
were 48 hours in every day, so I anticipate
there is plenty of work. Mrs. Uegge, a cool,
auburn-haired, brown-eyed vision in white
lace, seemed very concerned about taking her
10-year-old son away from Melbourne
Grammar School. Mrs. Ferguson, Mrs.
Legge’s mother, joins the departing party.
She hails from Sydney, and, for this cere-
monious occasion, inhabited a handsome
black lace frock. Mrs. Parnell, wife of the
V ictorian Commandant, shed smiles on the
gathering. Mrs. Pethebridge brought a
striking-looking daughter in blue. Mrs. and
Miss Wallace and Mrs. Coxon represented
their respective military men-folk. Mrs.
lork Syme spread purple draperies and
fashionable feathers in a window seat.
Hostess Mrs. Sellheim, who is used to the
tropics, and knows how to help people enjoy
life on a hot day, arranged a sit-down tea-
party. Mrs. Dodds, Mrs. Wanliss and Mrs.
Forsythe came along from Toorak. Pro-
fessor Elkington’s lady talked Bananaland
gossip with Mrs. Carroll, who hails from
the far north of the State, yet owns a peach
complexion. A string band played suitable
comic opera accompaniments to the conver-
sation.
Mrs. Geoffrey Syme is giving a party on
the 27th, to introduce her pretty mother to
Australian friends. Mrs. Garnett came out
with her husband, who is one of the six
members of the Trade Commission from
Great Britain. She intends to stay here
while the Commission progresses through
Maoriland, and will go to Sydney with Mrs.
Syme at Easter. The Commissioner will
take them both to the horse races that rage
over there at hot-cross bun time.
Melbourne last saw the dead explorer
Scott at a glittering mayoral ball, which was
held the night before his departure for the
Antarctic. He had every waltz with his
wife, who was very bright-eyed and flushed,
and seemed excited. He only spared time
from her for the necessary duty dances.
Mrs. Earle has just returned from a
sojourn with her brother, Dr. Pardy, of
Launceston. While she was on the Speck,
16 matrons of Launceston (how solid that
sounds!) gave a bridge party which knocked
feathers ofE anything the rival village of
Hobart had ever done. She did a good deal
of motoring over the smooth, convict-made
roads, and collided with the Gerald Buck-
lands in quite a friendly way at an awkward
cross road. The impact resulted in nothing
worse than a smashed lamp glass. The
Bucklands were then off to collect daughter
Helen, a bud of last Cup season.
Artist Bertha Merfield is just recovering
from a breakdown brought on by overwork.
Country air and a thoroughly lazy life have
set her up again, and she is now busily col-
lecting material for a show later in the year.
Bluie Atkins, an active member of the
Goodrest Atkins family, who has lately been
bidding for concert-platform fame in Tas-
mania, has added a diamond engagement
ring to her other assets. Senior-Master Kay,
of Scotch College (a. triple scholarship win-
ner), is the lucky giver. The engagement,
by the way, is a dead secret.
Colonel Stanley, who sailed by the Morea
to India, is on more than a health trip. In
his baggage is a commission from Defence-
Minister Pearce to inspect time-expired In-
dian officers and secure the most likely ones
for our new army. Stanley, who worked
his way up from the ranks, should know the
marks of a genuine warrior when he sees
them. The habitues of the Vienna and Col-
lins-street generally will miss his tall
familiar figure.
.The eyeglass and Asiatic turban of
Major Griffiths are to pass from Melbourne’s
sight in March, when the Anglo-Indian war-
rior, with his wife and polo sticks, will steam
back to his colored regiment. The dreadful
news has produced an exclamatory chorus,
tor everyone thought the nice exchange man
was to stay for at least another year.
Captain Buchanan, an attractive mer-
chant , service . sailor, was hitched up, last
ihursday, with Annie Mackinnon, second
daughter of squatter Godfrey, of the Corrie
clan. The joining was celebrated by a
strictly family circle gathering at Cousin
Angus Macinnon’s place in Toorak.
The • love of some women for a warship
passes all unuerstanding and meets it coming
back. A cheerful young widow who re-
volves in squattocratic circles had arranged
a party on board a visiting ironclad, then
lying in the stream. The appointed day
arrived, and with it a gale. Hut the widow
was undaunted, and duly arrived on the
windswept pier in a fetching rig-out which
displayed her silken ankles. A launch, with
an officer in charge, battled through the
wave tops and took off the shrinking party.
Half-way to the ship, the whole shore crowd
tell sick. The gale started to whistle louder,
and by the time the ship was reached, they
found it was too rough to get alongside. But
the widow declined to turn back, and the
launch stood by while a cutter was lowered
and rowed across to them. Six visitors ner-
vously transferred themselves to its depths.
That done, the sailors “gave way” with a
will, and, as it shot alongside, the dangling
tackle was hooked on and brawny arms
hosited boat and all to deck level, where the
reluctant, skirted voyagers were politely
helped out by waiting officers and assisted
towards stiff whiskies and sodas. The re-
turn voyage, later on, was the same ordeal
reversed. Those adventurous females now
giggle and brag about the adventure, mur-
muring : “We had just the loveliest time, my
dear! You should have seen how the officers
fixed our skirts.”
People who love gossiping about other
people are going around with gloomy faces.
A divorce suit which looked most promising
is to go undefended, and the expected shocks
will not happen. Socially, the local atmo-
sphere at present is that of a power house
in which the main dynamo has just broken
down.
Just as this epistle is tearing to press
arrives the news by cable of Mrs. D’Arcy
Osborne’s marriage to Mr. Theo. Marks in
London. He is the big Sydney architect and
sportsman of that ilk, and she is one of the
N.S.W. Osbornes, but they are both as well
known in Melbourne as they are in Sydney.
He is Australia’s heaviest backer of horse's,
and I don’t think he has missed a Cup for a
decade, or more. She divorced her squatter
husband last year. They should be on their
way bactc to these parts shortly, as he has
the extensive job before him of superin-
tending the building of the Sydney Tele-
gkaph office.
A recent cool evening provided sufficient
excuse for a jolly telephone dance at the
club house near the Gisborne golf links. It
was a purely impromptu affair, and was
started by Mrs. Ross Watt, of Macedon.
Mrs. Oswald Syme petrolled over from
Woodend, and the Best Macedonians hurried
along with all the guests within their fences.
The supper consisted of what was left of
the cold fowl after tea, some fresh salad, and
as many bottles as would go into the motor
hampers. A pale young moon, just .waning,
lighted the dancers home in the early hours
of next morning.
The Women’s Hospital bazaar was lately
under discussion at a meeting in the council
room of the Town Hall. Mrs. Hennessy, in
neat navy blue and with mignonette in her
hat, sat at the head of the table, and let
M'‘ s - James, hon. secretary, do the talking.
Mrs. Hood, wrapped in tussore, and wearing
a serious air, sat at her right hand. Mrs.
Hughes and Mrs. Maloney occupied other
seats; and Miss Mackenzie, a wealthy lady
from Toorak, figured among the leading
spirits. Eager feminine helpers sat in rows
and drank in the remarks, now and then leap-
ing up to edge in a suggestion. Into this assem-
blage of women strode big Oscar Lyon, of
the Bank of New South Wales, who is act-
ing as treasurer. His boots were very yel-
low, and he wore a double-width smile. An-
other lone hero sat surrounded by ferocious-
looking hats. He was brave Mr. Carroll,
and those about me whispered that he had
undertaken to keep the books in order. The
British race need have no fear while it can
produce dauntless spirits of his calibre.
By way of helping the Women’s Hospital,
principal boy \ iolet Loraine is arranging a
song matinee for March 3. The matineers
intend to use the city fathers’ council room
m the Town Hall. The apartment seats
.>OO, and that leaves plenty of spare space
for the corridor lingerers who want to flirt
and whisper on the lounges, and still be
within earshot of the music. The dais,
which, on ordinary occasions, is occupied by
the Gaud Mayor’s chair, will be utilised as
a stage. There is also a crimson carpet, and
the panelling is of rich dark woods, so the
prettily froeked stars will have a choice
setting.
Gregan McMahon and his Repertory The-
atre people filled the Princess’s, on Saturday
atternoon, with Shaw’s “Man and Super-
man” The cautious McMahon has always
held that, for repertory work, one should
muster an audience before attempting to
yard it in a permanent theatre. He has
been patiently rounding up his intellectual
some time now, and yarding? it in
odd halls, waiting for the time when it would
be numerous enough to comfortably fill a
full-sized olayhouse. After a few more ex-
periments like that of Saturday last, he will
begin to see his own playhouse looming be-
fore him. The stalls were one mass of well-
groomed feminine heads, while the boxes
overflowed with feathers and frills. An odd
man showed his cropped head here and
there. Society filled the dress circle and
stewed amiably for the good of its brain
refreshing itself during the interval with ice-
creams and oranges. When a sudden shower
of rain tell through the open roof on to the
bare heads in the stalls, there was a merry
laugh all round the sheltered perch. One or
two family brollies blossomed on the floor
°f fhe theatre, before the half-moon slices
or sliding ceiling came slowly together and
shut out the T, '^ather.
On the stage, Maude Joliffe, as Violet
Robinson, and Isabel Handley, as Ann Whit-
field, stewed heroically in heavy furs during
an indoor scene. The skins were horribly
handsome, and simply had to be shown off •
yet in the next act, which takes place out of
doors, and so promptly that the seasons have
not time to change round, the pair clad
themselves in light summer raiment, and
went off tor a motor run wranned in thin
dust coats.

RUN on strictly Continental Lines, newly and luxuriously renovated, this
up-to-date Cafe provides you at all times with the Best in Luncheons,
Dinners and Suppers —a treat for every epicure.
The cuisine and dining-rooms are under the personal supervision of L. R.
HOSSEN-LOFF (Diplome d’Honneur, 1889-90), who, as manager, has firmly
established his reputation amongst people who know “how to live.”
The Kitchens are always open for inspection, and only the choicest of foods
are employed in the difficult task of providing an ever-attractive and varied bill
of fare.
The Courtesy and Enterprise of the Management are well known to all who
patronise The Savoy; and to give the ever-increasing clientele new pleasure, an
entirely up-to-date system of ventilation has been installed, providing on the
hottest days delightful, cool, fresh air, rendering fans and other draught-making
systems unnecessary.
A Splendid Orchestra adds pleasure to your visits by supplying selections of
the latest and most popular music, while special artists have been engaged to
entertain you during supper time.
Banqueting Halls and Private Rooms have also been added to this already
extensive establishment.
All that can be done has been done to ensure every luxury and comfort to
the patrons of the one and only “SAA OY.”

PAHI HOTELKEEPER BAD WITH COLIC.
Mr. Joseph Ryan, hotelkeeper of Pahi (N.Z.), was
very bad with colic, and tried many remedies, with-
out results. Then the Honorable Mrs. Scotland ad-
vised his wife to give him Chamberlain’s Colic and
Diarrhoea Remedy. He says: “I was sceptical and
refused to take it; I reckoned such things were no
good. At last I got so bad that my wife prevailed
upon me to try it. After two doses I was right, and
have been so ever since. I was in a bad way, I can
tell you, but now I swear by Chamberlain’s Colic and
Diarrhoea Remedy, and always keep it handy.”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
C. G. Wade, of N. S. Wales, informed the
Brisbane press that he “did not fear any en-
croachment on Australia from Japan, which is
too crushed financially through the war with
Russia to plunge into another conflict.”
Only a few years ago neither Russia, China
nor Korea had the slightest fear of attack
from Japan ; yet what happened ? Has
Wade ever read contemporary history—or
anything?
Freetrade Britain is a wonderful place for
the working man. The Labor party in the
House of Commons recently tried to fix 205.-
a week- —a whole quid every seven days-—as
the minimum wage of the udult railway em-
ployee. The Liberal majority sympathised
with the idea, but its hair stood on end with
terror at the size of the proposed undertak-
ing, and the subject was dropped. A 12s.
minimum might perhaps have been carried,
but 205., according to English ideas, was cal-
culated to stagger humanity.
THE FINAL VOLLEY.—A TRAGEDY
OF R.I.P.
Nazim Pasha was wounded in the side, and then
shot through the head while lying on the ground....
A public procession followed his remains to the in-
terment at the Suginanieh Mosque. All military
honors were rendered in order to show that Nazim’s
death was due to accident. —Latest version of a recent
happening.
Being a prophetic portrayal of the only kindly
and agreeable feature of a most regrettable
accident—the result of a slight territorial over-
sight which occurred about 19 2- A.D.
“Ruru,” from Maoriland :
Ever since a mob of Maori chiefs came together
and set their strange and unhandy marks to the
Treaty of Waitangi, there has "been a big idea in
the minds of Maoriland administrators that the brown
person should be saved by law from his own childish
innocence and the raoacity of white exploiters. Hori
Grey carried things further, and tried to conduct wars
without hurting the enemy much; a business which
the said enemy so greatly appreciated that he
exalted Hori to his Pantheon, and has ever since
worshipped his memory. The most important sort of
protection, however, that the Maori has been given
was the land legislation which prevented his selling
his reserves of soil for tobacco or beer. Now the
Massey crowd threatens to change all that. Minister
Ilerries told a deputation of native landowners at
Hastings that the Government would endeavor to
have “the same law for the pakeha as for the Maori.”
Which means that a rapacious mob of speculators
have still a new kingdom to exploit, and that Massey
will help them to its exploitation. Of course, the
Maori will cry “Kapai te new Act.’ Why should
he stick' to his land if it can be made to provide
whisky, and motor-cars with loud horns, and concer-
tinas and weedy thoroughbreds and more whisky ?
When he hasn’t” got anv more land to sell, and the
car is worn out and the whisky bottle empty, he
will learn in sorrow that he hasn’t got a darned
thing to live upon except the sweat of his brow. It
might be good for the Maori to have some sweat on
his brow, but he won’t like the moisture, and his
howl aaginst Massey will be great in the land.
The ten-sliilling note is to be on the Aus-
tralian market shortly in place of the dreary
old half-sovereign which you give away in the
dusk in mistake for a sixpence. Just here The
Bulletin would like to mention that the
ordinary Australian note is a clumsy con-
traption and should be altered. A note
isn’t intended to be a mustard plaster for
an elephant or a covering for a haystack.
The Philippine Government note —which,
by the way, is issued for as little as 2s. —
will go into a small pocket-book if folded
once, and the Italian note will go in an
ordinary-sized pocket-book without folding.
After a little experience with them the
affair that looks like a pamphlet seems ridi-
culous. It is also announced that the nickel
penny and halfpenny will soon be on deck,
and that as nickel is white like silver they
will he differentiated from silver coins of
the same size by having the edges “scal-
loped.” A much more effective device is to
have a blow-hole or breathing-hole in the
centre of the nickel, after the Belgian
fashion. That renders a mistake practically
impossible. Even the drunkest person can
tell a nickel from a franc by pouring beer
through the hole.
From a persistent Laborite: —
The recent N.S.W. P.L.L. Conference capped its
other foolishness by appointing an inquisitorial com-
mittee, with headquarters at the Trades Hall, to
summon Labor members and Ministers and catechise
them as to their doings in Macquarie-street. Confer-
ence will reassemble a little later to receive the
committee’s report. The inquisitor-general is Paddy
Clara, Jtvho performed so well himself that he failed
to hold the Labor constituency of Ashburnham
against a hopeless Tory like Eden George. It is safe
to predict that few M’s.L.A. will tie a millstone
round the neck of their dignity and sink themselves
to the depths of this tribunal, .in which case they
may be recommended for expulsion. In which case,
again, it will be interesting to see what will happen.
A Labor member who pleases his local league and a
majority of his constituents is liable to give soli-
darity a bad bump if solidarity doesn’t bring along
a little more horse-sense to its next meeting.
Customs Minister Tudor ought
to be hunting up replies to
hand to the numerous out-of-
work “snobs” among his Rich-
mond and Collingwood constitu-
ents who will soon be asking
him unpleasant questions. Prac-
tically every boot factory in the
district has either shortened
hands or is working short time
since the New Year. Imports
of boots and shoes into the Com-
monwealth jumpeeb £163.881 in
the year. The importer has got
a strangle-hold on the industry,
and is increasing the pressure
all he knows. Why, in the
name of common sense, a com-
munity should import £546,377
worth of goods which it is quite
competent to make, is the ques-
tion which the pseudo-Protec-
tionist Laborite must face. The
employees have been granted a
measure of Protection by the
Yic. Wages Board and the Fed-
eral Arbitration Court, and the
manufacturers have not. They
cannot pay the minimum wage
of 545., as against the for-
eigner’s average of from
14s. to 275. on an alleged protec-
tive duty of 30 per cent, ad
val. And that is all there is
about it.
Pi ■ime Minister Fisher, at a recent Mel-
bourne A.N.A. scrimmage:—
With regard to shipbuilding, I recall the sentiment
to which I gave expression in this hall at a similar
gathering when the demand was made: “What will
we do in answer to a declaration of war?” I replied
that bur answer would be the clang of 5000 hammers
on the anvils of our country.
But wouldn’t it be rather late for anvil-
clanging then? What is needed is some-
thing substantial in the hardware line now.
For example, the ring of 1000 hammers on
the dog-spikes, and the creak of 1000 span-
ners on the fish-bolts of the straight Oodna-
datta-Pine Creek transcontinental railway
would be something to go on with.
“Hamer”: While Fowler, M.H.R., was
having his innings over the Brother Chinn
case, Melbourne Paunch had a cartoon in
which King O’Malley was credited with ex-
plaining that the reason he wore. a beard
was because he was ashamed of his Chinn.
It wasn’t paralysing humor, nor in specially
good taste, even if Fowler had made his case
good, but as things have gone it looks as if
Paunch and Fowler and a lot of other
people ought to be ashamed of their face,
cheek and every other feature, including
their front generally.

For the Yass vacancy in the N. S. Wales
Assembly two candidates only will face the
by-election. Labor’s man is Gregory
MeGirr, born in Parkes (N.S.W.), who is
by way of being a chemist, farmer and
printer. He appeared for Labor at the Sen-
ate election of 1907, and made a valiant at-
tempt to biff out Fitzparrot, at Orange, last
State elections. Paddy Bourke, a stock
agent, is the Liberal hope and pride. He
was born in Yass and, if he can convince the
free and enlightened about nothing else, that
is something to go on with.
Maoriland is wearing
peacock’s feathers in its
tail because its trade re-
turns show that last year
its exports exceeded its
imports by £795,167 —im-
ports, £20,975.076; ex-
ports, £21.770,243. It is
a better result than 1911
showed, certainly, for
then the imports actually
exceeded the exports. All
the same, Maoriland had
better remember that the
interest bill on its public
debt alone is £2,400,000 a
year; and the exports
must exceed the imports
by that amount before the
Ricketty Islands can af-
ford to wear any feathers at all.
The Tas. Liberal caucus has decided
that “Jacky” Evans, one-time mariner and
five years Premier of the Isle, shall be the
next Speaker of the Assembly. The captain
is a C.M.G., or something of the sort, and
gets his opportunity by reason of the fact
that the K.C.M.G. who last held the job has
had enough of it to make him tired. Ancient
Jonathan Best, who lost his seat to Cameron
last April, and recovered it last month, is to
be the Liberal nominee for the Chairman-
ship of Committees. Both the new Speaker
and the new Chairman are liable to call
“Horder” on the slightest provocation; and
both will up’old the dignity of the ’Ouse
with heloquent, not to say ’orrid, persist-
ence.
STILL ON THE WALLABY!
The party was the same “going- concern” as it was
when Mr. Wade left three months ago on his health
trip abroad. — Wood, M.L.A. (N.S.W.).
THE HOMELESS ONE (bitterly): “Oh, yuss!
I'm alius goin', but it seems a bloomin'
long time GETTIN' anywhere!”
Last Monday, either by premeditation or
coincidence, the Sydney Telegraph and
Herald both broke out in defence of the
N.S.W. Government’s loan madness. The
Telegraph opined that even the borrowing
of £9,000,000 per annum wasn’t necessarily ex-
cessive, and considered that the chief danger
was that the Government might not borrow
fast enough, so that one loan might be ex-
hausted before the next one was raised. The
Herald was more cautious; still it held that
if N.S.W. hadn’t borrowed “we might still
have to be content with a railway as far as
Parramatta, or thereabout.”
This pathetic faith in the theory
that no country—not even one
that has dug out £600.000,000
worth of gold, can have any
money save what it bor-
rows from another, sets one
wondering what country Eng-
land borrowed from when it
'started building railways. Per-
sia perhaps, or Greece, or
Morocco. As a matter of fact
the Parramatta railway is an
unlucky illustration. New
South Wales paid off its earli-
est loans out of revenue. That
was before the great financial
madness struck the country.
And the amount it paid off out
of revenue was rather more
than sufficient to cover the cost
of the original Sydney-Parra-
matta railway. Just at the be-
ginning of things the Ma State
demonstrated that the building
of railways out of revenue was
possible. Then depressing asses of the Tele-
graph and Herald variety got hold of its
unsophisticated and bowl-like ear, and poured
evil counsel thereinto, and the awful results
are visible to this day.
Now it is the Attunga (N.S.W.) branch
of the Farmers and Settlers’ Association
which has mistaken the ravening Socialistic
tiger for an affectionate milch cow. At its
last meeting it decided that the time was
ripe for the State to “grant concessions on
the railways and provide tent accommoda-
tion for country dwellers who want a change
at the seaside.” The Farmers and Settlers’
Ass. is pledged now to oppose the Socialistic
Labor party; all the same, there is hardly
a branch that can open its mouth without
asking for another helping of the curse.
THE SHIRKER'S POINT OF VIEW.
....Members of Parliament are servants —servants
of their constituents, and, in a larger sense, servants
of their country.- D. Telegraph, pompously.
The newly-manufactured Laborite is securely tied
up, branded with the union label, and installed in
Parliament, where the zealous eye of the boss watches
him, always prying for any eccentric movement which
may suggest the necessity for overhaul, replacement,
etc., etc. —Same paper, scornfully.
THE UUNRELIABLE AND UNEMPLOYABLE ONE:
“Bah! You poor miserable slave! I'd
sooner be without a job than work under
orders like you! ”
“Rule Nisi” : Having assisted
to howl down the six-hours’-
graft-a-day proposal of the La-
boring Bloke in New South
Wales, I now desire to draw the
mob’s attention to the coves at
the State’s Law Bench. A
study of the position shows that
the Courts are shut up for a
third of the year, but not be-
cause they are not busy. Last
term didn’t finish the list, and
litigants, being already aware
that Easter will be around be-
fore the Courts get nearly
through the work in hand, are
howling for extra Judges. It
may be true that Judges require
longer spells than most men.
but it is nonsense to say that
they must have four months
holiday per annum. Nobody re-
quires four months’ holiday per
annum in fair conditions, and if the eminent
Horsetails that decorate the Bench are not
being fairly treated, it’s time they mentioned
the matter.
RIPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The- time is not yet ripe. —Any Liberal publica-
tion, any issue.
I sat up late some halting stanzas making;
The night wind came in little; fretful puffs;
Loose flooring boards, from ghostly footsteps quaking,
With stealthy creaks excused thfe housedog’s
“wuffs.”
The lamp burned low. . A bough with peaches laden
Rubbed on the woodshed roof when swayed the
tree;
It sighed, and squeaked, and rustled like the maiden
, Who falls unto the villain in Act Three,,
I thought: “The fruit is ripe enough for picking.
Was it this evening I saw boys run,
And wait, far up the street, their pink chops lick-
ing ?”
BANG! RUMBLE! BANG It sounded like a
gun!
Two heavy thuds upon the garden grasses ; . .
Then silence as before. With martial frown,
I settled on my nose my foggy glasses,
And mumbled to the larhp, "Two peaches down ! ”
My flagging nib, like some Pole-finder tracking
Across the paper’s blank and snowy waste,
AVent on its way, to little purpose blacking
A wav’ring line to Nothing in fierce haste. .. .
The Fist of Doom upon the woodshed thumping
Jerked up again my leaden, nodding head.
I heard more windfalls on the back lawn bumping.
“Yes, yes!” I sighed, “the time is ripe for bed.”
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
When Truffier went into the ring at Syd-
ney Stadium last week against Jimmy Hill, it was
the palpable intention of the Frenchman to knock
the Australian’s head off. All evidence furnished by
the set of his jaw and the wind-mill swinging of his
arms went to show that he was still in the same mind
at the close of round 20. But Hill had use for his
head, and what was much more important, made
much use of it, in avoiding the bull rushes of his
opponent. Again and again the Frenchman, who
looked much the bigger, though there was nothing
to choose between the men in actual weight, seemed
as if he must score a knock-out, but Hill was as slip-
pery as a piece of fugitive memory pursued by the
duns of remorse, and ducked, dodged, glanced,
skipped, side-stepped, did the orange-peel and
banana-skin act, and hung on in a fashion which
rendered every effort of the man who carried the
punch null and., void. In the intervals between his
fortunate escapes from sudden destruction he was
peppering Truffier’s countenance with light, little
pussy-taps which could not seriously hurt the French-
man, but piled up points against him, and bathed
him in gore. At last the final round arrived, and
referee Arthur Scott pointed to the Australian as
the winner, amid tumultuous cheering. It was an
interesting battle between a born fighter and a clever
and tricky boxer, who does not carry a punch, and
the boxer kept his end up with plenty of pluck and
determination. Once when the referee wasn’t look-
ing br something, Hill appeared to , hit the French-
man'rather low, but no foul was claimed, and prob-
ably the tap didn’t amount to a smite within the
meaning of the Act.
* * * *
The battle at Sydney Stadium between
Jean Poesy and Herb. McCoy had enough thrill
about it to satisfy any patron who is contented
with plain bash without the gore which
so often paints a well-fought contest. For 10
rounds McCoy had all the best of the struggle, and
Poesy took any amount of mild disaster about his
countenance. On the appearance of things the Vic-
torian was outboxing and outfighting his opponent,
and a section of the crowd began to look for an
unsatisfactory finish. It seemed odds on that the
Frenchman would lie down somewhere or develop a
broken hand or something, and once or twice he
seemed pretty groggy and tired. Still, the crowd
has a deep and abiding love for Poesy, and he had
plenty of supporters who still thought he might pull
through. He did. In the middle of the eleventh
round he gave them all that they were looking for,
and, incidentally, supplied McCoy with as much as
he required, and enough over to put him to sleep.
The Victorian dropped his guard, and the half-
beaten man opposed to him saw his opportunity, and,
suddenly waking up, waded in with a supply of soul-
stirring wallops which set the crowd yelling like the
hosts of Tophet when they had suddenly been rein-
forced with the flower of Attila’s army. McCoy hit
back with all the cordiality he could muster, but his
blows lacked the tremendous force of those of his
rival, and a right to the jaw sent him down and
relieved him of the necessity of further strenuous
effort. It was a good go while it lasted, and if the
whole 11 rounds had been like the last, the Stadium
roof might have required strengthening. Poesy’s
punch is a thing of great virtue, and such articles ap-
pear at the present moment to have a higher market
value in Australia than anything else we manufacture
or import. They even beat all the products of our
boundless natural resources.
The boxing business in Adelaide is down,
taking nine seconds at the very least. One leading
promoter has dropped it; one or two alleged fighters
have disappointed the public; and the newspapers,
desiring to do full justice to some very ordinary bouts
at the Exhibition, put in such realistic touches about
eves and teeth and gore, that the infernally strong
Wowsing element in Adelaide has persuaded the Gov-
ernment not to let that hall for the purpose any
more.
* * * *
As a race, the struggle on the Parramatta
River for the Australian Sculling Championship, be-
tween Jim Paddon (challenger) and Alf Felton
(holder) wasn’t great, but the title has been apt to
go cheaply these latter days. For a mile and a half
the race was a break-neck affair, and after that
Paddon pulled away and won with plenty to spare.
He had 200yds. lead at Cabarita, and increased it
to 400 at the finish, but he could have improved his
time (21min. 12sec.) if there had been any point
in doing so. The crowd was not too sportsmanlike
when Felton began to fall back, and dropped his
head on his chest. “Get on Felton, yer wet,” they
yelled to the well-licked favorite, who was bravely
struggling behind the giant, but Felton was done
and there was no hope of bustling him.
BIFF!
Australia shows the Southern Cross to France.
After a twenty-round argument Truffier
(France) is convinced of their beauty by Hill
(Australia).
James Paddon, the new Australian scul-
ling champion, is one of a family of burly watermen
from Evan’s Head, between Richmond and Clarence
Rivers (N.S.W.). In heavy boats, Paddon was well-
nigh unbeatable—winning nine races in nine efforts ;
and in the outrigger he gives the impression of being
the hardest nut to crack on the sculling horizon.
(This is a feat which can only be accomplished with
two sculls hinged scissors-fashion on a person with
a reach of arm like a comet’s tail, and the strength
of a good young bull elephant.) Paddon sculled
cleanly and evenly in his big race with Felton,
except about the mile post, where he had to exert his
enormous strength and splashed a bit. How the frail
craft contrives to carry his weight of brawn and
grit is a mystery which science must be left to ex-
plain at leisure; but the new champion, with plenty
of time before him —he is only 26 —may balance his
6ft. 4Rn. to some purpose on the sliding-seat before
he meets his Waterloo.
* * * *
“Ru”: Sculling matters are moving.
Pearce has got away after the world’s championship,
and the very last remark he made as he left was
something about leaving no stone unturned. As long
as he stirs the mud of the Thames up to some pur-
pose all will be well. Meantime Dick Arnst is anxi-
ous to get busy again, and the moment Paddon was
acclaimed as Australian champion he slapped a de-
posit into the hands of veteran Beach, and called
on the giant to defend his new-won title. There is
now a prospect of something like a real revival of
the sport, for there are four men in the game—
Barry, Pearce, Paddon and Arnst —whose wrestlings
upon the waters might call forth the enthusiasm of
the whole earth. Any one of the four might be
champion of the planet, and to lick any one of the
four any other of the four must row the race of his
life.
* * * *
“Clam”: The Trumper match settled the
claims as to who is the most promising colt of the
season. Young Jack Ryder, of Victoria, wins out as
the article. Moyes, of S.A., is perhaps a better bat,
and Massie, N.S.W., a more dangerous bowler, but
all round Ryder’s superiority is undoubted. In cold
fact his batting figures are better than Moyes’, and
his bowling better than Massie’s. Victoria badly
wants a first-class recruit, especially in the face of
the temporary eclipse of Ransford, and Ryder seems
Pkely to fill the bill. He is a batsman of the forcing
tvpe, and a fastish bowler, especially dangerous on
fiery wickets.
* * » *
Queensland’s first innings in the inter-
state match at Sydney Cricket Ground was mostly
Thompson and Thomson. Out of a total of 276 runs,
of which 27 were credited to sundries, C. Thompson
made exactly a century, and J. Thomson supplied 62.
Massie’s bowling (6 for 73) was moderately deadly
against the Northerners, and six out of the eleven
failed to reach double figures. N.S.W. responded with
483. Macartney went in when threp wicketo were
down for 123, and in his first hour’s batting had
knocked up only 25 runs. After lunch he began hitting
in great style all round the wicket, and parried his
score to 154, when Bolton stumped him off "Rowe’s
bowling. The innings was fautless except for two
very difficult chances. Tozer’s 78, n. 0., was the only
other performance of magnitude put up by N.S.W.
Queensland’s second innings was a sorry affair, though
again Thomson (the one without the p) shaped well
for a few brief minutes. The last wicket fell for 115
and the home team were left victors by an innings
and 92 runs. Massie captured four wickets for 33,
and Scott three for 47.
Charles G. Macartney, the big, little man
who plays the deuce with all sorts of bowling, on all
sorts of wickets, is easily the top-notch Australian
batsman of the season. He has belted the ball to
this effect: 125 and 96 v. S. Aus. ; 94 and 74* v.
Vic.; 91 and 10 in the Trumper benefit, and 154 v.
Queensland—a total of 644 runs for six completed
innings, which yields an average of 107.33. In
many of the matches Macartney almost threw his
wicket away in his ardent desire to smash, burst or
otherwise destroy the pickets of the Sydney Cricket
Ground; and it is probable that his very tall average
might have been still more elongated had the ques-
tion of averages been uppermost in his cricket soul.
* * * *
In Sydney grade cricket last Saturday
there was some respectable scoring. T. H. Foster,
E. P. Barbour and H. Cranney all reached three
figures and over, while J. A. Randell (eight for 48)
and J. F. Bowden (seven for 50) made the best
showing with the ball. Middle Harbor put up 141,
to which Glebe responded with 131. Uni. reached
288 against Sydney, and the representatives of the
place where the landlord is glad had only time to
knock together 119 for a pair of wickets. Balmain
failed miserably for 87, and Central Cumberland, in
reply, compiled 231 for four wickets. Seven Red-
fern batsmen accounted for 290 runs off the Pad-
dington bowling. Against Gordon’s 134 Burwood
made 123, and the men from Wade’s constituency had
two wickets down in their second innings, at the
close of the day, for 48. Waverley responded with a
poor 120 for nine wickets to Petersham’s 264. North
Sydney still leads in the competition, but Uni. and
Redfern have a sporting chance of pulling off the
premiership. On Saturday the seat of learning was
short of some of its best men, who were playing in
the inter-State fixture.
* * * *
A couple of cricket performances worth a
corner among local records happened last week in
N.S.W. At Bathurst batsman Edwards smote 100
(retired) out of 126 —which left little limelight for
the balance of the team. The other effort was that
of a lad named Harris, who caught seven batsmen
out of the 10 in an innings.
* * * *
The ninth round of Vic. Pennant Cricket
commenced on Saturday, and the —er—monsoonal
disturbance of the previous night mostly spelt vic-
tory for the man who won the toss. In every match
the lucky coin-spinner put his opponents in, but
several of these got out in time to put the others in
and out also, on the sodden wickets, so their hopes
depend on next Saturday’s weather, and that lies on
the knees of the gods as yet. The chiefest encounter
was between the leaders in the competition, ’Roy
and ’Varsity, and the former, being sent in, acquired
the distinctly moderate total of 99. Then the
Students started. The wicket had grown worse, and
with seven down for the awful total of 29, ’Varsity
declared, in the hope of breaking the back of the
’Roy batting, while the game was still all in favor
of the bowlers, and having their own second turn
on better ground next Saturday. Little success
attended the strategy, for ’Roy contrived to scrape
51 for the loss of three wickets, and on present ap-
pearances ought to make good. The third-place
team, ’Wood, rushed North through the mill for 84,
but found the conditions a little better when it took
the bat itself and contrived to gather 108 for six
sets of timber. Northcote polished off Richmond
for the painful total of 43, and, with four wickets
down for 128, made fairly sure of its first victory
this season. Essendon managed to put together a
moderate 125 before its bowlers began skittling
’Pran, and then they couldn’t do as much as they
hoped, their opponents making 98 for five wickets.
’Don hasn’t won a game this season, but ’Pran has
only won one, so there is little satisfaction to either
whichever comes out on top. East, against South,
made a genteel 142, but as inter-Stater Baring
scored 93, not out, his 10 mates did little to brag
about. Of course, the bowlers got all the limelight
or laurels —Plant, of ’Cote, five for 31, and Lugton,
four for 11 ; Hartkopf, four for 30; Cannon, three
for six; Rogers, of ’Pran, six for 50 ; Pitcher, of
’Wood, four for 31, and Scott, of South, four for
28, being in the forefront. It was a day to test
a batsman, and Baring, aforesaid, withstood the acid
like pure metal.
Ward Prentice, who kept wickets for
N.S.W. in its struggle with Queensland last week, is
a good, all-round sport. He captained the Rugby
Union band which roamed through America last
year, and was also part of the tail of the Wallaby
team which fell heavily on the brisket of J. Bull a
couple of years ago. R. J. Massie (N.S.W.) S.
J. Fennelly (Q.) have also won their rep. caps in
the game of the hoof as well as in cricket.
Over £l7OO was taken at the gates and
on the ground during the Trumper benefit match at
Sydney. Probably the gross amount of the fund will
reach £3OOO.
* * * *
A case which has been. interesting the
“sports” has at last moved on a stage. A Sydney
bookmaker had an agent in the Randwick Tramway
Sheds, who, according to the evidence, accepted a
bet from three employees there on the double, Epsom
and Metropolitan, run at last Spring meeting of the
A.J.C. The double came off, and, in due time,
Noses was presented with the ticket and asked to
cough up £250. He did not seem overcome with
satisfaction, and, at the time, kept the ticket as
well as the boodle. Next day he handed back the
(or a) ticket to the bettors, who remonstrated that
it was a “ring-in.” Noses replied that it was, but
that the “ring-in” was their funeral. The upshot of
the argument was the arrest of the three punters on
a charge of conspiracy. ' Now a Quarter Session jury
has returned a verdict of not guilty, on the ground
of inconclusive evidence. Part of the £250 will go
to pay the costs of the defence —provided, of course,
the bookie decides to part with it.
* * * *
Racing at Caulfield last Saturday indi-
cated that the gees from N.S.W. are likely to make'
small potatoes of Victoria’s best at the V.R.C. Autumn,
although some of Vic.’s best were certainly not out
for glory on this occasion. For instance, The
Parisian, Ireland and Flavian ran unbacked for the
St. George Stakes, wherein Cider emphasised the bad
luck of his V.R.C. Derby defeat by beating Wolowa
at even weights, the distance (nine furlongs) being
all in favor of the latter. In the Bond Cup (if
furlongs) Bari os and Didus were too good for the
local opposition, which included Wallalo, Uxbridge
and Golden Point. Sprinters trained at Caulfield and
Flemington did no better in competition with visit-
ing horses. Burri took the Oakleigh Plate a bare length
ahead of Mala and Borrosov, who dead-heated for
second nlace. Burri mav be counted out of the New-
market Handicap, so Mala, on the Oakleigh showing,
has a chance to repeat his victory of three years ago,
but Borrosov will meet him on lib. better terms
anyhow. And there are others from the Sydney side. If
Popinjay is as ripe as his stable companions Cider
and Barios, he will have a great go for the six-furlong
flutter up the Flemington straight on March 1.
* * * *
The Futurity Stakes next Saturday should
prove a true trial gallop for some of the Newmarket
fancies, as it was last year. Popinjay, though
penalised, is not out of the hunt, but if Eudorus be
in his Spring form, or better, he will carry the
' opes of all who know what a flier he is when the
spirit moves him.
* * * *
It doesn’t appear at all likely that aged
horses will make history or cash or anything re-
markable in the coming Doncaster and Sydney Cup
events at Randwick. Of 110 prads in the Doncaster,
only half a dozen are over six years, while of 88 in
the Cup only four have acquired an equal amount of
antiquity. Evidently the old horses are considered
in their proper place on the macadamised highway,
or the sandy ruts that the shire council doesn’t
member to fill in, while the young ’uns have a more"
and more complete monopoly of the green sward and
the company of Moses.
Australia has promptly sent along a chal
lenge to England’s mighty tennis players to stand mi
and defend their right to the Davis Cup which thev
have just won. The battle is to be fought some
where about the end of the Fogland summer. Cham
pion Norman Brookes cannot go, but the Maorilander
A. F. Wilding, who fought side by side with Brookes
in the early struggles for the Davis utensil, is Dre
pared to lend a hand if needed. The choice of the
Australasian team is likely to give the selection
committee a deal of worry and anxiety.
* * * #
Adelaide tennis season, closing early
showed the tightest finish on record. Parkside (Roy
Taylor, Reid, Geoff Thomas and Homburg) had been
unbeaten till the last day ; Hyde Park (R. V. Thomas
Whitelaw, Mitton and Ponder) had lost to them only’
Then the two met for the return match. It was
three rubbers all, so sets had to be reckoned, and
Hyde Park had nine to eight. So the match counted
as its property, and the two clubs tied on the season
Then the whole season’s play had to be raked over
and Hyde Park proved to have won 45 rubbers to the
other’s 44. The win is popular, Parkside or Glen
Osmond (bad 3rd this time) having held the pre-
miership for years past.
* * * *
A light, unsteady breeze shook itself
through the atmosphere, or shook the atmosphere or
something over that part of the Pacific Ocean nearest
Sydney, when eight yachts strove in last Saturday’s
ocean race for the Basin Cup. Rawhiti pulled off
the event, with Culwulla 111. second and Thelma
third. Sayonara carried away her bowsprit and had
to drop out of the race, or the finishing order might
have been more keenly contested. As it was, allow-
ing for the handicap which the winner had to con-
cede to the second craft, the victory was only by
3min. 12sec., with 17min. 39sec. separating second
and third.
If Oscar Dickman, the one-time profes-
sional State swimming champion and holder of
the world’s pro. record (24min. 34 4-ssec.) is
a sport, and as such will take a little use-
ful advice, he will lay hold of the man who
manipulated the megaphone in Saturday’s League
Swimming Carnival at Sydney, and tread very
heavily on his ear. Dickman set out in the mile
race to lower his own figures. At 220yds., he was
3sec. inside his time, and evidently in good health.
At 1320yds. he dropped level with his time, and the
megaphone reminded the crowd that Dickman had in-
fluenza. When he got behind his time, the influenza
became serious (so said the megaphone), and when
he got home 30 sec. to the bad, the megaphone
poured forth excuses, either on behalf of Dickman or
on his own responsibility. For the sake of Dickman
let’s hope it wasn’t on his behalf. Anyway, he won
the championship with about 150yds. to spare.
Misses Tate and .Hooper dead-heated for the 50vds.
ladies’ championship, and Miss Tate got a question-
able decision over Miss Meads for the diving. C.
Scott, of R. and C., didn’t leave the blokes diving
in doubt. The 500yds. teams’ championship, after
much excitement and admonition to “Siddown in
front,” went to Balmain. Last year’s winner, Waver-
ley, was second.
* * * *
Longwortli continued his gathering of
Australian swimming championships at Hobart last
week. In the middle of the week he tore from his
rivals the 220yds., and on Saturday he left them all
behind in the mile. He has succeeded in hanging on
to every “free style” championship of N.S.W. and
Australia which lie collected last year.
At Ipswich (Q.) Frank Springfield burst
the previous Australian three-mile swimming record
by covering the distance in 70inin. 40sec. Although
he went through the water faster than anybody here-
about had ever gone before, his performance was not
great enough to win the race. Which presents a
problem that handicappers might lie awake and think
about.
* * * *
T. E. G. Smith, secretary of the newly-
formed Glenbrook and Nepean District Swimming
Club, has been interesting himself in a project which
may bear fruit or grow up to be famous or some-
thing. The Olympic swimming tests are to take
place in fresh water, and when the world’s cham-
pions gather in Australia the Nepean River will
offer, within reasonable distance of Sydney’s con-
gestion, a quantity of liquid of the desired sort, or
near enough to the desired sort to pass for the
genuine article. Long-distance swimming is a fairer
thing if conducted in open water where little or no
turning is necessary, and it is argued that three-mile
races and big breast-stroke struggles would be higher-
class events if decided in the broad, long, and some-
what fresh waters of the Nepean. There may be
something in the idea, and it is at any rate worth
consideration by those who have charge of arrange-
ments, and want the swimming branch of sport to
develop the best that is in it.
* * * *
In order to conduct with perfect success
that appalling waste of human energy, known as a
six days’ cycle race, it is considered absolutely neces-
sary that the thing should be run on a small lap
track under a roof. Bikarena, Limited, is out after
some such innovation for Sydney, and Alf O’Brien, who
directs the concern, has been holding earnest conver-
sations with Rosseau about the shape the track and
building should take. The idea is to put a swim-
ming bath and skating rink in the centre, and
arrange the snectators in three tiers all round. Then,
if the weather is cool, those who are dead sick of
watching the everlasting procession of wheels, can
do a little skating, and if the evenings are uncom-
fortably warm they can get into the water and keep
down their rising temperature while they occasion-
ally cast an eye in the direction of the riders.
* * * *
In view of the six-days’ cycle race to be
held in Melbourne next month, on the Exhibition
track, it is interesting to note how the mileage was
eaten up at the recent event of the same brand in
New York. Rutt and Fogler (the German-American
team) won with a total of 2661 miles. At the end
of the first 24 hours, 532 miles had been left behind;
at 48 hours, 998 miles; at 72 hours, 1445 miles; at
96 hours, 1874 miles; and at 120 hours, 2299 miles.
And through all this appalling whirling, maddening,
perspiring ride, J. Clark, the Australian rider, pushed
his wheel into third place, only a length behind the
winners. Other Australians at the finish were Hehir,
fourth, and Grenda, fifth.
* * * *
Paul Didier, a French racing cyclist, has
succeeded in flying a distance of 10ft. on a bicycle
fitted with planes, rising more than Bin. from the
ground. Didier got £BO for the stupendous per-
formance, which, after all, was rather a hoD than a
flight. Seeing that the record long jump is ° v "
24ft., it is pretty certain that Didier could have
flown much higher and about twice as far, if he haa
left the cycle out of the proposition. All the same,
if bikes can be taught to jump, a few steeplechases
might add much-needed zest to cycling sports.

THE FALLEN GLADIATOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where salt sea wavelets kist
Australia’s sandy toes,
I met a pugilist
With plaster on his nose,
A man of selid fist
Whose eye wa3 like a rose.
The day was bright and clear,
The air with pleasant sound
Blew sand into my ear,
And whirled my whiskers round;
And people far and near
Sat on the cheerful ground.
The person with the eye,
Whose nose had known repair,
Heaved a tremendous sigh
And swallowed quarts of air.
He said: “I cannot lie,
He licked me fair and square.
“Within the hempen ring
He showed his hairy might.
The people came to fling
Roses at me that night,
To hear my straight left sing
A lay for their delight.
“Sweet is the young lark’s song,
And sweet the sounding sea,
Young Clara’s voice is strong—-
But what are these to me ?
The punch that wrought me wrong
Held richer melody.
“He got all over me
With wallops hot and sweet,
He gave me stars to see
And slabs of dirt to eat,
And very frequently
He bashed me off my feet.
“Alas, for mortal hopes!
Alas, for fistic pride,
When one large punch elopes
That erst was deified ! ——
He chased me round the ropea
And scarified my hide.
“I went to sleep at last,
Such sleep as valor knows,
Amid the red gore cast
From a dismantled nose;
The count of ten was past
The war was at a close.
“And now beside the sea
I wander all the day,
And feel the loud winds free
Blowing the sand away,
And oft on bended knee
To various gods I pray.
“With frequent words and vain
I ask them to restore
That gift of tempered grain
Which nerved my arm before,
That I may find again
The splendid punch I wore.”
The day was bright and clear,
The air with pleasant sound
Blew sand into my ear
And whirled my whiskers round,
And at my feet a tear
Splashed on the heedless ground.
The person with the eye,
Whose nose had known repair,
Heaved a tremendous sigh
And swallowed quarts of air.
He said: “I cannot lie,
He licked me fair and square.”
N.S.W.

A recent statement of Hume Cook’s tliat
“while Protectionist United States imported!
goods at the rate of £3 6s. 9d. per head,.
Freetrade Britain imported at the rate o£
£l5 Os. 6d. per head,” moved one Freetrader
to exclaim hysterically in the Argus that
“the inference drawn was absolutely vicious,
and a lot more to similar effect. The obvious;
inference drawn was that America, desiring;
to see its citizens employed, kept as much!
work to be done in the country as it could,,
while Britain gave the foreigner at least 11-
worth per head of work that it might have
done itself. The inference that Freetrader
drew, however, was that it was intended to.
show that Britain is getting deeper into,
bankruptcy every year, wherefore he pro-
ceeds to quote Lord Avebury’s figures (from
his work on Freetrade), declaring that,
though, from ’95 to 1900 Britain imported
an average of £180,000,000 worth more than
it exported, yet it simultaneously earned an
average of £90,000,000 by its ships and an-
other £90,000,000 as interest on foreign and
colonial investments. That,, of course, im-
plies that the Cold Land is still solvent,_ but
it in no wav afiEects the fact that it might,
notwithstanding, have kept £l2 worth per
head of work for its own people instead of
giving it- to the foreigner. Which lesson
applies with still greater force to Australia,,
which hasn’t any foreign sea carrying trade
worth mentioning, and whose foreign invest-
ments wouldn’t buy a week’s refreshment for
the; present population of the Northern Ter-
ritory.
How these absentee landlords do howl! One
of the most vigorous kickers against the
absentee clauses of the Federal Land Tax is
Howard Spensley, whose father, an old-
time politician in Vic., had the good luck to
marry a Miss Staughton, of the squatto-
cracv, and got the Royal Arcade in Bourke-
street as a wedding present. The Arcade
yields a princely income, which has a ten-
dency to become kingly as leases fall in and
the incessant demand for rooms for small
tradesmen near the shopping centre of Mel-
bourne grows more insistent. Spenslet
condemns the wickedness of the r ederal
Land Tax all the while he is asking bigger
renfs. The absentee clauses of the tax
vielded only £62,757 during 3911, or a mere
fleabite of 44 p.c. of the whole tax. During
the same period the customs preference to
goods of British manufacture was worth
£1,070,823 to Howard Spensley and other
Britishers. Yet this petty sum of £6A<s<
is used by the ungrateful absentee as an
argument why people should not invest in
Australian dividend-paying stocks, and why
immigrants should avoid the country as
though it were a pest-house.
