A line from “Rosna” in London ;
The most fascinating thing at the Children s Wel-
fare Exhibition, at the Olvmpia, is a Magic City,
built bv E. Nesbit, England’s best and brainiest
writer for kiddies (and a near relative of[Adelaide s
KC Paris Nesbit, by the way). This Magic City
grew out of the exhibitors’ book called by the same
name, and has developed a certain dark educational
significance in the new craze for games to stimulate
the infant imagination and enterprise. It is built ol
all the things an average youngster can commandeer
| from an average home, from the kitchen pepper
caster to the drawing-room ornaments. Thus the
pillars of the Grecian Temple are made of a pennv
broomstick, painted white and cut in lengths- the
corner stone of the Elephant Temple is a Hf e 0 f
Johnson, and its marvellous carved gateway is J
old ivory fan. Brass and silver candlesticks make
gorgeous pillars, and an inverted spittoon and brass
bowl a round tower of great magnificence. The tomb
in the desert consists of a kitchen mortar, two pud
ding basins and an inverted egg-cup; while the
Silver Temple is built of kitchen tins. I don’t haD
pen to know whether every ma will give every pre
cious pet the run of her ivory fan, and I have an
idea that cook will give notice if she has to hunt
more than once for the pepper pot among the ruins
of a temple ; but the interest in the exhibition sue-
gests, anyway, that the modem precious pet j s
having a good time. She (or he) may not be a
numerous pet; but what there is of her (and him)
is made to feel glad it came. '
There has been a tremendous row over in
Tasmania about a tombstone. After the holo-
caust in the Mount Lyell mine a semi-pubii c
monument was put over the grave of one of
the victims, and thereon was carved the
dreadful words, “Sacrificed on the Altar of
Dividends.” Authority in the Queenstown
mining camp stood it for a few weeks. Then
the mining warden, or some other potentate,
ordered the obnoxious words to be scored
out, and the local council said “Hear, hear.”
“May be true,” remarked one alderman, “but
think of all the shocking allegations it might
lead to.” That s* so. If the habit of tell-
ing the truth on tombstones was not nipped
in the bud, so to speak, the alderman of
Queenstown might yet read over the grave of
a victim to typhoid: “Killed through the
neglect of the town council to mind its own
business and attend to the drains.”
“0.K.” : A few days after the production
of “Man and Superman,” at a London
theatre, Bernard Shaw prepared a list of
questions to be distributed amongst the
audience in circular form :
Are you aware that you would get out of the
theatre half an hour earlier if you listened to the
play in silence?
Have you noticed that in all good plays tears and
laughter lie very close together, and that it must be
very trying to an actress, who is endeavoring to keep
her imagination fixed on pathetic emotions, to hear
a burst of laughter at something she is supposed to
he unconscious of ?
Have you noticed that people look very nice when
they smile or look pleased, but look shockingly ugly
when they roar with laughter, or shout excitedly, or
sob aloud? (And so on.)
The last-quoted question seems the most
likely to induce silence in the audience and
thus* make Shaw happy—if silence is what
he wants. But there are other questions
which might be submitted to Shaw himself.
Is he aware that an audience that is quite
convinced and wholly enthralled by the
realism of a stage-play does not interrupt
the performance by applauding, roaring with
laughter, or sobbing aloud? The fact is
that devoted barrackers for intellectual
comedy can’t see a subtle joke, or perceive
a deeper meaning without proclaiming their
mental alertness in an aggressive manner.
And it may be that if they are not suffi-
ciently “held” by the pathos of a scene to
refrain from guffawing at a ludicrous casual
incident the dramatist is to blame. The
audience at a funeral doesn’t laugh aloud if
the undertaker’s tall bat happens to fall
from his grasn and roll into the open grave.
It smiles noiselessly. The real mourners
don’t even smile.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
H. Schutz: Don’t know who discovered or
first explored Australia. There is an ancient wooden
globe in Paris which says that Terra Australis was
found in 1499; but Marco Polo, somewhere about
1300, referred to Java Major, and he probably meant
Australia. The Dutch did some exploration on the
north and west coast in the early years of the 17th
century. But probably some long-bearded Arab
pirate stumbled on it in the name of the Prophet
centuries before that. Dampier came along in 1688
and Captain Cook lobbed in during 1770... .F.N.L.:
You had better consult a reputable patents agent;
one or two tell you in this paper where they hang
out their signboards.... F.L.Y.: Well enough writ-
ten; but the subject's done to death.... Buckley:
Better write to the Agricultural Department for
that information.... Hori Hori: Now, is it Anglo-
Chinese, Anglo-Maori or Anglo-Aborigine ?... .Ulti-
mus: Snake full of live mice? Phoo! Now, if you
had said live alligators we. might almost have dis-
believed y0u....01d Teacher: Take your word for
it that not one of the six inspectors appointed by the
S. Aus. Director of Education comes from Moonta;
also, that no headmaster of any big Adelaide school
was transferred from that dreary region. But even
that doesn’t get rid of the other correspondent’s
allegation that discontent is rife in the service. . . .
Aiwa Den: You take a quip too seriously.....
Eurowie: Mere statement that you knew a man who
suffered from moon-blindness doesn't add a peanut
weight to the general shortage of information on the
subject.... Tym : Tired transcriptions of daily-paper
pars, are no good to us....Kedge: Thin and hag-
gard little yarn, its plot having been so dreadfully
overworked... .C. 0.: Dunno for certain; but quite
likely we misread part of that writer’s screed. .. .
E. Jackson: Insinuate that you stole the poem? Cer-
tainly not! We merely insinuated that it wasn't a
poem... .Jonquil: No use sending us large, solid
slabs hewn from English papers. “Gallefy” will be
dealt with in due course. .. .Sir 0: “Bush Bloke”
fairly ingenious, but not well enough done; and
don't, care for the idea, anyway.... G.C.Y.: The
matter has had a par. already ; and your verse isn't
good enough to justify double-banking.... Conn:
Don't share your enthusiasm re “Mvola" as a
Federal capital. It is the sort of pretty-pretty little
name that the boarding-school miss confers on the
heroine of the immortal work of fiction of the
moated grange variety, which she secretly writes in
her bedroom by candlelight. And when she is
found out and spanked, and the immortal work is
confiscated, it is a fitting end to Myola. .. .Jessie
S. Litchfield: The reason why your various verses
have been steadily rejected is that, though the versi-
fication is correct and pleasant, the sentiment in
them all is trite. All you have to say is ; an echo of
something already better said G. Washington:
Quite right, and “Pariah” was quite wrong; Colac
isn't, and never was, on the Gippsland line.... Bo-
njour: Quite hopeless. .. .The Hager: In enumerating
the infamies you have witnessed in foreign parts, you
say, “This scribe has tasted the sweets of the Moulin
Rouge and Folies Bergeres by starlight and under a
cloudless sky.” When The Bulletin was in Paris,
the other day, the Moulin Rouge was an imitation
mill which did not admit the starlight, no matter
how cloudless the sky, and the Folies Bergeres was
a stoutly-roofed music-hall. These things make us
suspect that you are a bogus Rager, and that your
experiences have been much less dreadful than you
represent.... Benno: The one-time celebrity you in-
quire after went under long ago. He looked on the
wine when it was red, and on the ruin when it was
blue, and on the lady when she was golden about the
hair, and now he is dead K.G.: Disquisition
about the alleged alarming increase of weather dis-
turbances in various parts of the world carefully
noted. This writer’s theory is that somebody—not
necessarily J. D. Williams—has found out a method
of making glass bricks out of atmosphere, and is
consequently using up the air at a rate which is
bursting all calculations. The best thing would
be to hunt out the miscreant, and dissolve his store
of bricks. .. .Ken New: “Up the river” is something
in faint pencil on tinted paper. It may be worth a
lot: but we reckon our eyes are worth more. So
back it goes. ... J.G.M.: Received, one stamped en-
velope with “Cock-a-doodle-doo” on it and no further
explanation whatever. The stamp will come in
handy, but for the cock-a-doodle-doo we have no use
whatsoever. Beside, we never doodle in office hours.
. ...Nimos: This paper has to try to interest the
man in Thursday Island and the woman in Albany.
Do you think the small talk of any in-between ham-
let would interest those two people? H.M.K.:
“Freedom” is neat, but monotonous and strictly
Tory. Why not gladden the heaving bosom of the
Australasian with it?... .Omega: Clumsy work....
F. : Verses floated out. . . .R.F.8.: They break-
no new ground.... Skiralugh: Another bard swooped
down on that topic. Yours wasn’t much of a swoop,
anyhow. .. . J.S.H.: We observe you throwing yourself
round and trying to rhyme “scores” with “more.”
Get up and slap the dust out of your garments. . . .
8.P.C.: They would have a better chance. .. .N.S.:
Very crude.... Glen W.: As a topic, the Prince of
Wales’ bagpipes were exploded last week. .. .Errant:
Some promise, but your work is rough. Practice
may help, but we decline to issue any prophecies.

Public Opinion and
Beale High-grade Pianos.
The gieat majority of our Pianos are sold on the personal recommendation of users, and we are constantly receiving letters from our
customers praising the Beale High-Grade Pianos. Our instruments are in use in every part of Australia; in Hew Zealand, Fiji, Samoa, New
Caledonia, Mauritius, South Africa, India, and on the Continent of Europe; and everywhere they give the same unbounded satisfaction.
Below we give a few (greatly condensed) extracts from unsolicited testimonials from buyers of Beale High-Grade Pianos.'

We have literally thousands more
such testimonials, from almost every
part of the world. The immense suc-
cess attained by the Beale Pianos is
due solely to their high-grade quality.
From the very first we have given our
utmost endeavor to turn out only thor-
oughly reliable, soundly constructed
pianos, embodying the very best musi-
cal qualities. We have created a won-
derful factory organisation, and we
pay the best brains and the best skill,
so as to ensure a perfect result. We
realise that an Australian-made piano
is subjected to much closer scrutiny
in Australia than any imported piano,
and therefore we make —we are
compelled to make —our pianos much
better than those of our competitors.
At the same time, by making pianos
in Australia, we are in the position to
make them much better suited to Aus-
tralian conditions. Any competent
cabinetmaker will tell you that timber
seasoned in the cold, damp climate of
IQurope and America cannot be ex-
pected to withstand Australian ex-
tremes of dry heat as well as timber
which is seasoned in this climate. This
is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why
so many foreign pianos develop cracked
sounding-boards, split tuning-systems
and faulty casework soon after arrival
in this country. But no man can say
he ever saw a Beale piano with a
cracked sounding-board. We abso-
lutely guarantee that the sounding-
boards of Beale Pianos will never
crack in any climate, because our spe-
cial system of construction renders
cracking an impossibility, a* the same
time that it greatly enhar<- i the tonal
qualities of the instruir >■. Beale
Pianos contain the only p iv ucal steel
tuning-system in the world —-so there
can be no question of splitting of the
wrest-plank. And then our patented
system of cross-plied woodwork en-
sures permanence for the outside case
of the pianos.
Be sure of this: the better musician you are, the more you will delight in a Beale High-Grade Piano.
We have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of Beale Pianos.
Call and inspect the Beale Pianos now. Inspection does not put you under any obligation whatever. You suit yourself entirely as to
whether you purchase.
If you cannot call, write for Catalogue, which we will be pleased to send post free.

The Message of Sanatogen
No preparation of its kit d has ever achieved such
a reputation as Sanatogen.
Many causes have contributed to this unpre-
cedented result. r
Pre-eminent among them is the constant prescrip-
tion of Sanatogen bv the medical profession which
as a body, has awarded it the highest praise. More
than fifteen thousand doctors have written in the
most enthusiastic terms to describe the benefits they
have obt lined from the use of the preparation
which The Medical Press >uid Circular states "has
strengthened the physician’s hand a hundred-fold.”
Everyone knows now that it gives
To the Sick —Health
It does this by its power of revitalising the
nervous, vascular, muscular, and digestive systems,
which it rapidly restores from a condition of depression
to one of normal action. By virtue of its composition
—nure milk casein in chemical combination with
organic phosphorus, an essential constituent of the
brain and nervous system—it feeds the brain and
ne ves, it improves the condi ion of the blood, it helps
the stomach todi jest other food, and it therebv develops
and strengthens the muscles. In this way it gives
To the Weak —Strength
and that dePghtful feeling of returning vitality which
tells the sufferer that he has conquered th disease
whic > had conquered him, and is once more on the
road to health, and the possibility of taking up his
life again where he had laid it down.
Sanatogen, however, does even more than this.
It has its message for those whom disease has not
touched. Everyone is capable of increased vitality and
vigour. By feeding the vital centres Sanatogen gives
To the Healthy —Vigour
so that work becomes a pleasure, toil becomes enjoy-
ment, and the whole system takes on a feeling of
buoyancy, alertness and energy which is as delightful
as it is unusual.
Sanatogen’s merits have not only been eulogised
by the medical press, and by the medical profession,
but have also been endorsed by hundreds of the bes «■
known men and women who have testified to the
cures it has wrought in their cases.
Sanatogen may be obtained from all chemists.

Andrew Wilson. Please mention this paper, and
address:

The all-day-long' snaring of the oof-bird is to be
attempted by the frenziedly-financed Greater Won-
dergraph Co. in Holy Adelaide, where hitherto the
continuous picture-show wicket has been batted upon
only by West.
Michael Ford, one of the original “Two Mikes,”
the leading knockabout comedians of the London
halls of two or three decades ago, who got £5O a
week, plus travelling and hotel expenses, for a pro-
longed tour through America and Australia, has been
re-discovered. Ford retired from the business and
sank his money in a Covent Garden hotel. Now he
is shining boots for coppers in a London street.
When the “Ireland To-Day” picture show was at
Colac (Vic.) an aged Hibernian lady enlivened the
interval by dancing a jig in the gangway of the
hall. As long as the lights were down her friends
had held the excitedi old girl in her seat, but when
the interval came she had to dance or burst. The
Vic. country tour of the Shamrock show is drawing
ancients who haven’t been to an entertainment of
any sort for years and years. They come 20 miles
in buggies, some of them, and stump to their seats
on crutches after paying their respects to the ship-
ment of Ould Sod in the entrance way.
Sarah Bernhardt gets £6O for her performance in
a cinematographic drama, which lasts, perhaps, 40
minutes in action. The fee for preliminary rehear-
sals is not more than £6 or £B, but then the stage
directions are very explicit, and can be learned with-
out any study to speak-of. A cinedrama theatre has
salaried advisers, artistic and literary; and stage
managers (from other theatres) who drop in when
they are at liberty and earn a fixed salary of £l5 a
week each for hanging around. And who wouldn’t
hang around at the figure?
“J.F.H.”: Mrs. Adelaide Billineton, the grand old
woman of the English stage, entered her 90th year
in the second week of 1913. She was continuously
on the stage for <57 years, and is still astonishingly
vivacious and able to read and do fancy work —her
favorite occupation now —without the aid of glasses.
As a girl of 14 she saw her first play, “The Green
Bushes,” was stage-struck on the spot, and resolved
to become an actress. Years later she was the
original Mrs. Gregan in “The Colleen Bawn,” the
long-time popular Irish drama with the cave and
the diving scene that Boucicault, sen., constructed
out of Gerald Griffith’s story of “The Collegians.”
Among those she was associated with were Macready,
Joseph Jefferson, J. L. Toole, Madame Celeste and
Mary Anderson.
“0.K.”: Asche’s venture with “Midsummer Night’s
Dream,” at Melbourne Royal, has everything in its
favor, for there is 'no counter attraction to speak of
except the panto., and the panto, is wearing out its
Christmas welcome. The beautiful production of the
“Dream” that Musgrove imported 10 years ago had
cruel luck. After running for two or three weeks
to great business, gathering reputation all the time, it
got a terrible blow in the stomach of the pay box. The
enginedrivers struck. Train service was practically sus-
pended for a week or so. Then it was only partly
resumed. All the theatres suffered, but the “Dream”
(at the Princess’s) was the principal and most ex-
pensive attraction, and had most to lose from the
strike. People talked of nothing but the strike.
The “Dream” boomed no more whilst lingering on
for .some weeks. The next production, “As You
Like* It,” was a mild frost from the first, although
(he ! scenery was beautiful and the youthful charms
of Nora Kerin and Dora Rignold might have made
the fortune of a cheap and frivolous show. The
luck was against Musgrove’s opening season of his
first and only Shakspearian co. in Melbourne.
In “The Taming of the Shrew,” which appears to
be partly Shakspeare and largely otherwise, Petru-
chio gives two conflicting accounts of his roaring
self. Firstly, he introduces himself to notice as a
greten Johnnie who has just come into his father’s
money, and is anxious to see the world outside of
his native town. Soon afterwards, when his friends
warn him against the danger of catching a tartar
in Katherine, he speaks of the perils he has braved
in his previous career —the battles he has fought,
the lions’ tails he has twisted, and so forth. He
professes to have rivalled Othello in his moving
accidents by flood and field. He poses as the very
devil of an adventurer. And nobody knows —not
even the learned Shakspearian commentators —-
whether Petruehio is supposed to be a swaggering
liar, or whether the inconsistencies in the text are
the result of mosaic composition and careless editing.
“0.K.”: Of all the arts of entertainment that are
practised on the vaudeville stage the art of “bluff-
ing” the audience in an unfamiliar manner has my
deepest amazement. The wonderful juggler, acrobat
or gymnast commands respect for the infinite pains
and sturdy self-denying determination expressed in
the achievement of extraordinary feats. Many of
the “mixed turns”—comedy, dance, song and eccen-
tric fooling—show great ingenuity in their arrange-
ment. Also, some of the music-cnm-comedy turns
are very cleverly constructed. But the bluff solo
artist, whose work is commonplace in detail, yet
curiously effective in the complete turn, is as in-
teresting as any of them to a tired critic. What is
the secret of, say, Nella Webb’s popularity? She
calls herself a diseuse, which means that she talks
little rhymed pieces—of cheap sentiment and crude
human interest—giving musical endings to some of
the lines. Many a serio-chronic performer patters
as intelligently, sings as acceptably (or more so),
dresses as showily, and is quite as attractive in ap-
pearance as Nella Webb, whose occasional attempts
at dancing are far below the highest serio-chronic
standard. An analysis of Nella Webb’s turn reveals
nothing that couldn’t be as well done by a hundred
other fully-equipped descriptive songstresses. Pro-
bably a Fanny Powers imitation of Nella would be
a more finished performance. All the same, the
diseuse aforesaid “gets them” with exceptional suc-
cess. She is curiously popular. The strength of
her personality, which does the trick, is not strik-
ingly apparent, but it is generally felt, and it
bluffs 90 per cent, of the audience into the belief
that the artist is a remarkably gifted diseuse, where-
as she is merely doing nothing in particular in an
unremarkable way.
THE LAST STRAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A. E. Martin, the cheerful “Petramos” of many
Australian press contributions, has developed into
the most notable animal-hunter in Europe.... He
has got together a show that contains nearly every
beast on four legs. . . .There is a donkey with a col-
lege _ education, and a troupe of melodrama dogs
playing “The Bloodstained Hound.” They are to
open in Adelaide in February.— “Rosna,” B. 30/l/’l3.
Farewell, a long farewell, to my profession,
For mumming I shall practise never more!
To the dogs the Drama’s making swift progression.
It’s a wheeze I know that’s oft been cracked
before;
But it’s gospel truth by latest information.
The time is ripe to pawn my wigs and togs,
When a donkey with a college education
Gives a show with mumming melodrama dogs?
I’ve appeared in plays where corpses took the centre,
And ev’ry bit of limelight (sinful fact!) ;
I’ve “supported” in a six-months’ old tormenter,
Aye, a babe-in-arms who starred in ev’ry act.
But, beshrew me! are my name and reputation
To be ruined quite—are actors lifeless logs?
No, sir! A moke with college education,
I will not stand, or melodrama dogs!
I’m used to playing seconds to the scenery,
Which gets more space than I do on the bill;
But though my optic may be touched with green’ry,
Too much to swallow is this latest pill.
So to the whole d —d animal creation
I leave the stage! Let loose your frogs or hogs,
Your donkey with a college education,
And, last not least, your melodrama dogs!
N.S.W.
EARDLEY TURNER.

“J.R.”: He J. F. Hogan’s par. (B.
23/l/’l3), anent Gordon’s lines —“Life is
mostly froth and bubble,” etc., Canada may
have “been the first to engrave them on a
public memorial; but Papua wasn’t far be-
hind. The lines are to be found on the monu-
ment erected in Samarai Park, to keep in
mind that good but unlucky man, Judge
Robinson, the Commonwealth’s first Admin-
istrator of its nigger Possession.
THE WEAK SPOT.
THE WIFE: “And you wouldn't advise an
operation? ”
THE S PECIALIST: “My dear madam, in the
present weak state of his hank balance it is
quite out of the question."
Archbishop Donaldson, of Brisbane, has
just celebrated his 50th birthday. He is a
son of Stuart Donaldson, first Premier of
N. S. Wales. Donaldson, sen., carqe to Syd-
ney in 1840, made money, married (in 1854)
“Amelia, seventh daughter of Frederick
Cowper, of Carlton Hall and Unthank, in
Cumberland,” and spon afterwards went
back to England to live —and presently die.
The family never heard the wolf howling on
the doorstep.
A Political Nightmare.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Night and a mortgaged moon!
The borndrunks go in the back;
The note of the importoon
Is shrill on the trailing track.
Woe to the manufack
Who seeks for her vanished eggs!
The importoon has a silver spoon
And shell on his trousered legs!
The moan of the freetripe owl
Floats down from a blasted limb ;
He’s an old and sickly fowl
And we know what’s wrong with him !
Through glasses large and dim,
He peers at the corse of Time.
Yes, yes, he saw! But his whiskered jaw
Is locked on the needless crime.
Hark to the importoon !
The manufack sees him now !
She proves him by silver s'mon,
By invoice and forthright vow!
Now for a futile row!. .. .
The borndrunks gurgle and roar;
The forest wakes and the reed bed shakes
At the old, time-honored war.
A poltish whisked through the grass !
Whisked backward! An omen dread !
And a fiscal-sinking ass
Stood slowly upon its head ;
A tariff died in bed ;
A wage fowl put up its crest,
Then it said “Here’s luck!” and swiftly
struck!
{The nest egg hurst in the nest!)
Xiedit on the tradling track !
The sandshifts dig with their wings;
'The hands of the clock whirl back
And the borndrunk wakes and sings.
’“Warrer!” it pipes, and clings.
While the wowsit flops for rain.
Hut there comes no spate to its thirsty plate,
For the Dry Ghost walks again!
'QUEENSLAND.
COFFEE & CRAYFISH, LTD.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 15, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Governor Macgregor, who has been in the
country doing a few vice-regal jobs, is due
back at the city to-night to put in the
week-end. On Monday he will move on to
Allora, where he will be called upon to fix
his jaded eye on the prize pumpkin and the
creamy fruit of the cow what time he whis-
pers official nothings in the back ear of the
local mayor, or whoever is told off to lead
him round the paddock.
Death called round for Felix Clewett,
M.L.C., the other day. He was a Sydney
boy, born in 1836, and, by the death of his
parents, was forced into life’s battle at a
very early age. After a strenuous time, he
came to Queensland, where he engaged in
mining and sheep-and-cow pursuits. In the
Barcoo and Springsure districts he made a
good name and many friends for himself.
About 22 years ago he was persuaded that
he might help to bring the Leg. Council into
a state of enlightenment, but the people who
did the persuading evidently didn’t know the
Council. Three sons and two daughters sur-
vive their father. r
Popular “Billy” Norton also passed hence
on Tuesday night at the Gympie Hospital.
Pneumonia followed blood poisoning, the
latter being a memento of the late Bunda-
berg floods, where he worked hard, with
other rescuers, dragging population and
beasts out of the bosom of the deluge. Old
members of the Yacht Club acted as his pall
bearers when the body was brought to Bris-
bane, and the burial was at Toowong on
Thursday. In the 80’s of last century
Billy played football for N.S.W., and, later,
captained the Queensland team against his
old comrades. The saddest feature of his
departure is a widow and three children.
v Pretty little Hilma Ohman is ill in a pri-
vate hospital at Southport, suffering from
typhoid fever. Another invalid is Mrs. For-
rest, whose short stories and poems are well
known in Australia. She is in the Victoria
Hospital, where a surgeon had to take a
hand.
Parnell, M.L.C., with his wife and son
will depart from Rockhampton next week,
and will thoroughly inspect England, Scot-
land and Ireland before returning to Aus-
tralia.
Solicitor Tom Green and his well-liked
wife are likewise contemplating a trip to
England. They will sail next month, and
will go the long way round (via the Cape),
since the he partner’s health requires a sea-
voyage tonic.
The pretty Sydney Jones sisters, who are
at present in the Cold Country with their
mother, Mrs. J. T. Bell, will leave for Singa-
pore at the end of this month, on their kan-
garoo-ward way.
Mr. and Mrs. G. F. Scott and their daugh-
ter Mrs. Elmslie expect to leave on Monday
for Sydney, where they will join the Scharn-
horst for London. They were farewelled by
the Wharf-street Congregationalists (a sect
made up of 60 p.c. Christians, 20 p.c. Wes-
leyans, and 20 p.c. Sal’army) on Wednesday
evening, and on Thursday they in their turn
farewelled numerous friends at Mrs. Kerr
Scott’s pretty home in Brunswick-street.
Clergyman Mursell and his wife intend to
leave for England on a six-months’ holiday
early in March.
Schoolmaster Greer, of Southport, and his
wife have already left for Europe. Their
doctor son and his good-looking English
wife are to join them in London, but not
until Japan and America has been visited.
“The Monk and the Woman” began their
chequered career here to-night. The house
fairly bulged with an audience that, ex-
pecting to be shocked, was swindled. Seven
Wowsers were raving at the box-office for
their money to be returned when I left, but
I haven’t heard that they got it. In vain
shall the heathen rage, Thargomindah. The
cleric and his tart will sojourn here but one
week.
During the week Mrs. Swinburne-Taylor,
of North Toolburra station, crossed the great
divide. She was a daughter of the late John
Davies, one-time Postmaster-General in N.
S. Wales, whose name goes down to poster-
ity as “Jannery.” The deceased married,
first, Charles Swinburne, a wealthy young
Englishman, who was killed on the station.
The widow consoled herself with Dudley
Taylor, a popular Warwick dentist,._ who
forthwith retired from the tooth business,
and devoted himself to jumbuck pursuits.
Yours in the neap tide of things,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Condamine”: Denham’s assurance that
his Government is not in need of money is
quaint reading to those who know that the
building of the Miles-Taroon and other lines
is practically at a standstill. Earthworks
are lying unfinished and unfenced, and are
being steadily cut away by stock and rains;
salaries of an almost-idle staff keep on; and
the interest on the unfruitful money already
spent is growing day and night. Denham’s
one reasonable excuse for all this would be
want of money; and as he says he isn’t short,
he can take his choice —he is either a mud-
dler or the other thing.
“Northern Democrat” :
Re “Boswell’s” (B. 6/2/T3) assertion that the
“Denham Ministry isn’t half as strong as it looks on
paper.” It may be true, but this scribe regrets to
have to say that Liberalism in the Assembly is so
strong (owing to Labor foolishness last election) that
it can put out the Denham crowd to-morrow, re-
instate Philp or Macartney as head of a new Glovern -
(merit, and still present an invincible front to the
enemy. As to “Boswell’s” other assertion that
“Bananaland Labor is Big Australian to the core, he
should glance at the State Labor platform and note
that State-aided immigration is to be abolished, and
then ask himself what kind of a Big Australia we
are going to get under this absurd prescription. If
he likes to read up the Worker and other Labor
organs, he can find immigration cursed, stock, lock
and barrel. And no immigration means no efficient
Defence, and no efficient Defence is hardly a Big Aus-
tralian policy—at least, not of the white kind.
“Don Q.’s” case for the prosecution: —
Permit me to draw attention to the expensive
folly to Bananaland of Railway Commissioner
Evans’s importing craze. He has just landed from
the Land of the Wooden Ham five McKeen petrol
motor railway carriages at a cost (according to
Brisbane Telegraph) of £4600 per car, exclusive
of duty. Putting the duty at a modest 25 p.c.,
brings the cost of each car to £5750, making a
total of £28,750 for five carriages, each seating 69
passengers. This struck me as being a bit too steep,
so I made some inquiries. A man in the railway
workshops figured it thus: The ordinary carriage
turned out in the Government shops cost between
£l5OO and £2OOO. One only went over the £2OOO
mark. That was the Governor’s conveyance (burnt
about three weeks ago in the Wallangarra Are), and
known as “the £3OOO car.” In fairness to Evans and
the McKeen co., it is, admitted that the new petrol
cars are all steel. But allow a handsome margin of
£5OO as the difference between steel and wooden
bodies, and the difference between the State-built car
(at, say, £2OOO net) and the imported one is
£3250. Next, I got hold of a motor man who
knows his job, and he made no bones about saying
that if Evans knew how easy it Was to construct
petrol engines he would, in the circumstances, pro-
ceed to lay Violent feet upon himself. With the
plant and "staff in the Ipswich workshops, petrol
motors could be turned out by the dozen, and the
cost of the vehicles would be nearer £lOOO than
£3OOO. A 90-ton locomotive is made in the shops
for £3500. If you hear no more about this busi-
ness (or whatever it is) in the meantime, there will
be some anguished remarks when Parliament meets.
JUDGE LUKIN, Rockhampton (Queensland).
Died at the early age of 48, at Longreach,
Charles Barroineo Fitzgerald, who was At-
torney-General in Anderson Dawson’s short-
lived Labor Ministry—the first straight-out
Labor Government in Australia, in which
Andrew Fisher was Minister for Works, and
Turley, President of the Senate, was Home
Secretary. Fitzgerald was Rockhampton-
born, but was educated in France, and prac-
tised as a solicitor in several towns of
Bananaland. His connection with the
Labor movement ran back to ’92, when he
was defeated by James Dickson at Bulimba.
During the ’9l strike Fitzgerald was ordered
West, and on his refusal to obey, was com-
pelled to resign his commission in the De-
fence Force. Subsequently he represented
the Mitchell in the State Parliament, until
knocked out by grazier Cooper. Fitz. was
a brilliant scholar, a sound lawyer—and a
sadly imprudent fellow.
AN EPILEPTIC FIT.
A famous London physician, who for years
made a study of this disease, has, after a number of
experiments, found a prescription which affords re-
lief in almost a marvellous manner. This has been
put on the market by Mr. I. W. Nieholl, Pharmaceu-
tical Chemist, of Belfast, under the name of
“Ozerine,” and the number of unsolicited testimonials
received speak well for its efficiency as a remedy
against this appalling disease. Your chemist can
easily get it for you.*
Shooting recently at the Northcote Miniature
Range, Mr. A. J. Dalton, of the Collingwood Rifle
Club, put up the extraordinary record of 67 con-
secutive bull’s-eyes, using a B.S.A. Martini .22
calibre rifle, fitted wtih Aperture Sights. This con-
stitutes an Australian, and possibly a world’s, record,
and was done in actual competition under miniature
rifle club rules. The bull’s-eye was the regulation
one of half-inch diameter, which will give some idea
of the accuracy of the modern small-bore rifle. It
is noteworthy that the individual miniature rifle
championships of Victoria and New South Wales
were recently won by Mr. J. Mues and Mr. H. Mot-
ton respectively, each of them also using a B.S.A.
Martini rifle and Aperture Sights.*
Any Cycle cover which seeks to be popular must be
a combination of many virtues, and all appreciable
defects eliminated. It should be as resilient as pos-
sible, so that the rider may derive comfort from his
cycle; but, at the same time, resilience should not be
secured at the expense of durability. It must also be
reliable and able to withstand all the adverse condi-
tions under which it may be used. The cycle cover
which appears to attain this end closely is the
“Ordinary Roadster,” marketed by the Michelin Tyre
Co., Ltd. Its foundation is constructed of a material
which ensures the maximum amount of strength with-
out loss of resilience. It combines, as fully as pre-
sent-day skill in tyre manufacture will admit, all the
above-mentioned qualities, and, for every-day use it
is confidently JoVgivp the maximum
amount of satisfaction. Miew&WlMotor-Cycle Tyres
are of the same high standa,r<i‘!»jfir (indeed, are all the
products of this famous firm, ah'd are eminently suit-
able to withstand the strenuous conditions which
motor-cycling’ in this country necessarily imposes

Australia produces a lot of things of value
and delight, of which her average citizen
knows nothing. The other day some enter-
prising Bananaland growers sent to Mel-
bourne a consignment of that delectable
fruit, Monstera deliciosa , which had to be
sold for less than would pay the freight
simply because few knew what it was, and
others didn’t care to risk buying. It is the
same with many other tasty northern fruits.
There are perhaps three city fruit shops
where you can get the luscious mango, and
one where custard apples and pawpaws are
on sale in their season, while you would seek
in vain for Jack fruit, mangosteens or gran-
adillas.
Further testimony regarding the fact that
the North is no place for the white man who
is tired of life. The medical officer of the
Proserpine district hospital is explaining, in
his annual report, the dull time he has had:
This circumstance affords the strongest evidence of
the rarity of ordinary disease and the excellence of
the climatic and hygienic conditions of the district.
.... This is a matter of prime importance in regard
to future close settlement in the canefields of Queens-
land.
Later on, he remarks that he has had only
three funerals during the year, and these
were due to troubles “not curable by any
known method of treatment.”
How widespread are the evil effects oi
Republicanism! Hitherto, when there have
been floods in the banana-lands of the North,
dozens of Chows have been rescued from the
raging waters through the, agency of then
pigtails. Now the Manclm has gone, and
with the Republic has come the close-cropped
head; and simply because they have lost
their old life-line, many a Pong was swept
to glory during Queensland’s recent severe
wetting.

WATER TANK WAGGONS.
TENDERS will be received, until 11 a.m.
on WEDNESDAY, 26th FEBRUARY,
1913* for the Manufacture and Supply of 20
Water Tank Waggons of 2400 Gallons
capacity.
Forms of Tender, Snecification, Conditions
of Contract, and Drawings can be seen after
3rd February at the Commonwealth Railway
Offices, 84 William Street, Melbourne; and
also at the offices of The Works Director,
Customs House, Sydney; the Commonwealth
Public Service Inspectors at Brisbane, Ade-
laide, Perth and Ilobart.
The charge for each copy of specification
will we fl Is., and for each copy of plan 55.,
which must be paid before the Documents
can be handed over.
. Tenders, accompanied by deposit men-
tioned in form of tender, should be endorsed
“Tender for 2400-Gallon Water Tank Wag-
gons for Kalgoorlie to Port Augusta Rail-
way,” and are to be addressed to: The
Secretary, Department of Home Affairs, Mel-
bourne.

Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 12th February, 1913.
Appointment of a General Manager for
Cockatoo Island Dockyard.
PPLICATIONS are invited from persons with
Shipbuilding and Marine Engineering experience,
qualified for the position of GENERAL MANAGER
of COCKATOO ISLAND DOCKYARD, Sydney, New
South Wales.
Appointment to be for five years, terminable at
three months’ notice on either side. Extension of
appointment at the conclusion of the five years to
be optional.
If resident out of Australia, passage of General
Manager and family to and from Australia to be pro-
vided by the Commonwealth Government, unless
appointment is terminated at General Manager’s own
request before the expiration of the five years.
Applicants should apply by letter, stating date and
place of birth, give details and proof of their training
and experience, and should forward copies of testi-
monials; applicants should also state salary required;
salary to include all allowances except travelling
(no quarters will be provided).
Aoplications, addressed to the Naval Secretary,
Navy Office, Melbourne, should reach him not later
than 15th APRIL, 1913.
S. A. PETIIEBRIDGE,
Secretary, Department of Defence.

NOTICE.
Sale of Mineral Leases and Plant—
Northern Territory.
CT BALED Tenders will be received at the Office of
the Administrator of the Northern Territory of
Australia, at Darwin, up to the 31st day of MARCH,
1913, for the purchase of four (4) Mineral Leases,
each of an area of 80 acres, and comprising the
whole of that mining property known as the Daly
River Copper Mine, and for the purchase of a Re-
verberatory Furnace, with slag heap, at such Mine.
This property is situated about 60 miles west-
erly from the Brock’s Creek railway station, on the
Darwin-Pine creek Railway, and about four (4)
miles from the Daly River Landing; the river being
navigable to such landing to vessels drawing not
more than five and a half (sj) feet.
The present Mineral Lease of the property ex-
pires on the 31st day of March, 1913, when the
Leaseholders have to give up possession.
The smelter on the mine was built in 1908. Its
capacity is approximately 150 to 180 tons of ore
a month. It is pointed out that recently through
unauthorised tampering with the stays, the roof of
the furnace has fallen in, so that before the smelter
can be utilised, extensive repairs will be necessary.
The term of the Mineral Lease will be for 99
years from the 30th day of June, 1913, the rental
Is. per acre per annum, and 2i per centum of the
net profits; the conditions, reservations and stipula-
tions pertaining are, with two alterations, those
laid down in the Northern Territory Mining Act
and Regulations in respect to Mineral Leases.
Each tender must be accompanied by a cheque
for £2OO marked “Good” at the Bank.
Further particulars can be obtained at the Exter-
nal Affairs Department, Northern Territory Branch,
Melbourne, and the Government Secretary’s Office,
Darwin. ■ •
J. A. GILRUTH, Administrator.
Office of the Administrator, Darwin.
(This advertisement is in lieu of that which ap-
peared January 30.)

If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and ekptaiH
just how it acts. You use spectacles is sight magni-

able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust. Sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.

A curious sign of the times,is that Farthing,
MJJ.A., voluntarily abandons his license of the Duke
of Rdthsay Hdtel, Elizabeth-street, near the Mel-
bourne Railway Statiori, iri brder that it may become
part of the Craig-Williamsbfi 1 drSfpfefy shop. The
Licensing Commission would, never think of putting
this eminently respectable 61d putrip but bf action ;
nevertheless it goes, simply. because while Beer, pays
well, Bonnets pay better. Melbourne’s biggest change
is taking place in once-frowsy Flinders-street and
thereabouts. Pubs perhaps excepted, property values
keep on rapidly mounting, especially , near the two
entrances to the railway statioh. Prices in that
quarter already threaten almost the best in Collins-
street, and beat almost the best in Bourke-street.

'‘Pax”': ffl tepfy to C. J. Hutton” (B.
16/l/’l3) re the Dandfenhrig rib-operative bacon fac-
tory and the surprising gobdrifisfc Of Private Enter-
prise to the unsophisticated pig-breeder, let ffie say that
the Co-op. Cos. did not “spend the money it received'
from the trustful farmer in putting up a ,gi eat factory
capable of handling the bacon trade of Australia, and
forgot that some money would be required to run the
business.” The factory premises to date have cbht
under £25,000, and so far from being capable of
“handling the bacon trade of Australia,” they can at
utmost pressure deal with only 600 pigs per week,
about three-eighths of the output of Gippsland alone.
The real reason why the Co-operative factory ran
temporarily out of funds was that the farmers were
so satisfied with the private factories’ benevolent
treatment of them in the past, that the moment they
had a chance to sell their pigs to someone else they
rushed them into the Co-op. factory at the rate of
2000 a week. This is how the true position shows
out, taking the price of pigs in 1911 and 1912 :
i9ll (BtiVate Enterprise 1912 (The Co-op.
the Only Co. a Buyer).
July . . 425. to 475. 60s. to 07s.
August .. 445. to 48s. 755. id §4s.
September 455. to 50s. 90s to 105 s
October . . 40s. to 465. 675. to 775!
November 325. to 365. 665. to 755.
These figures are from Adamson and Strettle’s re-
ports, as published by that firm, and deal mainly with
Gippsland pigs in yardings of about 2000. Perhaps
Mr. Hutton can explain why it happened in 1912,
hen the Co-operative factory broke in on the “hon-
orable understanding” amongst buyers that the price
of pigs went up by 18s. to 50s. per carcase. He might
further explain why, when pigs were quoted at 365.
bacon was Is. per lb., the same as it was in Septem-
ber, 1912, when pigs were worth 105 s. Regarding his
contradiction of my statement that when the Co-
operative Cos. ceased to be a buyer the bottom dropped
out of the market, I again quote figures. In Novem-
ber the best price for bacon pigs was 755. ;in
December (with the Co-operative Cos. not buying) it
was 545. If a fall of 21s. per pig is not dropping
the bottom out of a market, I would like to know
what is. However, Mr. Hutton may rest assured that
the farmers intend to put up the money and see their
factory through. They have had such an enlightening
acquaintance with bacon firms that nothing will in-.
duce some of them to trust themselves to Private En-
terprise’s tender mercies again.

“Jag”: At the Castlemaine (Vic.) Police Court
the Other day, one Jones was charged before the
P.M. with “carrying away two bottles of beer from
licensed premises on a Sunday.” Jones, having no
defence, was declared guilty and fined 10s., and an
order was made that the beer be not returned to
Jones. In Jones I’m not interested; but lam con-
sumed with a feverish desire to know the fate of
Jones’s beer. What would you do with two bottles
of confiscated beer in midsummer ?
* * * *
A job in an English patent medicine factory has
its stern side. John Charles Umney, managing
director of a big drug co., in giving evidence before
a select committee of the House of Commons, ex-
plained that he tried his preparations first on about
20 or 30 of his employees. Dr. Chappie, of Maori-
land, expressed surprise; but Umney merely said
that he took some of the stuff himself and went
carefully. It seems an unhappy business; but in
the land of Cobden people have to do strange things
for a crust. Henceforth, the phrase “trying it on
the dog” is obsolete. Possibly through the pressing
claims of the meat market, dog is too valuable for
experiment. .
“H.”: Probably a proposal on the part of the
Commonwealth Government to run national liners
between Australia and Europe would move the Asso-
ciated Chambers of Commerce to indignant protest
that the time was not ripe, or insufficiently wet, or
something. But, all the same, the Associated Cham-
bers of Commerce realise that they are struggling
helplessly in the grip of the tremendous Shipping
Ring. Their experience over bills of lading has
taught them that. And they can get no redress. All
the lines issue practically the same bills; and not
one will amend the one-sided and objectionable
clauses. The Commonwealth, Canadian and U.S.
Governments do not allow the ship-owners to draft
their own B’s.L., but the British Government does;
and the attention of the Empire Trade Commission,
which will sit in Australia next April, is to be
drawn to the matter. Australian importers appar-
ently believe that the Commission may be able
to induce the British Government to compel the Ring
to give a fairer deal. But if the shipping cos. have
a horrible understanding among themselves regard-
ing this matter, they may have another with regard
to freights ; so the importers may merely pull them-
selves out of one pot of soup to fall into another.
“Frank S.”: Taken by and large, grocers, some-
how, are Tories of the third degree ; wherefore natur-
ally their trade journal prints things like this: —“An
American storekeeper writes: ‘lt takes something else
besides buying to make a success in any kind of
business. My first resolution was that the sun should
never shine on my front door. I would have it open
before sun-up, and not close it till sundown.’ ” The
journal’s precious comment on this is: “It is un-
necessary to say that this would be impossible under
local conditions, because, in Australia, though few
of us realise it, there is not much individual freedom
and the law decrees when we shall open and when
we shall close our shops.” So the grocer longs for
the good old times, when the shops were open all day
and most of the night, and the assistants had to work
like galley-slaves for next-to-nothing. In America,
where the ridiculous extract came from, shops are
open all hours. Yet, no matter when they open or
close, the sales in the aggregate are the same. No
one buys an extra bar of soap or a tin of kerosene
because the shop is open at sun-up. The Australian
system is really therefore a money saver, besides
giving the shop hands a fair deal. A fair deal—for
the other fellow —is, however, just what the average
Tory seems to hate most.

Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets.
A SAFE, SURE AND RELIALE REMEDY
FOR THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN
CHILDREN AND ADULTS.
Worms, by their movements, and the interrup-
tion they offer to the contents of the bowels,
generally occasion pain or uneasiness in the ab-
domen, sometimes spasmodic, sometimes gnaw-
ing or biting, and not infrequently of a vague,
indescribable character, and yet very distress-
ing. Sympathetic with this is a sensation of itch-
ing at the anus and at the nostrils, producing a
disposition to scratch the fundament and pick
the nose, which is characteristic of this affec-
tion. The bowels are often disordered, some-
times constipated or stopped, and sometimes
relaxed with occasional bloody discharges, as
in dysentery. The discharge is sometimes in
shreds or flakes, which are not infrequently
mistaken for fragments of partially-digested
food. It is not uncommon for portions of un-
digested food to pass with the discharges. The
appetite is exceedingly variable; in some cases
natural, in others deficient, craving or de-
praved; one condition not infrequently alter-
nating with another. The abdomen is often
hard, swollen and tympanetic, tile breath heavy
or foetid, the tongue furred, with disagreeable
taste and a copious flow of saliva; swelling of
the upper lip, bleeding of the nostrils, and dis-
position to grind the teeth during sleep are
other characteristic symptoms. The effects,
however, often extend beyond the alimentary
canal, and various derangements of health are
experienced in consequence either of the direct
irritation of the worms or of the disordered
digestion which they occasion. Among the
most common of these are flervous affections,
such as fretfulness, irritation of temper, wake-
fulness, or disposition to excessive sleep, dis-
turbed sleep, sudden starting out of sleep, as if
from fright, dizziness, spasmodic movements
of the eyelids, dilated pupils, perverted vision,
temporary blindness and deafness. General con-
vulsions are not infrequent in children, and
symptoms strongly resembling “Water on the
Brain” have been ascribed to worms and have
ceased on their expulsion.
Cases of St. Vitus’ dartre, melancholia and
nervous diseases, and even insanity, have been
attributed, with the most apparent reason, to
the same cause. In addition to these affections
may be mentioned obstinate cough, spasm of
the glottis, palpitation, languid circulation,
pale and yellowish skin, sunken eyes, a livid
circle about the eyelids, and a general emacia-
tion. This condition is often accompanied by
fever, and is then called worm fever. Persons
in apparently perfect health may be afflicted
with worms, and the only symptoms may be
periods of irritableness or a constant desire
for something to eat, which may last for
months until the system breaks down, and the
worms manifest their presence in some other
manner.
Worms have frequently been described by
medical men as ‘“the Children’s Enemy.” Un-
doubtedly there is rto greater enemy to health,
happiness and comfort in children than the
presence of worms in the stomach and intes-
tines; causing an indescribable irritation and
gnawing in the bowels, which upsets its vic-
tim, impairs the health, spoils the temper,
f^ d mother to distraction, and earns
for the little innocent the harsh and unde-
served reputation of being “a cross child.”
Unfortunately many mothers fail to recog-
nise the fact that their litle ones are suffering
intense pain and distress, and are practically
irresponsible for the outbursts of temper, rest-
lessness and peevishness, and until some more
serious symptoms appear nothing is done to
alleviate the distress. The symptoms of this
< lsease are somewhat variable, but generally
they are so characteristic that no mother need
have any doubts. The best proof of worms is
worms, and if the general conduct of the child
is such as to give the slightest suspicion, an
examination of the stools (evacuation from the
bowels) will afford ample proof.
A child who is affected with Thread Worms
will often appear to the eye to be in an indif-
ferent state of health. It becomes peevish and
irritable in its temper, and complains of itch-
ing of the nose, whilst the same symptom may
be very prominent indeed in the lower part of
the bowel. Another point to which the attention
of mothers should be specially directed is that
the child is apt to start in its sleep, its repose
being in this way of a disturbed nature. Note
also that many children who are in the habit of
wetting their beds do so by reason of the irri-
tation which these worms produce in the body,
the irritation extending to the bladder and
causing it involuntarily to part with its con-
tents.
In every heap of decaying matter there is
always to be found a nest of worms. They
breed and multiply in impure matter. And so
it is in the human system. The accumulation
of partially digested food in the intestines,
which becomes putrid when the bowels are con-
stipated, induces the presence of worms. A
few doses of Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets, followed by a dose or two of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills, will disturb them
in their nest and drive them out of the system.

THE USE OF PURGATIVES.
Although the use of medicines which are
simply purgatives may cause the discharge of
some small worms, such medicines are useless
to thoroughly eradicate the pest. Physics which
do not kill the worms and their eggs have no
permanent results, as the worms pass through
the matter in the intestines and reach the up-
per portion of the bowels or the stomach, or
fasten themselves to the membranes of the in-
testines until the effect of the purgative is
over. Worms require a specific medicine, and
there is no remedy so effective as Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets. They kill the
worms, destroy their eggs and dislodge their
nests.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets are a
scientific preparation, prepared in the form of
a sweet that children eagerly take. They are
packed in glass tubes with full directions in
each, and are for sale by chemists and stores
generally at 2s. 9d. per packet (or six packets
for 15s.); and if unable to obtain locally, will
be mailed post free on receipt of price. Pre-
pared only by the W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd.,
23 Lang Street, Sydney, N.S.W.; and Farish
Street, Wellington, N.Z.

The Banished Gods.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
NO more in groves of ancient Greece,
Nor in the Tuscan woods and hills,
The rapt soul trims his lamp in
peace;
Nor dwelling by Castilian rills
Beholds the dream-gods of old time,
And weaves them subtly in his rhyme.
The saga and the quaint romaunt,
The djinns of Asiatic cast,
No more take visual form and haunt
The shadowy pages of the past;
The Delphic and Egyptian glades
Have lost their tenants of the shades.
Lo, we have slain the fairy-folk,
And laid the gnome and centaur by !
No patron gods our odes invoke ;
They have abandoned earth and sky.
Only their ghosts dwell in the heart,
Lest we should wholly lose an art.
The nereid, satyr, nymph and faun,
Have vanished with the elder years;
Nor dances Pan across the lawn
With eerie notes for wondering ears.
The elfin and heroic themes
Are exiled to the land of dreams.
Unpeoples the enchanted ring
Where once the magic dance was trod,
And sealed the sweet Pierian spring
That ranged from goblm up to god.
Their place is not on sea or shore;
We have outgrown that antic lore.
But in this wild Australian brake,
Amid the casuaria trees.
And by the margin of the lake,
The banished gods from overseas—
The elves, the sprites, the fays, the gnomes—
Migrating, here have made their homes.
Their shadowy forms float down the aisles,
Dim-litten, of the forest maze,
Or hover in the deep defiles
And by the reedy waterways ;
Or in the mystic midnight moon
They haunt the still, remote lagoon.
Pan is a feathered wood-god now.
Who carols from some secret place;
Ilis dryads sins' from hough to hough
About his hidden dancing space;
Awhile the sweet HUglia dreams
Amidst her naiads in the streams.
Here Zephyr wanders, whispering low,
From sunlit banks to scented glooms ;
Furred goblins scurry to and fro.
And w'ingM elves assail the blooms ;
While massive boles, with heads sublime,
Stand like the titans of the prime !
Dim caverns house the darker gnomes—
Here’s sanctuary for all the crew;
The banished gods have made their homes
Amidst tecoma*; gemmed with dew,
And to these glades their souls impart
Lest we should wholly lose an art.
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.
The Mates of Torres.
THE WEALTH OF BRAMBLE CAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“'l l AYE a drink, mate?” asked Jack
|~| Wagstaff of the haggard man who
sat in the corner of O’Rourke’s
bar.
“No —thank you.”
“Don’t take a shingle off the roof.”
“Lave him alone, Jack,” said Dinny
O’Rourke quietly. “He never drinks wit’
anybody but the Oxford Don, which is him-
silf.”
The Oxford Don finished his drink and
walked uncertainly out of the bar to the
verandah, and there he sat, a haggard, wild-
eyed man, very pitifully alone, and staring
at the sea.
“He beachcombs around the reefs, but
always on his lonely own, an’ once a mont
he comes here whin the British India boat
is in from London.”
“That’s queer,” said Wagstaff. “Knocks
about the reefs alone?”
“A rotten life,” said Cravth drinking
alone, and without a mate. When did he
come?”
“On a British India steamer bound for
London, and lost his passage here, an’
stayed. A well-dressed man he was at the
beginning and then he neglected himself, an
drank alone, and at last he went to be a
hatter in the Straits; an’ away he’ll go
t’-morrer, an’ you won’t see him for another
mont’.”
“Why will he go away t’-morrer, Dinny?”
“Because the British India boat comes in
from London to-night, an’ goes out in the
morriin’.”
* * * *
The old bell of Thursday Island, used
most frequently in the Kanaka days, clanged
a fire-alarm in the early morn. Out ot
Dinny O’Rourke’s hotel came O Rourke,
Crayth and Wagstaff, half-dressed in haste,
naked feet thrust into sand-shoes -crying
with all other men, “Where’s the fire.
That red reflection on the _ water told
them. They scaled the stony rise, with its
infrequent stunted trees. On the sum-
mit burned a shack of wood and galvanised
iron, and around it were the shadows or
men. One of them suddenly darted through
a door from which the smoke was seen
wreathing only as yet, and in a moment he
was out again, dragging after him the limp
bodv of a man, so overcome as to seem dead.
The Mates of Torres ran to his aid ; they
lifted the body of the man, and as they did
so, a little ormolu cabinet —strange toy, or a
luxury foreign to Torres Strait fell to the
stones unnoticed. .
“Why, it’s the Oxford Don, said Dinny
O’Rourke. “I never knew where he slept
before. That shack belonged to nobody.
They bore the Oxford Don to the hospi-
tab and as the Mates of Torres left the
illuminated circle of the almost burned-out
shack, Crayth’s foot struck the little cabinet
of ormolu, and he stoooed and picked it up.
“What’s this, Dinny?”
i s at it.... Il’m. Some old junk I
lett be the Ohoinymin, I s’pose. ’Twas a
.loss house once. Bring it along an’ we’ll
Ink closer.
Crayth carried the little cabinet to the
hotel, and there, by the light of a candle,
the three half-dressed men turned it over
and over and found a tiny keyhole. Tint the
cabinet was locked, and the lock strong.
“Here’s a key that’ll tit,” said Jack
W agstaff, taking the poker from the fire-
place that was never used.
Crayth struck the top of the cabinet and
broke it in, and then he drew from the
opening a packet and two photographs. One
of the photographs was that of a man,
strong, very young, with a hopeful face —a
face of action, rather than of a thinker;
the other, that of a girl—a girl of the tro-
pics, though distinctively and softly Euro-
pean ; a girl lean, dark and deep-eyed, and
with the exotic charm and vitality of an
orchid.
“Who were the last people in the shack,
Dinny?”
“Choinase, an’ that was two years ago.”
“I suppose it’s all right, then, and we can
look. But if it’s letters we’ll burn ’em.”
So encouraged, Crayth opened the packet.
It was but one sheet of foolscap, enclosed
in several blank sheets, as if something very
precious was to be protected. Crayth
read a few lines, and stammered in his
surprise.
“Wh-wh-what’s this? Look here, Jack —
Dinny, look at this —what does it mean?”
Wagstaff took the paper, and slowly read
aloud this: —
My treasure—greater than all the riches of the
world—somewhere hidden in the world; but a treas-
ure almost as priceless in the coral, under the fig-
tree by the rock, on the most N.N.E. gut of Bramble
Cay.
“Why, what can it mean?” said Crayth.
“Well, if you ask me, it means another
galleon.”
“Or a clue to a bigger thing than that —-
‘somewhere hidden in the world.’... .H’m.
....‘But a treasure almost as priceless.’...
It’s good enough to try. What do you say,
Jack?”
“I’m with you,” said Jack Wagstaff,
giving his usual reason for the doing of
something unusual. “It will be a change.”
“I’m in, too,” said Dinny. “If you chaps
will get the lugger ready, I’ll be ready wit’
the feed an’ tools.”
And in the afternoon the lugger Margaret !
bore the Mates of Torres N.N.E., past Banks
Island, and then E.N.E. through Deep Pass,
and then almost due east to Bramble Cay, a
matter of 40 hours’ sailing in the light and
baffling winds.
They coasted its north aspect of high,
black ironised cliffs, and made south again
for a landing, and were there turned back by
a flat reef with trepang and brilliant fishes
and gayly-tinted corals in the rocky pools.
Then they took the small boat, and carried
blue water high to shore, and then on rapid
shoal, with the keel scraping through coral
branches.
Late in the afternoon they landed to the
accompaniment of the shrieks of thousands
of dispossessed birds, each with her allotted
nest noon the sand, each menacing the in-
truder open-mouthed.
A frigate bird, which is the pirate of the
reef, pursued a booby till she dropped a fish
from her claw to placate the thief, and the
frigate bird swooped and caught the fish be-
fore it reache'd the sea.
“Cruel as death,” said George Crayth, and
then his big heart began to pity all this
frightened motherhood, for another booby,
flittering before him, tried to save her blue
egg by vomiting a fish, which had been her
afternoon’s fishing and her supper. The
Mates retired before this fear and clamor,
and the shrieks died down to the mere bicker-
ing of nested mothers, whose places in the
sands overlapped, and the cries of hatred for
those thieves of eggs, the rats, which swarm
the island from the wrecked ships.
They went further into the island, and
found a park-like growth of trees, which was
almost dense enough to be called forest, over-
spread with blocks of coral reef —corroded,
bored, excavated, bitten and torn, just as if
it were but yesterday. Trees and ferns and
lycopods grew over it; a butterfly, with a
band of geranium dividing head and throat,
and whose wings were slashed with metallic
blue underneath, and above were blue-black
with brilliant bands of metallic emerald,
flitted seawards, but further south, and
they followed it.
They went on past the foetid beauty of a
stercuiia, on fire with crimson tresses of
flowers, and a fig-tree full of tailor birds, the
foliage almost obscured by hanging nests.
“S-s-sh,” said Dinny O’Rourke. “Whose
is the other boat?”
He pointed to where, in shallow water
nearby, two men rowed wearily in a dinghy,
and they left the trees and cautiously ap-
proached it.
And then Wagstaff laughed suddenly, and
said, “You’ve got rival men on the brain,
Dinny. That’s a nipa palm from Fly River
floating roots upward.”
“Well, it looked like men in a boat, any-
way,” said Dinnv ; “an’ you’ll have men yet.
You ain’t goin’ to have this all your own
way.”
The day was ending in slaughter—shoals
of young fish, driven to the surface by the
bonito, and there eaten by the tern; thou-
sands of young terns ran before the Mates,
and the old birds screamed overhead. Fri-
gate birds, boobies, gannets, noddies and
black and white terns—birds which could not
fly, looking fierce and snapping like cornered
rats ; eggs dropped anywhere without the pre-
paration of a nest, instinct guiding each bird
to its own place in an island covered with
eggs and nestlings. The conservative, love
for the patch of sand they were born on
made each unfledged tern stagger back to its
spot immediately it had been driven fiff.
Dinny O’Rourke swept a patch of sand
clean, saying, “There do be the poultry yard.
The newest laying of eggs is ours to-
morrow.” | i
And then, ere they reached the boat, came
the moonlight, with the homing birds still
flying overhead, their shadows on tljie sand
so dense that O’Rourke, hitting" p.t the
shadow for the substance, aarredThis hand,
and Paddv, O’Rourke’s dog, dasned. at the
bird shadows, filling his mouth with sand,
and slinking away as if ashamed of his error
—vet repeating it. <
In the moonlight they went aboard to wait
with little patience for the new day to tell
them the secret of this island of palms and

coral and birds and rats and coal; tho jetsam
of tin' scores of ships from all the world,
which had suddenly lifted this Cay out of the
coral sea and struck upon it.
At dawn next morning (hey were ashore
searching for a fig-1 ree at the N.N.E. end of
Bramble Cay, and for a treasure almost as
priceless as the trea lire which was greater
than all the riches of the world.
By noon they had decided on a space to
which the search for the present was to be
restricted, and then had to wait for a few
inches of water to ebb from a sandy pool that
filled the centre of the space.
Then they dug the sand away with diffi-
culty and at last came to the coral, and cut
it away with steel jumpers and found no-
thing, and sat down tired and disheartened.
“’Tis hard wurruk,” said Dinny O’Rourke,
taking off his hat and wiping the sweat from
his forehead. “’Tis corporation laborer’s
work wit’ the chance of gettin’ no wages.”
“Look at the paper again, George. What
does she say?”
Crayth studied the paper again. “Look
here—it says under the fig-tree, and the
branches cover a circumference of about
200 ft.”
“But,” said Crayth, “let’s think a bit. It
couldn’t be like that, because no man would
leave himself or any others he intended to
know such a vague direction. It’s closer
than that.”
He rose, and walked about the space cov-
ered by the umbrageous, glossy-leaved
branches, and muttered as the others did.
“ ‘ln the coral—under the fig-tree—by the
rock.’ I have it,” said Crayth, stopping
suddenly. _ “It doesn’t say, ‘under the coral’;
it says ‘in the coral.’ What’s the matter
with all this broken coral trash piled against
the tree?”
In a momeni ne was on his knees before
the great black trunk of the fig, and the coral
boulders piled against it, and tearing away
the upper layer he bared a chimney of coral
fragments, roughly squared, and shouted with
exultation as he put his hand into the cavity
and brought therefrom an oblong box of tin.
At the smallness of the find there was im-
mediate disappointment; but Crayth said,
feeling as greatly disappointed as any:—
“Never mind—good goods go in small par-
cels. Let’s look inside. It mightn’t be the
stuff, but it may tell us where the stuff is.”
There was no necessity to break open this
box; its loose lid yielded to the hand.
“More letters,” said Wagstaff. “Is this a
short story competition or a blessed literary
bureau?”
It was a large packet of letters, densely
written in a sprawling, feminine hand.
Crayth took it out and looked at it inde-
cisively.
“I don’t like this,” he said, “it’s getting a
little too private.”
As he spoke a photograph dropped from
the loosely-tied packet of letters, and Wag-
staff took it up with an exclamation of sur-
prise and handed it to Dinny O’Rourke.
“Why, ’tis the other photo, all over again.”
And so it was ; the same starry-eyed, dark,
exotic woman, whose picture they had found
in the ormolu cabinet by the burning shack.
“There’s too much sentiment about this
for me,” said Crayth. “I don’t like reading
a woman’s letters. She may be dead, and
that’s what makes it worse.”
“She’s sure to be dead. Code it may be, or
sentiment; but these letters are saved be-
cause she is dead.”
They rose and walked around the tree,
Crayth carrying the tin box in his hand, and
the two other mates followed him.
Behind the treasure a tangle persisted for
a hundred feet. Wagstaff stopped and
looked seaward, shading his eyes with his
hands to make sure. “Look out there,
Dinny,” he said. “There’s a boat.”
“Only some trader beating up among the
islands,” said Crayth. “But what’s this?”
He stepped seawards on the reef as he
spoke, and O’Rourke and Wagstaff followed,
and found him standing before a pattern
made in the rock pools, with great blocks of
dead coral, dirty-white upon the black and
green.
Crayth walked up one leg of the middle
character and down again obliquely.
“Why,” he said, “it’s some letter or other.
Let’s go on. There —A-M-Y —why, it’s the
signed to the letters.”
“We’ll never get to the bottom of it,” said
Wagstaff. “It’s a Chinese puzzle.”
“Chojnase puzzles have an answer to ’em,”
said Dinny O’Rourke. “Let us think now.
Would the sthuff be hid under them rock
letters?”
Crayth laughed openly at the possibility,
and showed him that the rocks below the
coral characters were flat and unbroken and
hard as adamant.
“Well, let’s go on.”
They came in shore again, following the
green woods that grew to high-water mark,
and then Wagstaff stopped and showed them,
deeply cut with a chisel on the rimose trunk
of a breadfruit tree, the letters “AMY,”
now half grown over by the bark. And then
the repetition of the name became so fre-
quent that they exclaimed dpon it no more.
“AMY” on the hull of an old ship, with the
coral growing up around it and the wind and
sea beating it down. On the black rocks,
“AMY” described with stones too heavy to
be disturbed by any tide, as if the name
should be changeless if the thing were not;
on a high patch of white sand. “AMY” in
letters of ironstone weathered black; on a
little bay of yellow sand. “AMY” in letters
of the red of oxidised iron, shining up
through the light green water. And every-
where along these quiet rocks and wooded
side of the island, where the screaming of the
seabird never came, and where the earth was
not dirtied by their clamorous rookeries, and
in the quiet green of palm and lvcopod and
fern, that word was repeated to infinity.
Brilliant pebbles had been gathered from
the reef pools to write the name upon a
strip of moss, under a tree and by a rivulet;
trunk of tree and shelf of rock bore that
word ; the purpose of a man giving even to
the earth the insistence of that fixed idea ;
stones and trees and sea made vocal with
love in grief.
“Lle’d got Amy on the brain,” said
Crayth.
“Poor wretch !” said Wagstaff. “I know.”
“You know, .Tack—how do you know?”
Wagstaff turned away, but did not reply.
“The question to me mind,” said Dinny
O’Rourke, “is not who Amy was—there do
be millions of Amys in the world, an’ ’twould
be better for a lot of min if some of thim
bad never lived. The question to me mind
is, how do Dinny O’Rourke an’ Jack Wag-
staff, an’ George Crayth turn a more or less
honest penny by findin’ where Amy keeps
the cash box?”
But Wagstaff, out of his experience,
guessed of a tragedy somewhere, and was
silent.
And as they came back to the place where
their boat w y as anchored, they found the be-
ginning of the solution. Around the most
north-easterly point of the island, a man
rowed a dinghy towards the great fig tree:
out at the edge of the fringing reef swung
the cutter that had brought him to the
island.
“Now, who’s this?” said Crayth. “Some-
body who’s after our game?”
Still in their sight, the man landed from
the dinghy, stepped into the foot or so of
water that covered the coral trash of the
tide-bared flats, and staggered through the
pools to the sand, careless of obstacles, and
making for the tree. And the dinghy
slipped away, and went out with the tides.
“He hasn’t moored his boat,” said Wag-
staff. “He’ll lose her. Hey ! Hallo ! Your
boat’s drifting.”
But the man seemed not to hear; he
splashed through the rock pools, and slipped
on the clammy cresses and the flat weeds,
and recovered himself, and went on, change-
less in intention, making for the fig tree.
He disappeared behind its trunk, and then
they heard his cry of agony and fear.
The Mates of Torres ran to the tree, and
saw behind the great trunk, and by the
rifled chimney of coral rock, wearing still
his half-burned clothes, now reeking with
sea water, gasping for the air that came to
him in rattling breaths, and his face trans-
figured with pain—the Oxford Don.
They lifted him, and he saw in Crayth’s
hand the tin box.
“Mine, mine ! Give it me !”
Crayth handed him the box, and said:
“The letters have not been touched.”
“Thank you, thank you. You found the
other box after the fire?”
“Yes.”
“It was —it was mine.”
“We didn’t know. We thought it was
treasure of some kind.”
“It is mine.”
“You’re sick, mate.”
“I am—yes—very sick. I’m glad—be-
cause all hope is gone—all hope is gone.”
“Let’s get him aboard the lugger, Dinny.”
“No, no, gentlemen, let me stay. I was
burned a little. It is pneumonia. I’m
finished. I had to follow. I knew I would
live just long enough.”
“Long enough. What for, mate?”
“To get back here. I used to be afraid
I would lose her letters among the reefs, and
I planted them here.”
“So you wrote all that about?”
“Amy, Amy; yes....we were married...
I thought of money—fame—l loved her
always, but I was abstracted. She wanted
me to see —to see and understand —she told
me —told me things. I was dreaming—l
never could hear —the world got between
u5....0n the British India boat bound for
London we were —she tried to tell me of the
hound who was pursuing her on the ship...
I trusted her so, I could not think any
man.... would dare to test her She
loved me, too, but her strength was broken
down by his persistence. ... I found it out
and went mad.... 1 was ashamed I could
not think nor act 1 stayed too long
ashore at Thursday 1 went back to for-
give her and kill him The ship was
gone. ...So I stayed on. .. .waiting for the
British India ship to bring her back....l
forgot to w0rk....1 spent all my money...
Then I took to the reefs.... All letters she
ever wrote to me, I kept 1 thought of
nothing but her name —waking—sleeping—
working....l wrote her name all over the
island.... here, and everywhere.”
“Come to the boat, we’ll get you back to
the hospital, and you’ll pull through, and
find her yet.”
“No Never now. Two days ago the
ship came in—the ninety-eighth British
India ship since I lost her....No it is
the end, and I am glad 1 haven’t eaten
anything I could —could taste for more than
eight years, and now sleep rest. I
remember... .a hymn we used to sing....
when I was a boy and used to go to
church....lt says: ‘When this poor, lisp-
ing, stammering—stammering tongue lies
silent in the grave.’ That is mine No,
no, if you are men, leave me here. . . .Break
me a grave in the coral. If you are men.”
. “ ’Tis all right, mate—’tis all right,” said
big Dinny O’Rourke chokingly. “We’re
men an’ you haven’t had a square deal.
We’ll do all you want.”
“Thank you. And if there is anything
you love, hold to it, and forget the world—
though the world won’t let you forget it, if
you—if you have to work and fill other
mouths than your own with bread.... But
don’t let the world get you. There isn’t
anything worth having in life but love —
nothing—nothing.”
“Don’t talk, mate. Rest.”
“I must talk, because I rest for ever,
soon very soon. When I had her love’
bread was sweet, and since then it has been
dust and ashes.”
“Quiet, mate. Quiet.”
“Lay me down —turn me to the reef. I
want to see her name.”
So they made his tortured body as easy
as they could, spreading the coarse beach-
grass and sea-weed on the sand, and pillow-
ing his head with their coats.
Butterflies of black and emerald flitted
through the copses—the many flakes of
gold that were yellow wanderer butterflies,
bravely ventured over reef and sea. The
tropic sun filtered through the leaves of the
great fig tree, and from its branches the
flute of the little blue dove fell softlv to the
broken man. And on a dead tree a white-
headed sea-eagle tore to morsels a green
snake.
He spoke again. “The best thing—in—
all the world is—clean love —and we think
the less of it —because we get it for
nothing.
* * * *
“ H ® was right, dead right, poor chap.”
said .Tack Wagstaff. as with the spade he
rounded the coral chips on the mound thev
raised above the Oxford Don. “Money can’t
buy the best of things—only the little ones.”

And then the Margaret bore them back
to Torres Strait, to struggle for the spoil
and *° f° r SC-t the lesson of the Ox-
ford Don.
